The Red Page
A World of Men
Weininger was but one of the many men
who have written about women—as females.
Here is the man —as male—put under the
microscope by a woman. And The Mari-
Made World, by Charlotte Perkins Gilman
(Unwin, per Lockley Library), is well worth
perusal by the insect under the microscope
as well as by the female of our species.
Charlotte puts on a scientific basis more than
Corelli has been stammering for years.
The author accepts, and has reason to
accept, the Gynsecocentric theory of the
development of the human race. This theory
may be defined thus: The female is the race
type, and the male, originally but a sex type,
reached, in the human family, a later equal-
ity with the female, and became her master
for a considerable historic period—and, it
may be added, still remains her master. The
human species has been, since historic times,
under androcentric rule—a domination by
the male. But biologists have tentatively
accepted the theory that at some remote
period the human species was a woman-ruled
world, the male being merely an accident,
till his sex attributes enabled him to snatch
the dominion from the female, engrossed in
her racial attributes, and to keep it ever since.
Thus the species has developed under a
masculine tyranny, and the masculine attri-
butes, lightly summed up by Charlotte as
“to butt—to strut —to make a noise—all for
love’s sake”I—have 1 —have become, in the view of
the ruling sex, human, arm not merely mas-
culine, attributes.
That stupendous and successful rebellion,
lost in the mists of the race’s remote past,
was the most pregnant event in the world.
The tremendous war waged by one sex, the
despised male, against the haughty female,
through millions and millions of years lias
unfortunately no chroniclers. Yet we may
picture it vaguety. Here was the human
race ruled by the female, with the man a
mere sex accessory, despised as are the
drones in the hive, yet handy to have about
the cave. The prospective husbands decorated
themselves, preened themselves and fought
each other in the meek hope that their
tyrant' queens would graciously deign to
select the victors for their consorts. Their
sole use was as fathers. They were set apart,
out of the human race, as a sex. And gradu-
ally they grew uneasy at their servitude, and
at length revolted. The long vicissitudes of
that tremendous warfare we know not; but
at the end the male had deposed the female,
flung her into a sex servitude, and has kept
her manacled ever since. The result is that:
We have been so largely taxen up with the
phenomena of masculinity and feminity that
our common humanity has largely escaped
notice. We know we are human but we do
not know in what our humanness consists,
nor how men and women may fall short of it,
or overstep its bounds... Since the range of
feeling and action proper to humanity, as
such, is far wider than that proper to' either
sex, it seems at first somewhat remarkable
that we have given it so little recognition...
We have taken it for granted, since the dawn
of civilisation, that mankind meant men-
kind, and that the world was theirs. Women
we have sharply delimited. Women were a
sex—“the sex” ; they were set apart for spe-
cial services peculiar to feminity. As one
English scientist put it, in 1888, “Women are
not only the race—they are not even half the
race, but a sub-species told off for reproduc-
tion only.”
This is a book about men —as such. It
differentiates between the human nature and
the sex nature. It will not go so far as to
allege man’s masculine traits to be all that
excuse or explain his existence; but it will
point out what are masculine traits as dis-
tinct from human ones, and what has been
the effect on our human life of the unbridled
dominance of one sex...lt seeks to show
that what we have all this time called
“human nature,” and deprecated, was in
great part only male nature, and good enough
in its place; that what we have called
“masculine,” and admired as such, was in
large part human, and should be applied to
both sexes; that what we have called “fem-
inine,” and condemned, was also largely
human and applicable to both. Our andro-
centric culture is shown to have been, and
still to be, a masculine culture in excess, and
therefore undesirable.
The mistake has been made by practically
all men —simply because they were men. To
the man, the whole world was his world, his
because he was male ; and the Whole world
of women was the home, because she was
female. He had everything else-, and. having
it, naturally he called it male. The author
insists on a “man’s sphere” as sharply de-
fined and even more limited than a “woman's
sphere.”
Woman’s natural work as a female is that
of the mother; man’s natural work as a male
is that of the father; but human work covers
all life outside these specialities. Every
handicraft, every profession, every science,
every art, all normal amusements and recrea-
tions, all government, education, religion, the
whole living world of human achievement—-
all this is human. /
So there!
In the animal kingdom—as it was in the
human race under gynsecocentric culture —
sex selection is always at work. The males
compete for the female, and she selects the
victor, thus securing to the race the best
father. In forming the proprietary family
as in the human race under androcracy, there
is no such competition, no such selection.
The man, by violence or by purchase, does
the choosing—he selects the kind of woman
that pleases him.
Nature did not intend him to select; he is
not good at it.- Neither was the female in-
tended to compete—she is not good at it. If
there is a race between males for a mate,
the swiftest gets her first; but if one male
is chasing a number of females he gets the
slowest first. The one method improves our
speed ; the other does not. . . When women
became the property of men, were sold or
"given away” by their paternal owner to
their marital owner, they lost this preroga-
tive of the female, this primal duty of selec-
tion.
Thus the race has progressed only through
the male: the female was kept in the posi-
tion of a servant, and usually shut up.
Woman steps from one home —her father’s
—to another —her husband's; and never en-
ters the world—man’s world—at all. And
the male has, too, been held back by this
reactionary force.
For each man to have one whole woman to
cook for and wait on him is a poor educa-
tion for democracy... The assault upon his
heart direct, or through that proverbial side-
avenue, the stomach, which the dependent
Woman needs must make when she wants
anything, is bad for the man as well as for
her.
It is not healthy to have a loving servant
always ministering to one’s desires. Less
devotion and more knowledge, less affection
and a higher grade of skill, are needed in this
vreat business of feeding the world. We
cater to the appetite continuously. We
know what John likes ; but we do not know
m the least what the various chemicals we
daily present to him do to his unhappy inside.
Neither do we realise that this constant min-
istering service in the personal desires of
men in the home is responsible, to a terrible
extent, for their helpless self-indulgence in the
world outside. The psychic effect of “mother's
cooking” is a thing we have not considered.
No art. no science, no business can grow
far when kept to. a domestic level, when the
product of labor is for one person only, and
is governed not by knowledge, but by desire.
But surely civilised man, as the selector,
has developed beauty in the female he
chooses? Charlotte’s answer is that much
of what man calls beauty in women is not
human beauty at all, but gross over-develop-
ment of certain points which appeal to him
as a male. E.g., fatness—really an element
of weakness, inefficiency and ill-health—and
the relatively small size of woman, “deliber-
ately preferred, steadfastly chosen, and so
built into the race. In his efforts to improve
the breed of other animals, man has never
deliberately tried to cross the large and the
small, expecting to keep up the standard of
size.” Also, her long restriction to house-
limits, and the heavy limitations of clothing
have made her disproportionately short-
legged - “ squatty ” Charlotte pleasantly
terms her sex.
So, also, the lordly male, largely occupied
with his eternal combat in this man-made
world with his fellow male, has deteriorated
in appearance. There is one feature which,
as can be seen from any illustration in a
novel, he roust have —a prognathous jaw.
The possession of this bulldog feature may
enable male to conquer male, but does not
make him of any more service to Society, of
any better health, or higher beauty.
Probably the most extraordinary result of
our androcentric culture is that the human
race is the only division of the animal king-
dom in which the female decorates herself.
Everywhere else, from the peacock to the
lion, it is the male who adorns himself and
leads the fashions. He has to, to attract
the notice of the female. But the human
male has evolved for himself the mainly use-
ful but ugly garments of to-day, while :
Women wear beautiful garments when
they happen to be the fashion, and ugly gar-
ments when they are the fashion, and show
no signs of knowing the difference... .Their
condition, physical and mental, is largely
abnormal; their whole passionate absorp-
tion m dress and decoration is abnormal. . . .
AY omen are not responsible for “society,”
anv more than they are for harems. In an
androcentric culture, “society,” like every
other social relation, is dominated by the
male and arranged for his convenience.
J*
Among the many things that the male has
seized upon as his own is art; and himself
going on to lonely heights of achievement,
he has found that the world is largely Indif-
ferent to his attainments.
As the world at large consists half of
women, and wholly of their children, it would
seem patent to the meanest understanding
that the women must, be allowed to rise in
order to lift the world.
Now for man-made literature. There the
most popular stories are the story of adven-
ture and the story of love. Conflict and love
the two essential features of masculinity.
If Charlotte had her way, in every paper
there would be a “man’s column” of news,
wherein would be put all the sporting items
not world interests, but exclusivelv male
interests. As for the love story :
It is the story of the pre-marital struggle.
It is the Adventures of Him in pursuit of
Her —and it stops when he gets her! (Well,
so does She.) Story after story, this cease-
less repetition of the Preliminaries.
And why? Because this brief period out
of his whole life is the male’s one period of
overwhelming interest and excitement. Char-
lotte forgets to add that it is also the
female’s. But she suggests that if “love”
is to be selected as the most important thing
in life to write about, then the mother’s love
should be the principal subject.
But, indeed, here the author —please note
how we refrain from dubbing her authoress
—cannot be speaking of modern fiction, whose
stories frequently begin after marriage, or
cover, as in Bennett’s novels, several gener-
ations.
So Charlotte Perkins Gilman surveys the
whole man-made world of law and govern-
ment, politics and warfare, industry and
economics, ethics and religion, education and
sports, and emphasises the maleness of this
world which the males have labelled human.
But she admits the present inferiority of the
female under male domination, and, solely
on the ground that the permanent inferi-
ority of one-half of the race is a drag on our
progress, makes her plea, good for greater
opportunities for her sex’s joint partner-
ship in this difficult business of life. Worth
thinking over is her contention that the
“squatty” sex is not by choice still a cave-
dweller, but has remained short-legged be-
cause a tyrannous male has kept her too long
in the cave.
REQUIESCAT.
Dropping petals fringe the urn
On the dais of the dead,
Yellow-haloed candles burn
At her feet and shining head:
Gently! Lest a footfall rob
This new stillness swathing her,
Lest a vibrant breathing stir,
While the faintest shivering sob
Of the slow bell sinks away
Into silence perfectest:
Veil her eyelids from the day,
Cross her hands upon her breast....
Looking at her, you would say
(Would you not?) that now,‘in jest,
She had hid her eyes away,
Crossed her hands upon her breast,
Still her mouth (that, like a bud
Over God’s high garden-wall
Dropped, and on her face let fall,
Seems yet ripe with running blood)
Lest it twitch, as though it were
Prisoning a laugh, and lest
That should break, betraying her —■
Lay her hands across her breast!
When the shutter-shaft of light
Vanished with the dropping sun,
Just a moment since, 1 might,
Dreaming souls, have said that one,
Slipping up the sun-line spent
Vanished with its vanishing—
Might have thought a brushing wing
Touched my temple as it went:
Turning swiftlier, had I
Glimpsed, perchance, some splendor-drest
Thing of fire flashing by ?—
Fold her hands upon her breast.
Flowers wilt and candles fail
As did she; the Mass is said !
What shall priest and prayer avail
Her whose rest is perfected?
How shall words her stillness move?
Close the door! Perchance she stands
There within, with glimmering hands
White against the dusk, above
That which once was she, that lies
At her feet, a thing apart—
Shining head and sightless eyes,
Fingers folded on her heart.
J. Alex. Allan, in “A Wine-Sliop Madonna.”
J. Alex. Allan, the author of A Wine-
Shop Madonna, and Other Verses (Argonaut
Press, Afel.h.), is to be commended for his
moderation. His daintily printed book con-
tains only IB poems, a frontispiece, an
author’s note and the printer’s colophon.
All the verses, except one, have appeared in
The Bulletin, and the one exception is not
worth inclusion. Allan has an ear for metre
and a sensuous delight in words ; his work is
above the average in feeling, but he writes
not because he must, but because he can.
He has nothing much that needs saying,
but he does not stammer in his utterance.
This is faint praise that does not mean to
damn; for a poet who can ask:—
Was there ever a kiss that I might have
missed,
Was there ever a mistress I might have
kissed —•
That I ever missed f —
That I never kissed?
has not published in vain.
Kipling is breaking out in a now place.
“A romance of the world as science and san-
itation will have developed it in another
century” is announced to appear in an Eng-
lish magazine. Trenching on H. G. Wells’
ground.
Australia by an Australian
The only objection the critic can find with
C. E. W. Bean’s Dreadnought of the Dar-
linn (Alston Rivers, per George Robertson,
Syd.) is that the title does not tell half the
contents of the book. Bean begins with an
account of a trip down the Darling on a
river boat; but even this is no ordinary
traveller’s description of a strangely interest-
ing journey. The author ranges wide over
the whole of outback N.S.W.; and succeeds
in picturing, ostensibly for the ignorant
Englishman, but also for the equally igno-
rant suburban Australian, the sparse life
that peoples these wide spaces of the map.
Journalism it is, but extraordinarily vivid
journalism, as an exposition of outback Aus-
tralia worth a thousand acre paddock full
of Foster Frasers. ' The Dreadnought of the
Darling is a book that if picked up by the
average Australian must be read right
through. But the author of On the Wool
Trade has already gathered his audience.
This, his latest work, should immensely ex-
tend that audience. For picturesque des-
cription, accurate and extended knowledge,
and an insight into character, Bean shows
himself an author who should, in his own
line, go far.
Some extracts, which hardly represent the
full charm of the book :
“The Murray is an old, old river. In his
upper reaches perhaps he is the oldest river
that still runs. As the lives of rivers go, he
must be getting near the end of his days. It
is quite possible that there are now only a
few hundred years left between him and the
day when the old sea-fioor across which he
runs'into the sea will have been shouldered
a few hundred feet higher, and he will cease
to reach the sea at all, but will peter out
miserably in a salt, shallow clay puddle of
his own making. Ilis only hope is that by
those days the earth will have heaved up
some new mountain ranges, so as to give
him a fresh start. And the earth is quite as
likely to do the other thing. ...”
“Then we first struck mulga country. We
were crossing some low, rocky ridges, when
all the color seemed to fade out of the pic-
ture. It was as though the earth had sud-
denly fainted, or as though you were looking
at it through a blue glass ; the whole place
had that curious anaemic pallor which it
puts on when you open your eyes after shut-
ing them for a long time in the bright sun-
shine. We had seen a few grey mulga trees
before among the green scrub, but now every
tree was grey....”
“In the country, in the pastoral industry
and at the diggings, the ideal of the Aus-
tralian is still made; or, rather, the stan-
dards of pluck, hardiness, unaffectedness,
'loyalty, truthfulness, hospitality on which
the rest of Australia consciously founds its
ideal. In other words, Australians invari-
ably reserve their greatest admiration for
the man from outback ; and perhaps that, as
far as one can tell to-day, is likely to be the
great value of this outside country to Aus-
tralia. It is true it produces wool, meat,
tallow, skins, perhaps ultimately a little soap
and some glue. But in the course of that
great pastoral . industry it produces some-
thing far more valuable to the country
than all the rest, and that is—men.
“In guessing at the future of Australia, it
seems essential to remember that the Aus-
tralian. 100 to 200 years hence, will still
live with the consciousness that, if he only
goes far enough back over the hills and
across the plains, lie comes in the end to the
mysterious lialf-desert country where men
have to live the lives of strong men. And
the life of that mysterious country will
affect Australian imagination much as the
life of the sea has affected that of the Eng-
lish. It will always be there to help the
Australian to form his ideals; and one
knows of no land where they have a more
definite ideal than in Australia, or where
the whole people, men, women and even
youngsters, are more consciously employed in
working it out. One cannot help thinking
it is really the chief amusement of the Aus-
tralian... .Australia is a big, blank map,
and the whole people is constantly sitting
over it like a committee, trying to work out
the best way to fill it.”

Poets and blokes are reminded that the
sonnet asked for in the Australian language,
a sonnet, not necessarily in Australian
slang, but one that to be understood outside
Australasia would have to be translated in-
to English, must reach this office by the end
of this month. A guinea for the best.
The latest items of the N.S.W. Bookstall’s
perennial flood of shilling Australian fiction
for Australian readers are The Outlaws of
Weddin Range, the life of Ben Hall, done
into fiction by the practised hand of Am-
brose Pratt —-with, of course, the moral tag
at the end, hinting at the annoyances and
inconveniences of the bushranger’s life Pratt
had so enthusiastically and sympathetically
told; and Stella Sothern, by Ivan Archer
Rosenblum, a capably written story that de-
pends chiefly on its exciting plot—the lost
explorer, the faithful Stella, the villainous
Dasro, the bush fire and the gold mine. Yes,
they’re all there.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Song of Ninety Drunks.
There were immense crowds in the city last night,
but everything passed off quietly, and the police had
no trouble. About 90 drunks were locked up at the
central station, Sydney. —News item, 2/l/’l2.
Oh, the brave New Year came rolling in
With a beautiful cellar breath;
Whisky and lager, brandy and gin—
The gay New Year was brimming with sin
When its father was still in death ;
The cab horse panted beside the pave,
While the constable groped about;
Unto each strong man an arm he gave
And each, still singing, was lifted out;
Old songs he sang of the rolling wave
As they bundled him gently out.
From ev’ry quarter the New Year rolled,
In a cab with due respect;
While sirens hooted and church bells tolled,
Backward and forward he gaily rolled,
Though often his hat was wrecked;
Not yet had Sorrow been born for him,
Nor had Poverty stood revealed;
No thoughts of morning his joy could dita
As out of his carriage he reeled;
Life was a joke, not a struggle grim,
So he sang as he sagged and reeled.
They shoved him into a plain, hard cell,
And they left him alone to brood;
He sank to slumber and snored right well —
Loud foghorn music no cop could quell
In his beautiful solitude;
But now and again he woke and sang
A ditty of one in a bunk,
Who thought and thought—so the ditty rang—
On a diet of old salt junk;
And they took it up, that merry gang,
When the year was young and drunk.
When January the First goes by
This world’s a peaceful place,
Where gay cabs follow the folk who die
(Let’s hope it is neither you nor I!)
At a mournfully sober pace.
The new bills come in the same old way,
And the very same bailiffs peer;
But while we’re asking for time to pay,
We look forward to next New Year
And dream of the christ’ning wild and gay,
With the coppers and cabs and beer.
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.
The Second Mate.
We’ve got a second mate, ’n’ ’e’s a tartar,
At times ’e doesn’t seem ter care a jot;
But I often see’d ’im thinkin’ (that wus w’en ’e
wusn’t drinkin’)—
Thinkin’ ’ard ’n’ growlin’ inward at ’is lot.
’E doesn’t seem ter know th’ name o’ civil;
’E curses ’n’ ’e scowls at us all day;
[ An’ this drinkin’, cursin’ dreamer onct wus skipper
of a steamer ;
But th’ Court suspended ’im—that’s wot they say.
For the second mate’s th’ navigatin’ orf’cer,
Th’ bloke wot makes th’ time ’n’ shoots th’ sun;
’E keeps th’ middle wotch ’n’ does ’is dooty
Ter find th’ ship’s position and th’ run.
Th’ second mate ’as ’ad ’is share o’ trouble!
’E uster be a clever bloke ’n’ smart;
But th’ flamin’ Court wot tried ’im common justice
■ it denied ’im,
And they say it nearly broke ’is blessed ’eart.
I’m sorry fer ’is Nibs w’en ’e’ gits broodin’;
I’d rather ’ear ’im rave ’n’ tear ’n’ rip ;
And I’d sorter understand it, for w’n ’e begins to
’and it —
Well, ’is language fairly shakes th’ blarsted ship !
And th’ second mate’s th’ navigatin’ orf’cer,
’E knows ’is bloomin’ work. ’E’s pretty fly !
“Keep closer to yer course, yeh blanky lubber!” —
That’s ’im. I growls “Orlright,” ’n’ winks me eye.
’E wishes now ’e’d never got a ticket;
I read that in ’is chivvy pretty slick;
For I’m good at readin’ faces, ’n’ w’en I spots ’is
grimaces,
Then I thanks me stars that I’m “afore th’ stick.”
Y’orter seen ’im one day as I see’d ’im,
Th’ skipper’d roused on ’im till all wus blue,
An’ ’e smiled at ’im so sickly that I tumbled pretty
quickly:
’E’d forgotten more’n th’ ole man ever knoo l
But th’ second mate’s th’ navigatin’ orf’cer ;
’E carries out deeiplin to a T;
’ls sextant, ’is chronometer ’n’ ftxin’s
Are ’is concern... . Wot’s that ter you ’n’ me?
But you should see th’ “second” w’en ’e’s drinkin’!
’E’s willin’ with th’ slowest bloke t’ chat!
And th’ way ’e ’as er chaffin’! —well, yeh jest carn’t
keep from larffin’
W’en ’e sez: “ ’Ere, Bill, ole boy, do this, or that!”
’E doesn’t know th’ way ter keep from smilin’;
Yeh’d think ’is life ’ad been a school-kid’s game;
But w’en ’e is orf th’ likker, we look out an’ skip
round quicker,
For ’is manner w’en ’e’s sober ain’t th’ same!
For th’ second mate’s th’ navigatin’ orf’cer;
’E’s gotter live, though owners keeps ’im down.
And all of us, like ’ens, must scratch fer tucker;
It’s starve ashore, or go to sea ’n’ drown!
CENTRAL PACIFIC.
ALBATROSS.
HOME.
Shut the world out,
Close the door,
This is our heaven when day is o’er.
Draw the curtains,
Pull your chair
Into the glow of the firelight there.
Turn your head, so
That I see
The love in your quiet eyes, for me.
Has the day been
Hard, my dear?
Well, day has gone, and the night is here.
Are you happy ?
Yes —and you ?
Look in my eyes when the flame breaks
through.
Shut the world out,
Close the door,
This is our heaven when day is o’er.
VICTORIA.
G. J.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin*” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 25, 1912.
A LESSON FROM TRIPOLI.
In Tripoli the badly-armed Arab and the
scarce and not too well-armed Turk appear
to be still holding off the great army and
navy of Italy. The war correspondents hav-
ing either left the Italian army, or been sup-
pressed by the censor, and the Turkish postal
arrangements, such as they were, being prac-
tically cut off by the enemy’s occupation of
the coast, information, except such as comes
from official Dago sources, is few and far
apart. And of late, owing to the rarity of
official Dago news —so far, at least, as the
land campaign is concerned —people began
to infer that there, were no tales of victory
to tell, and that the invaders were too
downhearted to invent any. The few scraps
of information that happened along all sug-
gested that Italy was still hanging on to
the coast-line, like a half-drowned female
clutching a rock. Nothing was said about
a triumphal march inland, and a series of
victories, and the scatteration of the turbu-
lent and brownish enemy, and the laying of
plans for the reorganisation and administra-
tion of the conquered country. A great deal
was made of certain scrappy adventures at
sea, but the really important subject was
covered with the cloak of reticence, and
buried in the grave of silence, with the
tombstone of obscurity above its bones.
Now, at last, some fragments of rumor drop
in from non-Italian sources, and though they
wouldn’t be worth much under ordinary cir-
cumstances, the consistent Dago murkiness
gives them an air of importance. It is al-
leged that the Mohammedans are holding on
good and hard to a line a few miles inland,
and even retain possession of the wells on
which Tripoli and the adjacent towns used
to depend for their drinking supplies. The
Italians are represented as penned up in a
narrow strip of coastal country, and often
reduced to consuming condensed or imported
water. Their army is very large, but a big
armv that is unaccustomed to desert fighting
is little better than a small one. Often it
is worse, being more cumbersome than a
small one. Their fleet is very strong, but
it.can’t wmlk overland. And Tripoli has no
Nile to supply an easy way to the interior.
It all suggests a gloomy outlook for the
invaders. Even if Turkey could be driven
to conclude a peace by which Tripoli was
handed over to the Dago kingdom there is
no reason to suppose that the Tripolitan
would take any notice of the arrangement, or
that the Turk could make him take notice of
it, or that the Turk would really w r ant to
make him take notice of it. The only
change would be that the war would become
technically a rebellion instead of a strife
between nations, but it isn’t likely that the
Tripolitan cares what it is called. If it
were called a Bible or a boiling spring it
would suit him equally well. The situation
is that there is a very large hornet’s nest in
a very inaccessible position. Turkey says
the inmates of the nest are its old and long-
established hornets, while Italy says that it
has just annexed the hornets. Even if the
Turks grow tired of the argument and tell
the Italians they are welcome to take the
whole darned crowd of hornets, the trouble
will remain much as before. It suggests
giving an infant a wild elephant as a birth-
day present, the animal being then at large
in its native forest and only in need of cap-
ture.
Just here or hereabouts is the place where
the anti-Defence person of Australia will
presently arise and point out that any large
expenditure on Defence preparations is
waste of money, and that what an untrained
or half-trained Tripolitan army can do in the
way of holding its country against fearful
paper odds an amateur Australian force,
raised on the spur of the moment, can do as
well, or better. And the anti-Defence
person will be the same unreasoning fraud
as usual. The strength of the Tripolitan
defence lies in the poverty and misery of the
country. When the hard-fighting man with
the fez retreated from his biggest city, he
had the blessed advantage of leaving his
enemy’s fleet at a very difficult anchorage,
because the unspeakable Turk had built no
breakwater, nor had he dredged out the
alleged harbor. In fact, the Tripolitan,
having no commerce worth speaking of,
wasn’t driven to make arrangements to ac-
commodate either his own ships or a
hostile squadron. When he fled in-
land he only deserted to the invader
a rambling town capable of holding
about 40,000 inhabitants, and the smaller
the town the easier it is for its in-
mates to flee into the wilderness and live on
locusts and wild honey. He left behind him
no valuable furniture or works of art or
priceless libraries; no cash in the banks, for
he owned no banks, and the visible cash was
about twopence ; no tramcars, ships or
trains. He sacrificed no costly docks, foun-
dries, engineering shops or factories. The mer-
chandise abandoned in shops and ware-
houses, after removing as much as possible
per mule, was hardly worth mentioning.
Mr. Tripoli didn’t even leave a decent pave-
ment behind him for the Italian to steal and
hang on his watch-chain, or a first-class
hotel, or a Y.M.C.A., or a House of Parlia-
ment, or a mayor and corporation, or a single
capable policeman. He took his rifle with
him, and the cannon he didn’t take with him
were as old as Beelzebub, and twice as
cracked as the Salvation Army.
* * * *
Furthermore, it wasn’t necessary for this
swift Mohammedan to cut the railway as he
evacuated old Tripoli, or to guard it against
seizure by the enemy as an alternative to
cutting it, for there was no railway. He
didn’t require to cover the roads behind him
with a strong rearguard, for there were no
roads —only a huge cloud of dust and a tre-
mendous buzz of insects. No regiments were
wasted in a desperate fight for the possession
of the city’s Prospect reservoir and Cataract
dam, or its Yan Yean water supply, or any-
thing of that sort, for the city water was
brought in per cart, or in a goat-skin bag on
a brown man’s back from wells some
distance inland. When the Italians
captured the metropolis on the coast
they didn’t even capture a reliable
drink of water. When the Mohammedans
retired into the desert they retired into a
place which was just about as good as the
metropolis, and which suited many of them
just as well. The dust was the same; the
garbage was less; the facilities for leading
the Higher Life weren’t much different.
They weren’t in the position which the popu-
lation of Sydney would occupy if it had to
abandon the beer and fleshpots of its city
and betake itself to the horrors of Tiboo-
burra. They left the enemy a million smells
and a few billion germs and sundry tunnels
and dead fortifications, and went themselves
to the fresh air and the springs. Because of
the lack of roads and railways they could
make their position as strong by retreating
15 miles as the population of a civilised
capital could if it fled 100 or perhaps 500
miles. Tripoli fell back on the entrenchment
of its ignorance, and got behind the solid
wall of its ancient retrogression. And the
Italians can’t depress its spirits by marching
inland and taking Bathurst and Goulburn
and Bendigo and Ballarat and Kalgoorlie.
It escapes depression by having no such
places.
The Tripolitan defence is interesting, as
revealing how a country with hardly
anything to lose can hold its own
against an enemy who gets annoyed
when he doesn’t capture anything worth
having. But it only shows Australia
how not to do things. A community with a
complex civilisation, including great coastal
cities, railways, tramways, telegraphs, tele-
phones, banks, insurance offices, a Federal
note issue, a huge public debt, a vast
gold reserve, old age pensions, theatres,
opera houses, mortgages, newspapers, docks,
shipping, ferry boats, Owe’Sulltvan’s won-
derful railway station, the largest com-
merce per head on earth, water-
works, gas-works, Speaker Willis, the fort
at Pinchgut, the merry-go-rounds at Manly,
and the like, can’t retire in a great dust-
cloud at a moment’s notice, feeling hardly
any poorer, and leaving its enemy no richer.
It has got to stand and fight, and to do that
it requires the means and apparatus for
fighting. If it did retreat, it has been spoiled
by the luxury of abundant baths and plenty
of tram-cars for the kind of life that the
wilderness would offer it. It has built too
many roads to make Granville a place where
a motley army could stand at bay, and wave
a green banner, and threaten the foreign
garrison of Sydney with death by thirst. It
would be struck by a serious trouble long
before it reached Granville. By the time the
retreat had got as far as Newtown, the
country would probably have made default
on its public debt, and hopelessly wrecked
its credit —-a disadvantage to which the
Tripolitan is a stranger, for he never had
any credit. Civilisation has its responsi-
bilities and its drawbacks. One of the draw-
backs is that the old, cheap, cursory methods
of defence don’t fit the case.
A STATE MINISTER WHO IS LABOR
BY TRADE AND “LIBERAL” BY
CONVICTION.
Attorney-General W. A. Holman, of
N. S. Wales, appears to think that any
frayed old yarn will do for use against the
Nationalist policy—so long as the tattered
circumstance is repeated often enough. He
claimed last week, for about the 700th time,
to be all for the New Protection, and all for
legislation which would curb or destroy in-
jurious monopolies, but wound up with the
announcement that: —
He could not see how the proposal to hand over
the control of domestic commerce to the Federal Par-
liament was in any way involved in effectuating the
widest ideals of the party to which he belonged.
It is the unconvincing, hypocritical old gag
that was trotted out daily and nightly
throughout the recent Referenda campaign,
by every Tory orator in the Commonwealth.
Such determined anti-Laborites as Irvine,
Cook, Wade and Garland repeatedly ex-
plained that they considered the New Pro-
tection ah excellent measure, and one that
would be supported by 90 per cent, of Aus-
tralians if it could be submitted, by itself,
in the form of a referendum question. Also,
they said that they wanted to see monopolies
efficiently controlled —though, in those days,
there was a tendency amongst the “Liberals”
to put up the bluff that injurious vends
didn’t and couldn’t exist here. But, on one
point, the entire gang whs absolutely solid.
Much as they yearned to see the manufac-
turer compelled by law to pay his hands
wages commensurate to the profits his in-
dustry was earning with the help of the
tariff; and earnest as they were in the good
cause of anti-Trustism, they firmly declined
to accept these boons at the price of an
amendment of the “Trade and Commerce”
clause in the Federal Constitution. They
protested in the sacred name of State Rights
against any such revolutionary change.
Holman’s pronouncement, in this connec-
tion, last week, was identically the same as
the pronouncements of ripe old Tories like
Bruce Smith during the early part of last
year. Said this amazing “democrat”: —
It (i.e., the proposed amendment) carried the most
serious consequences to the governing powers of the
States. Such a sweeping accession of power would
include all the instruments of commerce. Every one
of the agencies employed in domestic commerce would
be an agency subject to the exclusive control of the
Federal Parliament. Among the instruments of do-
mestic commerce were roads. It would be within the
Federal Government’s power then to strip the local
governing bodies of their powers. The railways
would similarly pass under Federal control. He did
not think those who put the Referenda proposals for-
ward fully realised this.
Domestic commerce itself was so universal that the
transfer of so wide a power would practically leave
all the vital powers outside the hands of the State
Parliaments. He failed to see, in that position, why
the State Governments should continue. It was a
most revolutionary change in the relations of the
States and the Federation. It would injure the States
without having any compensating Federal benefit.
* * • •
The danger that Holman, in common
with the “Liberal” party, sees, or affects to
see, ahead, is that the Australian Parlia-
ment, if vested with certain new powers in
regard to trade and commerce, inay at once
proceed to wickedly abuse them. Willie
Kelly invested the beginning of the Refer-
enda campaign with an element of low
comedy, not to say knockabout farce, by re-
tailing to a number of astounded old ladies
and flappers in Paddington (Sydney), a
number of the things that the Federal Par-
liament might do if his hearers didn’t vote
“No” to the “sweeping accession of legisla-
tive power” which the Federal Labor party
was demanding. “Give us this power,”
prattled William, agitatedly, “and we may
nationalise every fruit and lolly shop, every
newspaper stall, and every oyster joint in
the land. We may take over the ferry boats
and charge you ss. apiece to travel from Mil-
son’s Point to Circular Quay. We may re-
sume every ice-cream barrow in the Com-
monwealth, put our own officials in charge
of the vehicles, and sell inferior ices for
thrice —aye! ten times their present price.
We may seize and run in the most demented
fashion conceivable all the grocery shops,
butcheries, gin-palaces, warehouses, trams,
trains and ’buses in the land ; and there will
be none to say us nay, for Freedom will have
gone out of the business with a shriek, and
wire-stealing will have become a thing of
the golden past—the State Rights party
having been strangled and forever buried.”
He mayn’t have used those exact words, but
they faithfully represent the sense of his
observations. The foolish females wandered
out into the night air in a semi-paralysed
frame of mind. They didn’t reflect that
State legislators have for years enjoyed free
license to do all these things, and more, and
yet have never essaved any of them. Under
the N. S. Wales Constitution the members
of the Government to which W. A. Holman
owes allegiance ‘may, if they wish, pass a
law compelling every citizen of Sydney to
traverse George-street, one or more times
daily, on his or her hands and knees. They
ma Tr resume the Australia Hotel, and
turn it into a combined flat and winter-
jungle for the chimpanzee at the Tivoli
Theatre: likewise, they may burn all the
houses in Balmain that happen to have
striped blinds, and raze every church and
chapel in the State to the earth. In short,
there is no end to the wild things that the
State Parliament may 1 : do—but doesn’t.
It doesn’t do them for the same reason that
no conceivable Australian Parliament is
likely to do the mad, inequitable deeds which
State Frightism pictures as a necessary cor-
ollary of the “sweeping accession of power”
resulting from the “revolutionary change”
that Holman dreads, or pretends to dread —
namely, the simple, patent facts that Minis-
ters and members of Parliament mostly
want to hold their jobs, and that, to do
things which are (1) insane and (2) con-
trary to the public interest, represents the
surest means of losing those jobs and never
getting others.
The above are the oft-stated reasons why
the whole of the “Liberal” or anti-Aus-
tralian party, and such false-alarm demo-
crats as W. A. Holman, object to a Consti-
tutional amendment, which is at the very
root of the national policy. By themselves,
these arguments are a shade too thin to
carry weight with any but “Liberal”
females, commercial mfcn, cheap clerks and
the like, who are notoriously illiterate in a
political manner of speaking. So Holman
has to attempt some further justification of
his anti-Labor attitude, and he does it by
affecting to treat the principle which ap-
pears so vital to his Federal brethren as
quite negligible. “What Labor interest
necessitated this change?” he asked, ingenu-
ously, last week. The answer is that the
New Protection is a Labor interest, and that
Commonwealth control of monopolies (which
is to say, the only possible control wfiicli is
going to be effective) is a Labor interest,
and that neither of these reforms is likely
to be achieved so long as the Trade and
Commerce clause stands in its present form.
This view has been laid down, in set terms,
by W. H. Irvine, B. R. Wise, W. M.
Hughes, and every other Constitutional
lawyer of note —whether “Liberal” or
Labor —in Australia, and it has been up-
held by repeated decisions of the High
Court. The Excise Act was that feature of
the New Protection which had for its object
the securing of fair wages to employees. No
one disputed the justice or desirability of
the measure. But the High Court found
in Rex v. Barger that “even if this Act
were a proper exercise of the Common-
wealth’s powers of taxation, yet it was bad,
because it infringed upon the implied pro-
hibition against interference with the inter-
nal trade and commerce of the States.” In
other words, the Excise Act was killed by
the clause' whose amendment, according to
W. A. Holman, is not necessary in the inte-
rests of Labor. The Australian Parliament
thought it had power to make effective
anti-Trust laws. Also, without the slight-
est doubt, the vast majority of the electors
wished the National Parliament to have
that power. With the object of ensuring
that the consumer should stand on as good
a wicket, financially, as the manufacturers
on the one hand and the manufacturers’
employees on the other, Parliament pro-
ceeded to “make laws respecting trading or
financial corporations formed within the
limits of the Commonwealth.” The High
Court decided in Appleton’s case that “if
the words, ‘trading or financial corporations,
etc.,’ stood alone, without any qualifying
or controlling context, they might be capa-
ble of bearing the construction contended
for by the Commonwealth Parliament. But
if,” the Court proceeded, “Parliament can
declare an act of a trading or financial cor-
poration, which is lawful under State law,
to be unlawful under Federal 1aw.... this
is a flagrant invasion of the powers of the
States to make laws in respect of their own
internal trade and commerce.” The prac-
tical effect of that judgment was to give
Trusts the freest of legs in Australia. And
alleged Laborites of the Holman stamp
can’t see what Labor interests necessitate
a change in such a state of affairs!
* * * *
The Holman alternative to the amend-
ment of a Constitutional clause which has
nullified practically all the industrial re-
forms attempted by the Australian Parlia-
ment, is, seemingly, some vague scheme
based on a unanimous handing-over of cer-
tain powers by the States to the Common-
wealth. Setting aside the circumstances
that unanimity in regard to free gifts of the
sort is not usual where State Governments
are concerned, anu that, at the very best
and brightest, there is no likelihood of such
a scheme coming to anything for years,
while more Trusts are arising, and more
wage-earners are being sweated every week,
Holman’s proposal exhibits him in the
anomalous light of one who poses as a
democrat, and yet fears to trust the demo-
cracy. The fresh powers asked for by the
Nationalists are not to be handed to for-
eigners, but to Australians — Holman’s own
countrymen, returned to Parliament on the
most democratic franchise in the world. Of
what is he afraid? To the impartial on-
looker it appears that his fears are bunkum,
and that he is merely playing the old game
of the immemorial Tory obstructionist
i.e., clouding the real issue by means of
vague, horrible threats, and putting forward
impracticable proposals in order that demo-
cratic measures may be delayed or defeated.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Sydney’s Wonderful Kailway Station.
It is possible to talk* so long and unani-
mously about the urgency of a work that
everybody loses all inclination to do it. That
is to say, if the work were done, nobody-
could talk any longer about the urgent need!
of doing it, and that subject of conversation,
having become an institution, life would be
a blank without it. The case is akin to.
that of the Society for the Conversion of the.
Jews. It exists to convert all Jews, but ifc
doesn’t want to do it, because, if it did it, the
society would lose its job, and might have
to take to repairing the roads. It is on ai
somewhat similar nrinciple that the N.S.W.
Government is still talking about the des-
perate need for a Sydney city railway, and
a lot of new suburban lines, and tube rail-
ways, and railway communication across the
harbor, just as many of these works have
been talked of for the last 50 years, and yet
it isn’t digging the first hole or laying the
first brick of any one of them. Rather, it talks
vaguely about referring them, for the 10th
or 50th time, to a committee of inquiry, or


some such refuge of undecided politicians. On
the other hand, it proposes to go ahead im-
mediately, and at a cost of nearly £2OO 000
ilwhoenlargement and completion of the
mi solaced terminus—that palatial
Sdle out near Redfern The great Owe’-
Sullivan mistake is bad enough as things
stand, hut it isn’t too late to repair it,
whereas, if it is anchored down on its arti-
ficial hump by such a vast amount of new
Sowed money, it will probably become a
permanency. Its great drawback is that,
as it stands, it is only fitted to be a terminus.
The trains come in on an up-grade, and end
their journey on top of n m3.nufn.ctui ed hill, so
that they won’t be liable to arrive at a pace
that is beyond reason or control, and knock
the dead end to perdition that dead end that
shouldn't be there. If the line is extended
through the city and across the harbor, as it
ought to be, then either the whole place must
be reconstructed, or else the trains must re-
sume their journey by falling over a preci-
pice. No one would have built such a place
unless he was under the delusion that he
was the last word in railway extension, and
that, after him, everything was finished. The
only advantage that has ever been claimed
for the misdirected castle at Itedfern is that
it is “central,” which is about the worst
thing that could be said for it. The beastly
centralness of the place has been its undoing.
When a large city has only one line, and it
ends in the centre of the place, the natural
result is that the line only traverses half
the city, and the other half has no line at
all. The failure of the central policy is evi-
denced by the fact that the Railway Depart-
ment runs a line of trams to carry the rail-
wav passengers the rest of the way, and how
little use the bulk of these passengers have
for the “central” station is evidenced by the
facts that three-fourths or more of them
rush for the trams and hang on like grim
death till they get to about Iving-street.
The present central station would have some
points as a terminus for trains coming in
from the northern suburbs and crossing the
harbor, but the folk from the southern and
western suburbs want their final stopping
place not far from Circular Quay. In other
words, the line should run right through,
and a main suburban train should be a train
running from Parramatta or Ilomebush. to
somewhere in the vicinity of Hornsby, while
branches from the eastern suburbs should
join the main line somewhere in the city.
But no city railway system would fit in
with the plan of a big station when the
trains stop three stairs up, so to speak, and
can’t progress any further unless they throw
themselves out of the front window and
arrive with a crash in the street below. It
was expressly devised to prevent extension.
When the folly began to be realised, various
attempts were made to explain away the
position, and plans were drawn showing how
a sort of twopenny branch line might be
carried into the city from the basement of
the amazing structure (there was something
humorous about the idea of the biggest and
proudest city in Australasia being content
with a branch, as if it were Gundagai or
Brewarrina). Yet the old blunder refused to
be covered up, and if another £175,000 or
£200.000 is spent on it, it will be the same
old blunder, only bigger and madder and
more conspicuous.
——.—
A Bridge and Its Relation to Party Politics.
From the S. M. Herald (18/1/T2) :
We cannot imagine the very serious matter of con-
nection with North Sydney by bridge or tunnel being
delayed as it is if that constituency were represented
'by a Labor member, or if Labor constituencies were
‘clamoring for relief.
It is the identical tip which this paper of-
fered the Northern Suburbs last month. For
years the short-sighted little snobs, who are
in the majority on the other side of the
water (the three-hundred-a-year millionaires,
as someone has called them) have been vot-
ing the Tory ticket With an unwearying
fidelity, which is rarely to be found outside
the kennel. As a natural result, the yearn-
ings of Gordon,- St. Leonards, Middle Harbor
and Lane Cove have been simply scouted by
the “Liberal” party bosses during the de-
cades that they have held the keys of the
Treasury. The party organisers have hardly
troubled to throw to these reliably anti-Labor
constituencies the mendacious promise which
cheers, even if it doesn’t inebriate, the
hearer with hope and joy. Actually, the other
day, C. G. Wade, who represents Gordon,
had the cynical nerve to go up-country and
denounce the idea of blewing money on the
bridging of Sydney Harbor, while there were
deserving primary producers clamoring for
more railways in the backblocks. A rare
liberty to take with a constituency like Gor-
don, which is understood to regularly incor-
porate a request for a bridge with its grace
before meat. It was ever thus under party
government. North Sydney’s tragedy is that
it has nothing to sell, in a manner of speak-
ing. The “Liberal” League or Caucus, which
has most say, in the last resort, regarding
the way in which the cash at the disposal
of the party shall be expended for anti-
Labor purposes, would no more dream of
wasting big grants on the faithful of Wah-
roonga, Turramurra, etc., than a sane house-
holder Would dream of offering an immense
subsidy for the affection of his aged gardener.
People don’t want their gardeners’ love. All
they do want is to be got for a sovereign a
week, or thereabouts, and they know it.
“Liberalism” has got all it wanted out of
North Sydney, in the past, for the price
of a few school-houses and drill-halls.
To squander a vast bridge on the district,
when there are scores of doubtful constitu-
encies waiting to be won over by means of
railways and so on, would be such rotten bad
business from the party standpoint, that the
politician who suggested it would inspire his
colleagues with doubts as to his sanity. Tlie
McGowen Ministry has talked largely about
the harbor connection (just as every other
Ministry has done), but, on all the evidence,
it is- not going to attempt one iota more than
“Liberalism” has attempted—chiefly because
Labor can see no tactical points about pam-
pering a batch of constituencies which have
always been bitterly hostile to it, and" which
show no signs of changing. And, meanwhile,
North Sydney remains as distant from the
goal it hungers for as it was half a century
ago. There are two ways in which the un-
pleasantness might be remedied. One is for
the Northern Suburbs to take the S. M.
Herald’s gentle hint, and elect a Labor
member or two. The oth«r is
them to join in the agitation, which
is gradually gaining ground in Aus-
tralia, for the purpose of abolishing
party government. Till one of these changes
materialises, the North Sydneyite is going
to risk his life and lose his time on com-
fortless ferry craft, as before.
A Bill that Grows Bigger Every Day.
THE.State Premiers who foregathered at Mel-
bourne pottered round somewhat with the
question of a uniform railway gauge. Every
conference potters more or less with that
well-worn subject, and one that didn’t pot-
ter would be null and blank, and no real
conference at all, but a base imitation with-
out the name blown in the bottle. The
lack of results was much as usual, and the
three unfinished failures which it arrived
at were the old familiar three: (l)It didn’t
contrive to decide which gauge it would
like to have; (2) it didn’t resolve how to
set about establishing that gauge, sup-
posing it knew which gauge it was;
and (3) it got no nearer to finding
out how to allocate among the differ-
ent States the cost of establishing the
gauge it was unable to decide on. Of course,
there is a remedy, but the State Premiers
become so heated when it is mentioned that
a steam rises from their heads. The plain
and easy way out of the difficulty is to hand
over the whole blessed railway system to the
Commonwealth, taking its solemn league
and covenant that it will establish a uni-
form ga”uge within a certain period. Then
it could charge the bill to the general rev-
enue, and thus there would be no question
about any division or subdivision of the
bill. If it was considered unnecessary to
interfere with the isolated Tasmanian sys-
tem it would only be necessary t« calculate
how much pm- head the reconstruction had
cost, and refund Tassy its share —or take
over a corresponding bit of the little soot’s
public debt. But the dignity of State Frights
revolts at this obvious way out of the diffi-
culty, and the cantankerousness of State
Frights—each Fright being unable to agree
with any other Fright save in the matter of
hostility to Australia —has always • pre-
vented them doing anything for themselves.
So time rolls on, and, meanwhile, the Frights
are building miles and miles of new railway,
most of which will have to be reconstructed.
The inability of either the Frights or the
Commonwealth to realise that this question
requires to be settled in boiling-hot haste is
expensive. The work must be done some
day, and every day’s hesitation probably
adds £IOOO or £2OOO, or it may be even
more, to the bill that must be paid in the
end.

The Sydney Wharf Labor Difficulty.
A report is on deck to the effect that the
Sydney wharf trouble will be settled, if it
hasn’t already been settled, by private
negotiation. It has been a dreary sort of
business all through. The matter was sup-
posed to have been fixed up by arbitration
some time ago, but there w T as presently a
disagreement, and the same old strike. Then
there was another reference to the Arbitra-
tion Court, and another arrangement. The
employees obeyed part of the award, and
took, no notice of another part—one which
didn’t please them —and nothing happened.
There was a period of semi-deadlock,
and finally an appeal, the other day, to
Minister Beeby. He was asked if the
award would be enforced or not. The reply
was vague and dodgy. It included an allusion
to the wages paid in Melbourne, a complaint
about newspaper criticism, and a sort of
lamentation about the Government’s failure
to get its new Arbitration Bill through last
session. But the fact stuck out conspicu-
ously that there would be no attempt to
enforce the awards of the Arbitration Court
—against the employees, anyhow—so the
. old method of private negotiation is re-
sorted to, and, if it fails, presumably there
will be the old strike. This is, presumably,
the end of compulsory arbitration in N.S.W.
industrial disputes—for the time, at least.
The Government —not having the courage to
enforce the law-—now apparently proposes to
go back to the old voluntary conciliation
method, by which a Court arbitrates if both
parties to the dispute are agreeable, and the
parties accept its decision if they both feel
like it. It is a good arrangement for the
workers while the boom is going strong, and
the demand for labor exceeds the supply.
But it is desperately bad for then in dull
times when work is scarce, as was proved
by the case of the old N.S.W. Conciliation
Court, which had some two or three matters
brought before it in all its useless career,
and did no good with them. Its trouble
wins that when the employees wanted to
conciliate, the employers didn’t, and vice
versa. The weak party always said “Yes,”
and the strong one said “No,” and the em-
ployers then represented the strong party.
Ih days of poverty it is the capitalist who
requires to be coerced to make him live up
to his agreement, and it is then that the
workers are liable to be very sorry if the
habit of enforcing awards has been dropped
and the machinery of compulsion has gone
to the scrap-heap. Already there are signs
that the boom has seen its best days; so,
if compulsory arbitration is abandoned, it
may finally disappear just about the time
when the aggregate boss is very glad to do
without it.

The Troubles of Bathurst and Its Show.
Some day, in straining after a point to take
against Labor, N. S. Wales “Liberalism”
will assuredly burst, and ruin a large por-
tion of the surrounding scenery. Its latest
grievance ’arises from the scurvy treatment
the McGowen Government is represented to
have accorded Bathurst (N.S.W.) in deny-
ing it certain moneys. The S. M. Hekaed
opened out as follows on the subject:—
If a Government chooses, or is Obliged, to squander
its income on Socialistic enterprises, which promise
to involve great risks, it naturally has not the money
available for legitimate public works. No words of
condemnation' can be too strong for such a state of
affairs, but it is hopeless to expect any improvement
while Parliament remains in its present incompetent
condition, and we fear that Mr. Miller’s protest
will prove of little avail.
Miller is the M.L.A. for Bathurst, and here
is his published protest against the “legiti-
mate public works” which have been denied
his constituency:—
A Labor Minister has refused the request for a
national show for Bathurst, and, moreover, has de-
layed the improvements to the model farm, simply
bpcause both had been promised by a Liberal Min-
ister.
In the first place, this enraged politician
holds his seat on the understanding that he
is an anti-Socialist. The bulk of his sup-
porters claim to regard Socialism as some-
thing demented and wrong. But a “Na-
tional Show” is merely a euphemism for a
"Socialistic Show.” Not only does this
variety of Show pocket the usual £ for £
subsidy, but it also pouches a further grant
of £SOO or more from the chronically bleed-
ing State. In fact, it is as much State-
owned to all intents and purposes, as the
Sydney tramway system. If there were any
consistency in Miller and the false-alarm
anti-Socialists who support him, the entire
crowd would be found protesting as deter-
minedly against the violation of their prin-
ciples that is expressed in the proposal for a
State-run Show, as the Bathurst laborites
would protest against an agitation for the
de-nationalisation of the railways. But con-
sistency and logic are things that the back-
blocks “Liberal” does not know by sight
when the prospect of tearing money out of
the State looms ahead. As a matter of fact,
the McGowen Government has done well to
shut down on the “National Show” swindle.
And it will be a glad day for the community
at large when a Government materialises
with sufficient backbone to reform the exist-
ing arrangement of subdising Shows, lock,
stock and barrel. Bathurst presents a par-
ticularly horrible example of the evil effects
of the system. It runs one of the biggest
Shows in Australia. Thousands flock to it
every year, from all over the State. But its
attraction, as regards the vast majority of
its patrons, has about as much relation to
agriculture or to horticulture or to the pas-
toral industry, as a race-meeting has to the
cult of Wowserism. The “ring events” con-
stitute the chief lures. Here trained high-
jumping horses (miscalled “hunters”), ani-
mals which are utterly useless for any but
Show or circus purposes, amuse the crowd
by the hour. Trotting exhibitions, which
are really ; races, give the betting enthusiast
opportunities to back his fancy. Trick-riding,
buck-jumping contests, ladies’ hurdle events,
tandem r&ces, an| so on, draw well. And
then there are the "“side shows.” The letting
of booths and stands to the folk who run
Fat Women exhibits, Aunt Sallies, bars, etc.,
brings in considerable revenue to the promot-
ers of these concerns; and, in some cases,
A.. 11. and P. Societies have been known to
take the Government down for several addi-
tional hundreds per annum, in the matter of
£ for £• subsidy money, by faking the ac-
counts between themselves and the showmen.
In short, many of the A., H. and P. Shows,
though prosperous, are rotten. The only
unquestionable use they serve is to provide
the district where they occur with a magnifi-
cent two or three days’ circus, at Socialistic
prices. As against this, they mop up Social-
istic money, which might be better applied
in a hundred different directions. The idea
that such institutions should be rendered
even more bloated than they are, by means of
further public . grants, is absolutely gro-
tesque. What’most of them need is to be
put on a diet of financial starvation till they
pledge themselves to cease being gigantic vau-
deville entertainments, and to devote them-
selves to the ends for which they were
formed.
r ihe Cause of the Diverse Railway Gauges.
The S. M. Herald adopted a painfully su-
perior attitude towards Victoria when the
latter, the other day, asked the Ma State to
put her hand in her pocket and help in con-
verting the Victorian railways from the sft.
3in. to the 4ft, gauge
Since the change must he made, surely it would be
mere madness to choose a wide gauge simply because
V ictoria made the mistake of having one, and, like
the fox in the fable who had lost his tail, expects all
the rest of us to imitate her. The truth of the
matter is that Victoria is disinclined to do her duty
to the Commonwealth.. . . But those States that have
the standard gauge, and the Commonwealth, which is
about to build its transcontinentals to it, have no
real responsibility for Victoria’s policy, and they
could not reasonably be expected to shoulder her
burdens.
As a matter of fact, N.S.W. has all the re-
sponsibility in the unfortunate matter. The
Sydney Railroad and Tramway Co. pushed
an Act _ through the N.S.W. Parliament,
making it compulsory that all railways, in
the State should be built on the sft. Sin.
gauge, and Victoria and S. Aus. were ofli
cially informed* of the measure. They
both growled at not having being con-
sulted about what ought to have been
a Federal matter; but Ma was a
bumptious and arrogant Ma, then, just
as she is now; so, accepting the situa-
tion, the Vic. railway authorities —two pri-
vate cos. just starting—placed large orders
for sft. 3in. rolling stock, and S.A. did like-
wise. After _ they had got a lot of work
done, Ma, with her usual inability to keep
faith, changed her mind, and passed another
Act repealing the first; whereupon the
N.S.W. line was built on the 4ft. B£in.
gauge. It was this shuffling of Ma that
caused the whole of the present vast trouble.
N.S.W. first beguiled or bullied the two
southern States into adopting the sft. Sin.
gauge, which they didn’t really want, and
then deserted them. Both in selecting the
sft. 3in. gauge, and the 4ft. BJin. one, it
acted as the usual immensely self-important
old character who professed to give the law
to all Australia. In the matter of tin*
change to 4ft. S-jin., it hadn’t even the
decency to notify its neighbors that it had
left them in the lurch.
We—and Our Friend with the Armed Fist.
Something about the friend’s training:—
The small brown men who have been swarming
like white ants over Sydney don’t belong to a nation
that trusts to luck and the school-kid for its defence.
The Jap law requires service from all able-bodied
males of from 17 to 40 —-first preparatory service,
then, at 20, active service, and afterwards reserve
service and depot service. Nobocly, with one excep-
tion, can dodge it if he isn’t a cripple ; the exception
is the only son of a parent of over 60, who is judged
unable to support himself without the son’s help.
The gilded youngster may, as usual, crawl out with
some slight concession ; but even he has to “possess
scholarship at least equal to that of graduate of the
Middle School” ; and the Jap can’t, as a rule, enter
the Middle School until he has had six years at the
Primary School, and he must do another five at the
Middle School before he can graduate. Even this
gilded young scholar’s concession only permits him
to escape with what is known as one-year service-
barrack service, that is. Having got that licking he is
passed to the reserves, and is liable to be called out
for shooting practice like the rest’of them. In 1909
—last figures available—s69,3l7 Jap lings were sum-
moned for the universal-service examination, and all
but 113,318 were passed on to learn' how to defend
their own country or collar somebody else’s. Those
113,318 were temporarily exempted for these reasons:
Necessity to support the family .. 623
Studying at schools . . . . . . 42,394
Studying abroad . . . . . . 33,275
Missing, or under criminal trial . . 27,861
Convicted . . . . . . . . 434
111, etc. 8,731
Total 113,318
The school-teacher is put in a separate class; he gets
six weeks’ training, and is then sent to “terri-
torial service.” The youngster who can’t climb up to
the dizzy height of sft. 3iim can't be an A 1 soldier,
and is passed on to the transport or some such ser-
vice—or, apparently, the navy. The navy is mostly
manned by volunteers, though if there are not enough
of them forthcoming, the complement is drafted in
from the universal-service roll. Thus, in 1907, when
the volunteers didn’t come along freely enough, the
figures were: Volunteers, 4048; drafted, 4564. The
active service in the navy lasts four years and the re-
serve service seven. Youngsters who expect to be-
come officers are attached to either the Naval Cadet
Academy or the Naval Engineering School. The
course runs four years in each instance, “including
one year of practical training on distant voyage”—
which explains the present swarm of white ants.
There are other colleges, but they are not for the
cub —they are for the subsequent production of the
specialist. But not for the officer alone. Thus, the
ordinary artificer on board may go into the Mechanic
School and take a course; but, in return, he must
stay in the service for an extra three or four years.
As for pay, the Japling who is under training in the
army, gets from 156 to 195 sen a month, and a sen is
a farthing or thereabouts. But besides his 3s. 3d. to
4s. a month, he gets 4d. to 6d. to cover the cost of
writing home to mother, and 2d. a day to buy food
to supplement his allowance of rice. Not that there
isn’t other food in the army besides rice, as a rule;
but the Jap Government is a lavish affair, and throws
the 2d. in for luck, and in case the poultry caught
in the rice shouldn’t be as good as it might be.

“A Young Soldier” writes :
The decay of Australia’s compulsory military kid-
training becomes more and more obvious. At the be-
ginning, a few of the youths who were liable for ser-
vice, refused to turn up, and scored at the idea of
learning to defend their native land. If they—or
their parents—had been promptly suppressed, accord-
ing to law, the trouble would probably have ended
there. But a humanitarian Government wanted com-
pulsory training without compulsion. It wanted to
get the applause of the people who believed in Aus-
tralian defence, and not lose the support of those who
didn’t. It was the sort of Government that was
strongly patriotic, and at the same time wouldn’t
speak unkindly to a caterpillar. So the youths who
scoffed and remained away became more numerous,
and though the law gives officers certain (yery
limited) powers of summary jurisdiction, they wore
forbidden to use them. The blessed Government Sus-
pended the law. Then the youths who didn’t actually
scoff began to remain away, or their parents made
them remain away. Universal training puts all on a
lev,el, but when one youth spent part of his time at-
tending drills and another didn’t, the law-keeper pre-
sently found himself at a disadvan.*ge in the indus-
trial market as against the law-breaker-—the hoodlum
who jeered at the idea of military training, and
called his officer “turkey legs.” As the Government
kept all its sympathies for the law-breaker the disease
spread. Some absentees —youths employed at the
Victorian State coal mine—declared that they were
threatened by a mine official with loss of a shift if
they attended drill, and nothing happened to the
official. All that happened was that the matter was
hung up for three months. Officers, left with practi-
cally no power, are growing weary of the business.
Now the second year’s recruits are due for enrolment,
but so far they aren’t enrolling to any great extent.
A conviction is spreading that Defence is all a moth-
eaten election jest. Possibly it will require tome
thousands of prosecutions to produce the same effect
now that could have been effected not many month*

tions in the Grand Canyon.

— Casual, customary par. in S. M. Herald.
“The horseleech hath two daughters”—
singing all day long:
Give! Give! Give! is the burthen of their
song;
Solomon, who heard them, tells us this is
so;
But they were modest persons to some we
know!
Solomon, the Wise Man, knew an awful
lot,
Used to furnish wisdom right upon the spot;
Knew both men and women, and their little
ways—
But never met the cadgers we have nowa-
days !
When the Shechem Council, anxious for a
loan —
Say, to build a goat-pound—waited on the
Throne:
“Daughters of the Horseleech!” Solomon
would say—
“lf we do it for you, how much can you
pan?”
Then the Shechem Council vowed and raved
and si; ore,
By their fathers’ whiskers, they were very
poor!
Still, he was a great King, and —they were
content
To repay his Treasurer, plus ten per cent!
Whereupon the Great King sweetly smiled
and said :
“See to it, O Treasurer —upon thy head !
Make it known in Israel, while \Ve live,
Those who often seek boons must some-
times give!”
If you doubt the story—as, of course, you
may—
Recollect the maxim— Someone has to pay-
How do you imagine Sol. kept up his
Throne,
If, at times, he didn’t charge Shechem for
its loan?
As it was in Salem, so it is to-day;
Even in Australia, someone has to pay!
When the cadger Council for “special
grants,”
The Little Boy at Manly has to pawn his
pants!
Let our busy Beebys take a little leaf
From the Book of Solomon —’twill save
them grief!
To our cadging Councils let them gently
say:
“Daughters of th n Horseleech ! How much
ran you pay?”

Wanted, an Export Duty on Fire Insurance
Offices.
In days gone by, England built up its wool-
len industry by prohibiting the export of
wool; quite unlike Australia, which tries to
build up the Japanese woollen industry by
giving a bonus on the export of partially
prepared wool, so that the foreigner may get
the stuff more cheaply than the Australian
manufacturer does. South Africa boosts up
its ostrich-feather industry by prohibiting,
under enormous penalties, the export of
ostriches. This country badly wants a Gov-
ernment which will prohibit the export of
stud sheep, crude ores and fire insurance
offices. The latest thing in the fire insurance
line is the proposed sale of the business of
the old Derwent and Tamar Co., of Tas-
mania, to the London and Lancashire office
of England.' If the transaction comes off, it
will leave Tasmania with the very smallest
fire office of the Commonwealth as its sole
remaining asset in this line of business. The
little isle has a wonderful a-nd abiding faith
in the absentee, and believes cordially in sell-
ing him all its assets and paying him tribute
in the shape of interest, profits and rents for
ever. It must be admitted that the other
States have done a good deal in this line also,
but they realise the danger, which, appar-
ently, Tasmania doesn’t, and they aren’t
proud of their absentee, which Tasmania
apparently is. Moreover, though they have
sold a good few fire insurance concerns to
the foreigner, they have started a few new
ones to partially fill the gap, whereas Tas-
mania never starts anything new these days.
So Tassy is the State which has enjoyed, in
proportion to its population, the biggest in-
rush of foreign capital. And it is, as a
natural consequence, the poorest and most
backward and most hopeless member of the
Australian brotherhood, despite the fact that
it has the best chances and the best natural
endowments. Of course, it will get a certain
price if it sells out the assets and goodwill
of almost the last big financial institution
that is left to it, though it seems to be sell-
ing at a wretchedly poor price. But
even if it got a good price it also
sells the possibilities of the future, and
in a country with so much room for new
population they are immense for any office
with a good reputation .(which the Derwent
and Tamar has) and enterprising manage-
ment (which it hasn’t). With its re-
putation, enterprising management might
have made it 10 times its present
size. Australia and Tasmania could
have sold all their fire insurance con-
nections about 50 years ago for probably
50 per cent, more than their then paper value,
and spent the money on drink or wild cats,
but when the vast expansion of the business
is considered, it is evident they would have
made a rotten bargain. Tasmania certainly
has become a less and less important mem-
ber of the group, as it went more and more
out of the job of being its own financier.
The money it got for jobbing away its fin-
ancial institutions to outsiders has appar-
ently drifted away from it, just like the
money which Charles Surface got when
he also sold the family estate. Australia
wants a heavy absentee tax on outside in-
surance offices which do business within
its borders; and a law to absolutely pro-
hibit the sale of the assets and goodwill of
any Australian insurance company to out-
side capitalists. Also, a Government fire
office wouldn’t be amiss. It could at least
be guaranteed to remain Australian all the
time.
o
So far as any normal human being can judge,
the wearisome uproar concerning the tempor-
ary site of the Naval College is at an end. Gee-
long (Vic.) was well up in the betting at
one time. Its strong suit was the posses-
sion of Osborne House, a vast edifice capable
of housing all our embryo mariners, and a
few over. Greenwell Point, which is on
the Shoalhaven River, near Coolangatta,
was seriously considered by Captain Cham-
bers, the Government’s adviser. And about
420 other sites clamored insistently for re-
cognition. The selection that was finally
made last week, namely Captain’s Point, at
Jervis Bay, seems about as good as any that
was practicable. There is plenty of water
adjacent, fresh and otherwise; the air and
scenery are unimpeachable, and the students
will not be distracted by the festive tripper
who is so out of place in a scholastic en-
vironment. The only conceivable hitch is
the unwillingness of the N. S. Wales Govern-
ment to link up Bomaderry and Jervis Bay
by means of a railway. It is a dreary fact
that the McGowen Ministry is just as
much the slave of Sydney vested interests
as any of its predecessors were. Sydney has
always fought strenuously against anything
tending to benefit rival ports, such as Jervis
Bay, and it may be relied on to continue do-
ing so. If the inevitable movement suc-
ceeds the whole futile, unprofitable business
of tearing about, searching for sites, will
start once more. If it doesn’t, N. S. Wales
will have gained in at least two ways: it
will have had an awkward question settled in
as satisfactory a way as was possible in the
circumstances, and it will have made a
feeble, tentative sort of move in the direc-
tion of decentralisation.

This is the State Premiers’ day for clamor-
ing about their special “right” to the use of
the savings of the Australian people, as
against the Federal Government’s equally
good “right,” and it is their time
for quite ignoring any claim the
people, who own the savings, may
have to be consulted in the matter. Still,
the case for the people—the depositors—
might be considered. The State Premiers
have three reasons, or sets of reasons, for
hanging on to the Savings Banks: (1)
Meanness, quarrelsomeness and hatred of
the Australian Government because it is
Australian; (2) a wild idea of their own
importance; (o) the fact that the State
Savings Banks are a means whereby they get
cheap and fairly-abundant loan funds. The
people put their money into the Savings
Banks because they are regarded as safe
institutions. The State Government’s
annex a great deal of the money at rates
fixed by themselves. It is quite probable
that the management could often make big-
ger profits for its clients, the depositors, if it
went into the open market and bought up
such existing securities (of any Australian
State) as might chance to be offering, in-
stead of taking new securities from the local
State Government at par, but this is a view
of the case which apparently doesn’t appeal
to the State insects. One of the bitterest
objections which they offer to the transfer
of the savings bank business to the Com-
monwealth is that, under Federal manage-
ment, the money of one State might some-
times be invested in the securities of an-
other. Why should it not be so invested if
it happened at the moment that the return
was better? Surely the depositors should
have a look in now and then. The fixed
opinion of the State Premiers appears to
be: (1) That the people have no right to
transfer their Savings Bank business to their
own Australian Government if the State
Premiers object; (2) that they have no
right to even say whether they want to
transfer it or not, unless the State Premiers
are willing; (3) that they have no right
to get the highest interest they can, but
should take whatever figure the State Pre-
miers choose to borrow their money at. In
fact, Denham and McGowen and Murray
and Verran don’t seem quite sure that the
people have any rights at all.
