Here beginneth the weary weekly task of
saying that “The Quaker Girl” continues to
be a tuneful success at Her Majesty’s, Syd-
ney. The writer rpakes the full and frank
confession that he hasn’t seen it for 10
days, and doesn’f know a thing more about
it than he did 10 days ago; but the impetus
which it got at the start may be relied upon
with perfect safety to take it along for
weeks. Meantime, he recalls that he didn’t
previously mention that Bantock makes a
really picturesque Quaker; that Mrs.
George Lauri is back on the programme,
keeping the pub where the runaway couple
do the cooing while the landlady does the
billing; that once more Miss Nellie Wilson
is a bad, bold actress (why are actresses
always asked to represent actresses as bad,
bold hussies?) ; that Miss Galvin is a very
acid Aunt Rachel, of the (Quaker persua-
sion ; and that Board and Little have
painted some scenery much better than Na-
ture does it as a rule.
“A Woman of No Importance,” now resid-
ing at the Palace, under Plimmer-Denniston
management, is, theatrically speaking, a per-
son of great charm and considerable import-
ance. It is the sort of play and company
that the discerning play-lover says farewell
to with sincere regret. In the meantime, he
goes along as numerously as possible, and
hoards up pleasant recollections against the
long, lean months when the theatrical ban-
queting board will be bare save for a little
cold tripe and chestnuts.
is. is is *
Ike and Will Scott got back to the Tivoli,
on Saturday, after an absence of four years.
Their business is foolery and stepping; the
foolery is smart and the stepping is light.
The boyish Du Foy Trio of dancers also
came back, on Saturday, for a short season.
Artist Bert Levy is due to depart on Friday;
but the names of Cakewalk Johnson, Pastor
and Merle, Tom Dawson and Fanny Powers
still lend enchantment to the bill. Herbert
a comedian from England, will endea-
vor to bring down the house for the first
time this (Thursday) evening.
The few careless individuals who have
missed seeing the other side of Ethel Irv-
ing’s art —the tempestuous, cyclonic side —
as expounded in “Dame Nature,” at the
Criterion, are hereby warned that the storm
centre will not remain longer in Sydney
than Saturday week. After that, Ethel
Irving will take her* finished art back to
dissipate the fogs of her own land; _ and
modern light comedy, as far as Australia is
concerned, will be but a remembersome
memory in an arid waste of “Quaker Girls”
and “Cingalees.”
“The Girl in the Train” ends her long,
cheery and profitable journey on Friday
night next. She has been over two months
travelling from the front door of the Cri-
terion to the back aperture of the Royal;
and it is something of a tribute to her
charm that she has not bored a soul during
the trip. That well and favorably known
old chestnut, “The Cingalee,” succeeds her
on Saturday night, at the usual hour and
prices.

National Amphitheatre is a popular haunt
nowadays, and the vaudeville enthusiast
who would win him a seat must come early
and push hard. One Alick Lauder, a humor-
ist and a brother of Harry Lauder) is in
the cast. It is alleged—and after observing
the visitor’s turn the writer is fain to think
that there is something .ip the tale; —that
Alick is exhibiting his aft 12,000 miles from
Scotland, in order to be wpll out of the reach
of brother Henry’s vengeful claymore. He
is essentially one of those wits whom the
family prefers to see acting under an alias.

people cover a range of sub-
jects at their three Sydney houses, and the
compilation of six distinct programmes a
week must call for some huqtling —it means
anything from 35 to 50 items. The Lyric
has been showing two absorbingly interest- 1
ing films, one picturing aeroplanes and the-
other torpedo-boat firing. Now, on the
change of programme, the humorous side is

1. Colonial No. 2 has had a good indus-

Earthenware” now takes its< place. But
the educational picture seems tp occur less
frequently than it used to. The bulk of the

dies and melodramas —pretty “cheap” stuff
mostly.
Parsons and otherwise are reminded that

Salv’army ballet beseeching the house to
“Yield Not to Temptation” would be a wel-
come addition, what there is of it is real
24-carat melodrama and worth while.

The Exhibition Building, in Prince Alfred
Park (Sydney), is being swept and gar-
nished, prior to receiving the inhabitants
of Tiny Town as its guests. They will be
at home there to the general public twice a
day from February 3. Ladies who attend
are requested not to knock over the Tiny
Town post-office with their skirts, and any-
body who puts the 30in. Gaud Mayor in his
pocket will be prosecuted with the utmost
fury of the law.
The foregoing reads like a charitable
notice of that old-established outrage, “The
World Against Her/’ now dragging its
loose-jointed narrative around the stage of
the King’s (Melb.) for 12 nights only. Per-
haps the most conspicuous crudity of a terri-
bly popular attraction is that its two princi-
pal events are both due to the drapery and
millinery trade. When woeful Ned Duggan
is required to meet his lost daughter by
accident, or Miss Duggan needs to be brought
face to face with the husband, who cast > her
off, they are sent out with drapery parcels
to deliver at the respective domiciles of their
kith and kin, and thus the long arm . of coin-
cidence pushes them, a» it were, into the
way of reconciliation.
The Plying Ballet, the tricky Sleeds, the
Shipwreck, together with all the other fea-
tures of “Sinbad,” are repeated eight times
a week at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne), as
before. Last week thg principal girl (Nell
Pinnis) was invalided/and her part fell to a
pretty understudy, who wanted only a fuller
sense of self-sufficiency to make her a com-
plete success. The Dame (Pip Powell) is
still going strong; her new qnd tuneful hus-
band, Howard, has shaken down into his
place; Drew and Alders perpetrate their in-
genious mechanical jokes without fail; the
Emperor of Sahara has not ceased to be less
funny than he might be ; and Celia Ghiloni
still persists in looking handsome, although
the panto, indicates that ugliness is expected
of her. And everything in the garden of
girlhood is lovely.
Life at Cripple Creek, made up of ex-
cursions and alarms, with amorous and alco-
holic interludes, is still being vividly and
vociferously represented at the Princess’s.
The buxom hero exudes moral claptraptious-
ness with the fervor of an indignant jelly-
fish : the villain-in-chief displays a criminal
energy, which is wasted on account of its
alliance with Colossal incapacity; the fun-
ereal “Injun” wins sympathy by intoning
the narrative of his lost love; the reformed
Dynamite Ann keeps off fermented and
spirituous liquors ; and Ben White, the nig-
ger, wanders about like a fugitive from a
variety show. For the rest, the show at the
Princess’s is mostly revolvers and female
screams. It includes all the elementsof cow-
boy picture-drama except wild horses.
In “Leaves of Romance,” at the Prahran
Lyric (Vic.) last week, the audience got its

thrown in. The hero of the film was a.
buckskin Apollo called Bud or something,
and he raised loud cheers by eloping with a
lady who had gone quite “potty” on his
handsome eyes and the flaxen curls on his
noble brow. A picture that was almost
equally cheered was “The Train Des-
patcher,” in which a crippled kid and a
forlorn young female figured with tearful
results. Of more than average merit was a
scenic subject, showing some views of Trieste
Harbor and its vicinity, including the beau-

THE WORLD AGAINST HER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A is the Anguish a matron goes through,
Whose love for her husband is loyal and true;
B is bad Beckett, the traitorous friend,
Who sticketh at naught to accomplish his end ;
C’s the Chee-ild, who is kidnapped, of course,
From mumma, poor victim of wrongful divorce ;
D is Ned Duggan, a spectacle sad,
His flighty young daughter has gone to the bad ;
E is Eugenie, the mourner-in-chief;
F is her Foolishness,
G is her Grief;
H stands for Happiness (also for Hat —
To speak of Miss Duggan reminds me of that)
Which crowns, like a garland of roses, at last
The brow of the wife with a wonderful Past;
I is the Insult, so hard to endure
By matrons in drama whose morals are pure;
J is the Johnnie or Juggins inane
Who loves the young heiress, nor simpers in vain ;
K is the King’s where ill-doers are “dirt,”
And Virtue is Virtue, and Bailey is Bert;
L is the Larf that he labors to raise ;
M is the Mirth which his efforts repays;
N stands for Nemesis, stealthy and slow,
Who follows the wicked wherever they go.
O is the Orchestra, giving the cue
When dark is the deed that the wicked would do;
P is the Public that holdeth its breath
If the big double bass sounds a warning of death;
Q is the Quidnunc, so blas§ and proud,
Who knows what is coming and states it aloud ;
It is Roy Redgrave, who “chucks” with disdain
The wife of his bosom, then weds her again ;
S is the Scenery, gloomy or smart;
T is the “Triumph of mechanist’s art” ;
U is the Uproar that always is heard
When the gallery’s feelings are properly stirred ;
V is the Villain who falls with a crash ;
W’s Miss Welton, his wonderful mash.
But vainly the poet has tortured his head
To find fit excuses for X, Y and 52.

West’s Melbourne building last week, deals

stuffed with a profusion of exciting epi-
sodes in which' well-drilled supers unam-
Si, perished while defending their
lunch-bags to the last. . A few.drops of ink
can be expended in praise of Gipsy Blood,
a stormy yarn of Danish extraction ; while
“A Ride for a Bride” is also worthy of
limited approval.

Quite a number of the daily paper obitu-
aries of Henry Labouchere, proprietor of
London Truth, who passed out last week,
at the age of 81, remark that “he always
declined office.” Labouchere entered the
British House of Commons in IS6G, and
stayed till old age climbed on his shoulders.
He was a brilliant writer, a brilliant
speaker and a courageous exposer of shams,
frauds and thieves —-in short, he was so far
above the ruck that it was regarded as the
right thing to say that he always declined
office in order to explain why he never got
it. As a matter of fact, he had as much
ambition as any other M.P., and Gladstone
once included him in the scheme of a new
Ministry. But Queen Victoria loathed bril-
liance and originality, and had no use for
anybody who threw bricks at the conven-
tional shams of our civilisation. So
Labouchere was barred, and probably some
dull old beer-prig with a purchased title got
the portfolio that should have been his.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Edith Crane, whose death is reported f roin
America, though not a gifted actress, made a strik-
ingly realistic Trilby. An intelligent artist enough,
with the necessary stage experience, Miss Crane had
the right face and figure for the part, ana equally
important—the right voice. A strong voice, neither
harsh nor shrill nor husky, but distinctly taulty in
its intonation. The tone-deafness of Du Maurier s
Trilby was suggested as soon as the actress opened
her mouth. She had no music in her speech, hence her
impersonation of a Trilby who couldn’t sing a note
save when Svengali hypnotised her, was as nearly
convincing as the theatrical character of the 1 1 lay
would permit. And the play had some memorable
moments of silent theatrical effect. The changing
picture of the dumb group in the studio, sentimen-
talising under the spell of Svengali’s music, was a
finely arranged scene. People unconsciously leaned
forward to watch the mimic effect of the music on
the three men and the girl they loved Miss Crane
being the girl. Trilby was the only part she played
during her first visit to Australia in 1896. When
she returned some years later, with Tyrone Power,
and was starred as an emotional actress in three or
four characters, her Trilby was still the best of them,
although it had grown older. Tyrone Power and his
wife ended their visit disastrously by going into man-
agement at Melbourne Princess’s and getting out with
a heavy deficit.
“Dog’s Body”: John McCormack has been telling
us, in Maoriland, some- .cheerful things about Aus-
tralian (he called them “Colonial”) audiences.
“Absolutely,” says Mac.-, “I have no cause for com-
plaint in ' regard to the appreciation that colonial
audiences give to what might be called the better
class of music. I have found an educated apprecia-
tion and a critical faculty that one scarcely ex-
pected to discover in so young a country.... I en-
joyed very much singing to the Sydney and Mel-
bourne audiences —especially the Melbourne people.”
Incidentally, McCormack seems to put the pot on the
much-labored story that the recent opera season in
Australia was a gift from Melba to her home
land. He was asked if he thought it was a paying
proposition for artists who have the highest engage-
ments in the world at their command, to pay Aus-
tralasia an occasional visit. “Well,” he said,
“Melba is scarcely a philanthropist, and she has
been out more than once.”
“0.K.”: “An actors’ union, affiliated with the
Trades Hall” doesn’t strike the ear as a very har-
monious band ; and close union between various
grades of mummers, who are mostly hankering for
somebody else’s job, seems out of the question. Mum-
mers are always looking for limelight rather than fair
industrial conditions —friend Greasepaint would rather
star in the arduous role of Hamlet for £l2 a week
than walk through the part of Horatio for £l5.
The most that can be expected of mummer unionism
is a whole-hearted demand for a minimum wage from
those managements which run big businesses on regu-
lar commercial lines. And the dignity of the purfesh
demands that much unionism at least; for the more
that a manager has to pay to the stage footman or
parlor-maid who carries a tray and speaks only a
dozen words in all, the more he will trouble himself
to select Jhe brightest stage-struck novices obtainable.
“Harrison O.”': Though I had seen Ethel Irving
many times at the Melbourne Royal, I scarcely recog-
nised her when I heard her in “Dame Nature” at
Sydney Criterion —which emphasised the fact that
there is not an available theatre in Melbourne suit-
able for the display of an art such as Miss Irving’s.
Many fine points must be missed in a house like the
Royal, formerly sacred to the delicately-modulated
histrionics of the Blandolt and Bilandersonian schools.
In the Cri., with its cosy dress-circle close to the
stage and its general structural compactness, the
actress is able to get into touch with her audience
in a wav she could not hope to do in any Yarraside
playhouse. No wonder “the firm” realises Mel-
bourne’s need of a small theatre, and intends erecting
one in Russell-street, opposite the King’s.
George Edwardes, of London Gaiety and Daly’s,
and inventor of musical comedy, from whom J. C.
Williamson purchases most of the sprightly Com-
modities he places before Australian playgoers, has
been entertained at a banquet, by way of celebrating
his 25th year of continuous management. In eulo-
gising him, Beerbolim Tree, the chairman, referred to
his great services to the nation, in “infusing a good
deal of new blood into the peerage.”
Florence Imeson’s name is, seemingly, Simeon, with
the first letter put in the fourth place. Anyhow,
the little lady is very palpably Jewish.
The animal “trainer” has been getting some more
limelight on his risks. This time the trouble was at
Wirths’ Circus, which is touring Maoriland, and it
happened during the big thrill in which the lion,
lioness, dog, goat, bear, man, and other wild animals
are locked in the iron enclosure. The lions had been
sent up on their pedestals, and the trainer, raising
the grizzly to his hind hoofs, proceeded to promenade
him. Thereabouts, the hairy pug. swung Iris dirty
left, downed his man, and commenced lunch to the
accompaniment of shrieks, shouts, curses, pitchforks,
tent-pegs, hysterics and Ancient Henry. The proceed-
ings were only one degree worse than the “Liberals”
attacking Speaker Willis in the N. S. Wales Assem-
bly, by reason of the fact than the snarling lions
kept the police from interfering. Fortunately, they
took no further part in the argument, not even when
one Mclnnes entered the enclosure with a sizable
hunk of gas-pipe, and induced the bear to listen to
reason. The trainer was then dragged out and carted
to the hospital. He was not dangerously wounded ;
but the Wirths, reckoning that things might not end
so satisfactorily on the next occasion, took the bear
by the forelock, and made a present of him to the
Wellington Zoo.
One Alexander Galley, who is something in the way
of being a big hat in vaudeville business in England,
has been passing a few opinions concerning
the cinematograph business in England. Galley
admits promptly that the moving picture boom
hit the music-hall where it resided, but he
reckons that the picture craze is on the slump. The
music-hall, or variety theatre, he declares with his
hand upon his reputation, is coming back to its own,
and he desires to be informed how his statement can
be doubted, when a hall offers on its programme
much of the cream of the world’s stage talent. Says
Galley: “It has been a staggerer to the theatrical
world' during the past few years to witness the num-
ber of ‘top-liners’ that have come over to vaudeville.
In one music-hall sketch (say) of 20 minutes,
they can earn more money than for an all-night per-
formance at the theatres.” Galley’s opinion is that
vaudeville will not only entirely recover from the set-
back given it by the picture-show, but will presently
become more popular in Britain than it ever was
before.
Oscar Hammerstein’s new Opera House in Kings-
way, London, seems a comfortable sort of crib. It is
absolutely stiff with lounge- and cloak- and refresh-
ment-rooms ; and, as a further antidote to the sing-
ing, has a delightful roof-garden, where the best of
drinks and so forth are obtainable at corresponding
prices. It also has a “wireless” office, through which
customers at sea can book their seats and send notes
and messages to their friends. There is accommo-
dation for the same number of people, roughly, as in-
habit Mudgee (N.S.W.). The stalls and two galleries
absorb 2300, the rest beng distributed over the 53
proscenium and grand tier boxes and the considerable
acreage available for standing room. Special innova-
tions are the private entrances, one of which is re-
served for Queen Alexandra, while another is kept for
London’s Gaud Mayor. The vast place cost £200,000,
but as an Americanised German Jew is running it,
it should get back all that and a bit over.
“Lud”: A feature of the late Henry Labouchere’s
variegated career that does not seem to have been
touched on in the local papers was his connection
with the stage. After he retired from, or was fired
out of, the profession of diplomacy, he married Hen-
rietta Hodson, an actress, and went into management
at. the Queen’s Theatre, London. He had a good eye
for acting-talent, and engaged a number of mummers
who were “on the rise,” and destined to make names
for themselves, among them being Irving, George
Rignold, Lai Brough and Charles ’VVyndham. Toole
was also one of his employees, though that comedian
had already made good when Labby booked him.
Irving came to Labby from the Princess’s, where,
with Miss Herbert’s management, he had done well
as Doricourt in “The Belle’s Stratagem..” He was en-
gaged there on Boucicault’s recommendation, after
his Bawdon Scudamore in Boucicault’s “Hunted
Down,” at Manchester. Transferring to Labouchere’s
Queen’s, nominally under the Hodson, he won the
high praise of Dickens as Sikes. He acted at the
Queen’s for the first time with Ellen Terry. This was
in “Katharine and Petruchio.” She got twice his
salary or more. Byron’s plays of “Dearer Than Life”
and “Uncle Dick’s Darling,” lifted him somewhat.
He went to the Vaudeville, for his Digby Grand, in
“The Two Roses.” Meanwhile George Rignold ad-
vanced to leading man at the Queen’s, with a par-
ticular hit as Caliban. Irving changed again to the
Lyceum, where his Landry, in “Fanchon,” did not
give satisfaction. Manager Bateman wanted to re-
place him with Rignold, but Rignold declined, on the
advice of Jack Ryder. About this time Labouchere
became an M.P., and dropped the stage for good.
An absolutely inexplicable bit of recent cable
news was the announcement of the death of a mum-
mer named Jacob Strennet, in a Toronto lodging-
house. Described as a “friend of Edwin Booth, and
a co-aetor with Sir Henry Irving,” he may have
been anything from a cheap, low comedy man to
a lugubrious ghost in “Hamlet.” Who was Jacob
Strennet, anyhow? The statement that “he retired
from the English stage in 1889, when threatened
with blindness,” merely intensifies the mystery.
Frank Talbot, back from S’Africa, where he acted
as the Taits’ advance agent for Clara Butt, gives
figures to prove that Capetown is the best city of its
size on earth for “star” concert business. The Butt-
Rumford Co., with Nevin Tait in charge, gave 29
concerts in nine towns. Johannesburg, which has
100,000 whites, was good for nine concerts, but Cape-
town, with only 31,000 whites, swallowed the bait
four times at the outset of the tour, and another
three times (at reduced prices), before Clara left for
England. The Town Hall at Capetown seats 2000
people, and was rushed every time, the prices for the
first season being a guinea, half-guinea and five
“bob.” The Butt programme was much the same as
in Australia, and, strange to say, although S’Africa is
growing more Dutch, and the Taal is supplanting
English in all official directions, audiences everywhere
went wild over the Jingoistic hymn, “Land of Hope
and Glory.” The Mayor of Johannesburg wanted to
engage Clara Butt to sing it at a big municipal ban-
quet, where the Fat men of both nationalities were
to meet for the purpose of clearing the air of all
racial animosities. But the “loyal” corporation wasn’t
prepared to Supply more than half of the fee which
the Taits would have had to pay Clara, and their
“loyhlty” didn’t run to the idea of standing the dif-
ference in order that the lady might sing outside of
their concerts, and her “loyalty” wasn’t reckoned
equal to the strain of warbling at bargain prices. So
the rival Fat men never got the opportunity to be-
come united by silken bonds of song, as it were. An
actor who greatly impressed Talbot in Africa was
Henry Herbert, touring with a co. in Shakspearian
plays. Herbert, he says, is a fine performer and
wonderfully versatile. In one week, with two mati-
nees, he played King Lear, Malvolio, Julius Caesar,
Caliban (in “The Tempest”), Macbeth, Bottom the
Weaver, and Hamlet (twice on the Saturday). Her-
bert, like Asche, graduated in Benson’s school of
poetic drama, and knows his business from A to Z.
Moreover, his talent for all sorts of Shakspearian im-
personations extends to something very like genius
for two or three of them.
“D.” : For sheer unoriginality, conservatism, loath-
ing of change, and so forth, the music-hall pro. is
surely unsurpassed. At a Sydney vaudeville show,
just now, the management is starring one Cavalieri,
a quick-change artist. Fifteen years ago I saw my
first quick-change expert, and his name was Fregoli.
Fregoli made his initial bow in a blue silk knicker-
bocker suit, black stockings, a garishly painted face,
an upstanding lock of hair, and other bizarre appur-
tenances. I identified every one of them in Cava-
lieri last week. The Hebrew monologuist has worn
immense flat boots, a frock coat and a bowler hat,
pressed well down over the ears, since the beginning
of time. Then there are the perpetrators of coon
songs, “refined ballads,” soubrette turns, acrobatic
acts, etc., all of whom have a time-worn livery, and
stick to it like glue. The original comic singer of
vaudeville was, so delvers into history tell me, a poor
man. In fact, he had to appear in rags or in the
nude. His nose was crimson—partly from drink,
partly from the cold, which he had not the means to
ward off. Ever since, the comedian of the music-hall
stage has been a forlorn, tattered, red-nosed outcast,
and it seems that he will continue to be so till the
end. One advantage, and one only, the conservatism
of the vaudeville artist brings to the public. It may
be compared to the R.C. Church’s immemorial prac-
tice of having all its services, the world over, in the
Latin tongue. The vaudeville patron is never at a
loss, or afflicted with a feeling of strangeness in a new
hall. Everything is as it was in the establishment
he visited a week or so previously ; in fact, all is as
it was in the halls he visited in earliest youth.

The Asches, who leave London for Australia early
next month, are going to put in a fortnight’s rest at
Colombo by the way. Their hopes of another good
Australian time are somewhat tinged, with regret that
this visit couldn’t be postponed for a year at least.
The tremendous success of “Kismet” was not foreseen.
Two years ago Oscar and his pretty spouse left Bri-
tain to better themselves, but this time they are m
the thick of prosperity, and have nothing to gain by
shifting camp.
“C. Ockney”: Re-enter Charles Haddon Chambers,
It is long since we have seen a play of his.
In his “Passers-by,” described as a “comedy-drama,”
there are only seven adult characters, and one cheeild.
Haddon is fond of the short cast. His “Tyranny
of Tears” had only six speaking parts. When done
in London, “Passers-by” was a good ad. for Aus-
tralia, because the Australian author selected an
Australian actor for the comedy-character part—-O.
P. Ileggie, to wit —and the mummer justified his
selection by getting great kudos for a clever perform-
ance. George Shelton, who was in these latitudes
with the J. L. Toole co., also appeared in the
London production ; and Chambers’s old friend, “the
long arm of coincidence,” held a hand in the
business.
Until his “Passers-by” was put on the bills,
Charles Haddon Chambers had been played so little
in Australia of late that the theatre Suffer and the
young male friend who buys chocolates for her,
scarcely know that he is Australian. Chambers’s
father was in the N.S.W. Lands Department, and
the youngster who wrote “The Tyranny of Tears”
got his schooling at Fort-street, in Sydney.
Wellington (M.L.) is the only place in Austral-
asia where the local council refuses to issue a license
to a circus until an insurance policy has been taken
out to cover accident to the public. Wirth Bros.’
big show struck the place the other day, and was
called upon to take out a £lOOO policy, at a cost
of £2O for a week.
The portrait of Melba, which Australian
artist Florence Rodway recently produced,
has been hung in the National Art Gallery
at Sydney, where it will remain as a per-
manent reminder of the idealised songstress.

PENEOLD'S
WINES
At least one person in Melbourne has a
heart-racking reason for asking information
re the fate of the tiny Pandora yawl, which
sailed from Australia 18 months ago, with
Captains Blythe and Arapakis aboard. The
mariners navigated their little craft to
N’York, and left there on July 20 last year.
Nothing, seemingly, has been heard of them
or the Pandora since, though a Sydney paper
is said to have published a rumor that the
craft was “wrecked off Madeira.” Can any-
body throw a ray of hope on the situation?
From S. M. Herald (19/1/T2) :
We used to see advertisements in the papers, “Mar-
ried couple wanted ; no encumbrance.” And it would
seem that married couples without encumbrance are
still preferred. Is Australia, then, to become another
France, even while yet its population is so small ?
Who would think, to read those fine, ringing
words, that the Herald has been banking
the proceeds of “no encumbrance” advts.
almost since it first began to own a cheque-
book? The first thing for a sturdy, reform-
ing paper to do is to raise the price of the
“no encumbrance” advt. to £7OO an inch.
* * * *
One of the annoyances of Ivew (Mel-
bourne) lunatic asylum is the reckless way
in which patients break their ribs. No
less than four have recently been discovered
with broken ribs. One, who died a few days
ago, was found to be suffering from bed-
sores on both hips, abrasions on hands and
legs, and the usual and frequent rib break-
ages. A theory advanced by way of ex-
planation is that the ribs of lunatics are
very brittle. Doubtless this is quite cor-
rect, for it is well known that, if you look
too hard sideways at the middle part of a
lunatic, or blow on him vigorously, he is
liable to suffer damage. In fact, there are
records of a lunatic having been blown in
two. In the old, bad days of England, it
was the custom to suppress the angry,
obstreperous or fizzy variety of madman by
tying him down on a bed, after which a
warder walked up and down his chest on his
knees. This was liable to break his ribs, or
even his brea.st-bone, without leaving any
outward evidence, for even in those days
lunatics were a brittle lot. When the
warders learned this they abandoned the
practice, which had only been adopted with
a mistaken idea of amusing the patients and
preventing time hanging heavily on their
hands. Nowadays, patients mostly break
their own ribs, by hitting themselves on
the stomach with a cruet-stand, or some
such device.
Victoria’s Department of Agriculture has
been indulging in a one-sided argument with
the Bailway Department over a matter of
bees. It appears that the bee expert shipped
several hives of the birds from Tooborac to
Glenorchy, and the railway people wanted
to charge £l2 Bs. for the job. They said
this was at t*he usual rate for lots under
three tons, but the bee man said the insects
weren’t worth that much, and he would
rather forfeit the goods than pay. Not only
that, but he claimed that the hives had
been bumped in transit —which seems pos-
sible—and that some of the contents had
got lost, while others had been forced to
“whip behind,” and had become fatigued
and unfit for use in the dairy, or wherever
bees are used. The railway bosses
perceived that they had no earthly
hope of raising £l2 Bs. on a few
bees, and they couldn’t save them up
for a good market, because labor is already
scarce in the department, and it was no good
putting them in the cold stores, because the
manager advised that there was no market
for frozen bees in England. So the depart-
ment compromised at £9 Bs., and left one on
the counter for ithe expert.

“Belaying Pin’s” hard mozzle:—
I notice by meagre paragraphs in the press that
Justice Higgins has thrown out the case of the
Merchant Service Guild v. inter state shipowners, on
the ground that there is already in existence an
award which he could not vary. That may be so ;
but to me, one of those who suffer from its wording,
there appears something wrong when a bad award s
patent anomalies cannot be righted. As things are, a
shipowner can allot an officer a “home port” whicli
his ship seldom or never visits, and which, if it visits
at all, it leaves after a few hours’ stay. Take my
ship as an instance. On signing articles a year ago,
I was allotted (we’ll say) Port Pirie. I protested;
the ship never went there—-or, at most, did so only
once or twice a year. The other officers were given
other ports. One of them got Melbourne; and dur-
ing the past 12 months that officer has had 84 clear
days away from the ship and all responsibilities,
whilst I, whose wife and family reside in Melbourne,
have never had one day, apart from my annual
leave. Further, if the vessel did go to my “home port,”
and arrived (we’ll say) on Sunday or Saturday, I would
have to spend my eight hours on board. It is good
to see Jack and the firemen going ashore at five
o’clock on the day of arrival, and feel that a fool
award will not allow me to go until the completion
of eight hours after arrival. An officer could take
his midnight to 4 a.m. and noon to 4 p.m. watches
at sea, and, arriving at five, still be liable for duty
until midnight without payment of overtime. I feel
sure Justice Higgins never intended that a man
should spend Sundays and holidays on board in the
port where his home was, or that one man should
have 84 days off in one year and another not one, or
that any man could be liable for duty for more than
eight hours any day except under exceptional circum-
stances.

It is “generally understood in church
circles,” according to the daily press, that
another parson is to be imported from Eng-
land for an important billet in the diocese
of Sydney. There are just two more things
left for the Anglican Church to import—
the congregation and the collection. It is
up to all good Australians to make a note
of this fact.
THE SONG OF SOLOMON.
The blather of faith healers, Theosopliists
and numerous other misty sectarians of the
liighah culchaw class amounts to luminous
logic compared with the cackle of Latter-
Day Saints. Notoriety hath no charms for
the Saints, neverthe-
less their long-haired
canvassers are found
scattering printed
matter in the rural
highways and byways
of the Common-
wealth. The indefi-
niteness of Latter
Day doctrines was
drawn, seemingly,
from a maze of Scrip-
tural quotations, the
concoction being de-
scribed as Bays of
Light, Glad Tidings
of Great Joy, etc.,
etc. It is charged
with orthodox Iver-
ristian spirit, for al-
though brimstone is
not mentioned, brim-
stone can be smelt
between the lines of
the- pamphlets. Un-
less ye believe ye
perish. Salvation is
obtainable only
through the ministra-
tions of them who
cheat the barber.
This news item of
great joy, “start-
ling, though it may
appear, is the eter-
nal truth which will
stand the test of
both reason and rev-
elation,” A man
never knows for cer-
tain what will happen
to his soul tintil he
has swallowed the tid-
ings that his melan-
choly, unbarbered
brother Melchisedec
drops over the back fence. Then he knoweth
that, not having been born to recognise the
early priesthood of Aaron in the knowledge
of the Holy Ghost and the unity of the faith,
and being, moreover, the victim of a baptism
which is void in the sight of Heaven, and-
therefore, worse than useless, he was born
to be damned. The cry of the uncut hairy-
man is the oddest message of damnation
ever forwarded to this office.

“Adelaide”: Someone ought to speak to
Conan Doyle. In his latest Sherlock Holmes
story he has a criminal identified by a torn
ear. It is “Holy Peters, one of the most
unscrupulous rascals that Australia has ever
evolved —and for a young country it has
turned out some very finished types.” And
the ear? It was “badly bitten in a saloon
fight at Adelaide in ’89.” One can see this
town as the author imagines it: the fierce
cowboys riding their unbroken wallabies in
from their ranches at Kent Town and Bow-
den, letting off their six-shooters all along
King William-street, foregathering in the
gambling-hells with which that thorough-
fare is lined, and scattering the gold-dust
acquired without trouble on the banks of the
Patawalonga. For recreation, they roll on
sawdusted saloon floors, and gouge and bite.
That is how Conan Doyle no doubt pictures
it. The reality is different. There certainly
were plenty of Holy Peters here in 1889, and
are still; but they bite your ear with a col-
lection plate, and leave a hole in your
pocket.

“Martha”:—
The S. M. Herald published an article, last Fri-
day, headed: —
BAN OF MOTHERHOOD.
SERIOUS PROBLEM.
CHILDREN, OR IMMIGRANTS?
In this yarn it was made to appear that no one who
is afflicted with an infant has a hope of getting em-
ployment in this country. My experience is that,
though the daily papers “no encumbrance’’ advts.—put
in, I presume, from sordid and mercenary motives—
have done a certain amount of damage, things are by
no means so bad as they are represented. For ex-
ample, last week, I drew a registry office in quest of
a “general servant.” There was only one on offer in
the whole large, dusty establishment, and the pro-
prietors warned me that she didn’t profess to be a
cook, had been accustomed to “better things,” and
would require accommodation for her three-year-old
child. I replied that so long as she was not a con-
genital assassin, and did not get drunk more than
three times a week, she would do me. After an im-
pressive wait the employment-seeker strolled in, tow-
ing a frowsy little girl behind her. She said she was
from Manchester, and wanted 255. a week and beer-
money. She then asked if she could have a separate
room for the child. I thought it might be managed.
Was there a garden for the child to play in? Any-
dogs? The child disliked dogs. Any other children?
The child was shy, and got worried if there were
other babies about. I answered searching questions
as to the drains, the water supply, the aspect of the
room the child was to occupy, the name and reputa-
tion pf the milkman, and so on, till my head was in
a whirl. At length her interrogatories finished, and
she plunged deep in thought. She emerged from her
broodings with a start, said civilly, but firmly, that
she feared I wouldn’t suit, and left. I hadn’t banned
her; she had banned me. And, apparently, it was
mostly the child that had caused the rift. For me,
I am prepared to house an entire family so long as
I can get some sort of a servitor thrown in with the
bunch. And dozens of my friends look at things in
the same light.
BONES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WANTED, to SELL Half Set of HUMAN RONES ;
cheap. A.8.C., Argus. — Advt.
Is he an immature M.D.
Who badly needs some cash,
This sacrificing A.B C. *
Who owns the bony trash?
Has he got sick of seeing it
On one leg like a crane?
Or did he keep the other bit
When it was cut in twain ?
Or has the other bit been sold
To one who wields the shin
When clamping down the carpet’s fold,
And driving tin tacks in?
(Some folk like this there are, I know,
And I would angered be,
Though I had died long years ago,
If they drove nails with me.)
Whose are these bones he has for sale?
Who grew them—Smitn or Jones?
When' o’er the hill I take the trail
I mean to take my bones.
But if my ghost evaporates
And leaves the burthen sad,
I shall turn back from Heaven’s gates
To save a similar ad.
And if the cloakroom cherubs cry,
“No bones! We ain’t allowed!”
I shall go tramping down the sky
Far from the haloed crowd.
A bag, a halo and a shroud!
Myself I plainly see,
With some most precious burthen bowed
AValking eternally!
N.S.W.

“Mayne Stay”: I agree with “Belaying
Pin” (B. 21/12/T1) that Justice Higgins’s
new Arbitration award for seamen is a good
one, and, for myself, I believe it goes about
as far as anyone could expect in present
conditions. I agree, too, with “8.P.” that
the union should try its hardest to keep its
members up to the mark, but I can’t endorse
his method of turning the undesirable men
out of the union. It would probably lead to
abuse. The continuous discharge system
would straighten out the kinks quicker and
better than any other method at all likely to
be adopted, although there is bitter opposi-
tion to it from the men who are likely to get
bad discharges. The regrettable thing about
it is that while these men are in the minority
they appear to have the most say in the
union. Personally, I have the idea that an
extra 10s. or fl a month to the man who
stays over six months in one ship—on the re-
commendation of the mate or engineer—
would be a big step forward, and would pay
in the long run. Also, I have visions of the
millennium.

Police Inspector Ellison, of M.L., gave
evidence before the N. S. Wales Totalisator
Commission in Wellington last Thursday,
and, according to the S. M. Herald, the fol-
lowing dialogue took place :
Mr. Ellison: The totalisator, in his opinion, was a
far better system for the public than bookmaking.
The machine carried on the business of the course on
a race day only, not all the year round. Race crowds
were more orderly nowadays than in the old days of
bookmakers.
Mr. Bruntnell: Are you aware we have a very
reputable class of bookmaker in New South Wales?
Mr. Ellison: I am aware you have men who will
pay what they owe, but I don’t believe you have
bookmakers who would not “stiffen” a horse if they
got a chance.
Mr. Bruntnell: I differ with you.
Will any Anti-Liquor champion, Social
Purity expert, saint, Two-up scotcher, or
other Wowser, contend, in face of the above,
that professional holy men, of the Bruntnell
type, are not the warm friends and valued
advocates of the Ring?
The whit e-vested
person who read
Gaud Mayor Allen
Taylor’s proud New
Year remarks about
the continued swelling
of Sydney during
1911 glowed with
pride—until he found
the usual item about
some workers’ de-
mand for another in-
crease in wages. Then his joy
passed away like thistledown be-
fore a racing southerly buster, and
all was dark as the bowels of a
cellar. A heap of the fat citizen’s
unhappiness is generated by the
big city he cherishes so violently.
He loves to see it put forth sky-
scrapers ; and every time the Gaud
Mayor tears up a place where
workers used to live, and plants
the stone seed of a tall importer’s
warehouse, he cheers and cham-
pagnes. Then the unhoused work-
ers ask for another penny an hour,
because the new landlord wants
more money, and the tram-guard
they didn’t have to pay when they
lived close in holds out his official
hand for his official coin. The stout
person reads about this in his
morning paper, as he travels to
his desk, and goes temporarily
mad. A bloated city is a sort of
commercial centipede, which keeps
on stinging itself with its own
tail. London, the world’s prize
example of bloatedness, has almost
ceased to build battleships, largely
on account of its high cost of liv-
ing and heavy local taxation, and
the battleship business has moved
to less inflated centres.
Auckland (M.L.) is beginning to have
some doubts about the “child-like wonder”
(as one of the Sydney dailies described it)
of the Japs who recently crowded ashore
there from the Aso and Soya, war cruisers
belonging to the child-like person recently in
Port Jackson. Note-books and maps seem
to have been much in the Aucklander’s eye,
although he appears to have been too polite
to mention it until his bright and merry
friends had departed. One newspaper re-
lates that a Jap lieutenant, accompanied by
a party of cadets, appeared at a house oc-
cupying a commanding position on the cliffs
and asked permission to use the garden, os-
tensibly to adjust compasses. The lady was
charmed to oblige, but, when the boss blew
in from work, and found his lawn turned
into a sort of naval base, he didn’t contri-
bute to the general friendliness. In fact,
he was as disconcerting as the Face at the
Window. Another joke—for the Japs—was
provided by the chairman of the Harbor
Board inviting a party of officers to have a
look round the harbor works. Still, that’s
not as good a one as the permission given
recently to the Nipponese explorers or some-
thing to camn for a month or so alongside
the forts at Sydney before they left for the
South Pole or somewhere.

* # * *
The U.S. is the land of religious fads. The
17th of February, 1915, will be tbe hundredth
anniversary of the day when a British army
and a Yankee army last gunned at each
other in anger. Accordingly, Senator Elihu
Root, of the U.S. —a name curiously sugges-
tive of the famous but mythical Esau Slodge
of the same country—proposes thsit at a
fixed hour, minute and second, on that day,
the English-speaking folk all over the world
shall drop everything and spend five minutes
in silent prayer. When the silent prayer is
over, they will pick up everything and re-
sume business. And the man who leaves off
prayer first will pick up most, while the one
who prays longest will wonder where the
devil his goods have gone to.
A “small but enthusiastic” meeting has
been held in Adelaide, an Anti-Compulsory
Military Service League has been formed,
and “most of those present joined a com-
mittee to carry out the objects of the
League.” The objects are, it seems, “to
stand shoulder to shoulder in resisting the
compulsory clauses of the Defence Act.” So
they see that it is necessary to stand shoul-
der to shoulder to resist a piece of home-
grown legislation; also they see that it is
necessary to organise the resistance. But
when it is a matter of arguing with a for-
eign invader, apparently any old sort of un-
organised resistance is good enough. These
musty anti-Peace cranks are an illogical lot.
A Melbourne man writes: —
The recent burning of The Grange boardinghouse,
which stands in extensive grounds at the corner oi
Domain-road and St. Kilda-road, has recalled to Mel-
bourne notice the fact that the freehold title of the
propputy has always been under suspicion. A few
years back Premier Bent tajked of buying The
Grange estate for a State Governor’s residence, but
the project died on a rumor of a flaw in the title.
Seemingly, the State Government of long ago, when
it railed off the corner allotment and built a house
on it for the military commandant of the period,
was guilty of an illegal grab. The land is palpably
a portion of the original South Yarra Domain and
Botanical Gardens, dedicated to public use for all
time. The Government cribbed a bit for the com-
mandant’s residence, and when he left the country
or died, the same Government, or a successor, raised
a good few thousands by selling the property on cer-
tain “conditional” terms, which constitute the mys-
terious flaw in the title. Victorian Governments of
long ago were terribly hungry for land sale “re-
venue,” and this transaction was rather worse than
most others. So The Grange question had better be
settled by the Weary Government resuming posses-
sion of the land at its last cost price, and restoring
it to the Domain it was stolen from.
Melbourne’s latest tragedy was a study in
order. A young man called at a Jolimont
villa, and presently came out with his “girl.”
The r>air had gone only a few yards when a
man stepped from a shadow and accosted the
youth. There was a slight scuffle, then two
shots, and after that a dead man. Without
any fuss somebody telephoned for a doctor,
and somebody else called a constable. These
presently arriving, the victim was taken to
the morgue, and the youth and the woman
went in the doctor’s motor to the lock-up,
where the former was arrested on a charge
of murder. He calnily admitted the shoot-
ing, on the ground of self-defence, and the
woman just as calmly backed up his story.
Everything was tidy, orderly, matter-of-fact
—orderly and quiet as the dead man, who,
being shot through the heart, dropped where
he stood, without making a mess or creating
an uproar. The dead man was Frederick
Tullidge, who, in August last, had divorced
his wife, and it was the co-re. whom he was
about to rebuke when a bullet ended the
story. It is an unhappy tale, and the real
tregedy of it concerns the victim’s two smell
children, given to the deceased’s custody
when their mother was divorced.
“10-2-4” moralises: —
In Europe, when a country gets right down on its
financial uppers, when the last asset has gone up the
spout, and the bank account has been garnisheed,
and the bailiff lurks, ca. sa. order in hand, on the
doormat, it has still one last feather to fly with. It
may, as Portugal did last week, legalise the public
playing Of games of chance. When Homburg and
suchlike fashionable gaming resorts started to go
under before the combined assaults of German
Catholicism and German Wowserism, 50 years ago,
the Prince of Monaco saw his chance of evading
bankruptcy, and swiftly took it. Ever since he
made his famous contract with old Blanc, the Prin-
cipality has been a model of luxury and cheapness
from the point of view of the natives. Their roads,
bridges, water and sewerage supply, baths, opera
house and every other national and municipal ser-
vice have been paid for during all that time out of
the casino profits, and are the last words in perfec-
tion and efficiency. And so it will soon be, no
doubt, with Portugal’s large towns. Monte Carlo
has lost its vogue. It has become the Manly Beach
of Europe—a cheery but sordid little place where the
fastidious only go in search of health or of such
amusements as they may wish to enjoy under the
rose. It is the haunt of the raffish London man-
about-town, the Parisian “rasta,” the small, seedy
punter, the wilting lady of pleasure, and the German
Baron and French Count who hardly make a serious
pretence at being other than bogus.
Cintra, one of the places mentioned by Reuter, as
a future gaming resort, is even more beautiful,
naturally, than Monte Carlo. When it has had part
of the vast profits of a casino or two lavished on its
parks and public buildings it should be a paradise.
It is within a week’s comfortable ship and train
journey of London. The climate is perfect, unlike
that of Monaco, which is death on those who can’t
stand abrupt changes. It is practically run, in a
social manner of speaking, by an English colony
whose members are accustomed to say that it would
be one of the pleasantest places in the world only for
the Portuguese. The result is that all the sports
which Monaco discourages for the benefit of the
casino are in full swing ; and it is by so much the
more attractive to foreigners.
Certainly Portugal stands to make a fortune by
the new departure. On the other hand, it will lose
such faint remnants of moral and social prestige as
remain to it. The modern nation which legalises
baccarat and rouge-et-noir resembles the lady who
goes on the town or the gentleman who takes per-
manently to bar-loafing. Even Belgium shut down
firmly on the Ostend casino, the other day, though
the edict meant temporary ruin to the town. It is a
somewhat depressing thought that one of the first
uses the Republican Government of Portugal is
making of its rise to power is to turn the old semi-
nigger country into one of the few remaining two-up
schools of Europe.

January 14 was the jubilee-day of historic
“Black Monday,” when the thermometer in
Melbourne touched 109°, and the city—closed
in by bush in those times —was singed on
all sides. Nature celebrated the anniver-
sary by putting up the season’s first
scorcher, with smoke shrouding the ranges
to the north and east, and bushfires sweep-
ing many patches of the State, and partly
wiping out the townships of Willaura and
Wontha"gi.

RATS!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Age, starting an anti-rat crusade, describes
Melbourne as “a rat-infested city,” and calls upon
the council to make stringent law, compelling in-
dividual citizens to destroy their own rats.
This Melbourne is a stricken place,
And plagued with vermin gross and grim,
By reason of its lack of grace
And need of municipal vim.
At night come forth the warty rats,
All grey and grim and big as cats,
In bakers’ bins and brewers’ vats,
On bread and booze to sow disease.
The tradesmen must look after these.
But there are other rats that haunt
Our hapless town in light of day,
And they are old and foul and gaunt—
Sad is the havoc that they play.
Each stout old gentleman has got
One hid beneath his high stove-pot;
And hungriest of the awful lot
Is Anti-Sosh, a rat that weds
And builds and breeds in Fattest heads.
There are the State-Frights rats that swill
Their victim’s brains, and blind his eyes.
’Twill take a hefty swipe to kill
The rat of Private Enterprise.
The Argus has a fearsome one
Of capital upon the run.
Oh, Johnnie, Johnnie, get your gun
And shoot yon dilly rat that yaps
Of ruin wrought by “Labah” chaps!
The bigot rats from every clime,
The Orange rodent, and the ro-
Dent that makes of Defence a crime,
They’re dangerous, and all must go.
Fine, fine, indeed, when Reason reigns,
To see Fat citizens with canes
Engaged in cudgelling their brains,
Or, armed with clubs and cricket bats,
Devoutly beating out their rats.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

“Pick”: “Jaloom”
(B. 28/12/’ll) says
he can walk at the tail of a
hurried or express snake.
Well, I once saw a carpet snake, considered the laziest
of the land-eels or animated ropes, catch a wildly-
fleeing kangaroo-rat after a 40yds. chase, and if
“Jaloom” can walk alongside a fully-extended ’roo-
rat he should come out and have a word with Don-
aldson. On level ground the snake’s progression is,
as a rule, leisurely enough; but when he makes a
break downhill, lightning has to shovel coal in the
furnace like mad to catch him. He seems to strike
the floor just twice in half a mile. I’m not talking
about Boxing Day snakes, either, nor about dragons,
flying devils, vampires, ghost-fowl, or anything of
that sort.
“Exile” (from U.S.A.) : Is coo-ee a
native Australian word ? It is, 1 find, commonly
used in the south of the United States as a cow-call,
and the folk there say that it originated in New Eng-
land. The darkies towards evening may be heard
calling, “Coo-ee, coo-ee!” and when the milk enve-
lope hears it she starts for her domicile immediately.
“J.G.”: All I can put forward in reply to
the scepticism of “Bilbax” (B. 7/12/’ll) regarding
the belief generally held by miners that dynamite
strikes downward, is my own experience of explo-
sives. Wherever I have seen dynamite at work it
has torn things about below its bed, whilst blasting
powder, on the other hand, unless put in a deeply-
drilled hole in a rock or a log, or something equally
solid, and tamped in and rammed solid, does no
worse than puff out in a cloud of smoke. Where the
powder teas tamped in properly, the result of the ex-
plosion was the shattering above and round about of
the object attacked. It was very seldom that the
effects could be seen further down than the bottom
of the drilled hole. Dynamite requires no tamping.
To tear out a tree stump, for instance, no more is
necessary than to bore a hole, insert the plug and
pour in a pint or so of water to keep the air out.
I have seen the earth ripped open for several feet
below the depth of the plug in such cases, while, if
powder had been used the result would merely have
been a miniature geyser. But if “Bilbax” is really
anxious to learn the comparative effects of explod-
ing dynamite and powder, all he has to do is to fire
a charge of each from a gun. If I don’t hear from
him again I’ll know he’s done it.
* * * *
“Kangaroo” writes from the island of that
name: “Kangarooster” (B. 30/11/’U) does well to
advocate Nepean Bay, Kangaroo Island, as one of the
naval bases for the Australian Navy. With the build-
ing of the Perth-Adelaide and Port Darwin-Adelaide
railways, Adelaide will become, perhaps, the chief
defence railway centre in the Commonwealth, and
will need to be protected accordingly. A fleet, con-
sisting of torpedo boats, destroyers and submarines,
stationed at Nepean Bay, would be able to command
both entrances to St. Vincent’s Gulf. They (the en-
trances) are within two hours’ steaming of each
other, and the waters are ideally suited for submarine
work. Additional protection could be given by erect-
ing big guns on Capes Willoughby, Jervis, Point
Marsden and Troubridge. With a railway across the
island, Vivonne Bay, on the south coast, could be
made a coaling depot for the cruisers, etc., patrolling
the Southern Ocean in time of war, and it would at
the same time control the only other landing on the
island by which an enemy would be able to reach
the base. With St. Vincent’s Gulf thus protected,
mail and inter-State boats, harassed by an enemy,
could make for either Backstairs Passage or Investi-
gator Straits, where they would find protection. It
would make no difference how many boats sought
harborage to escape the reach of the enemy’s guns;
the gulf would have room for them all. As a harbor,
Nepean Bay is protected by a sandspit, about six
miles long, inside of which submarines, etc., would
find smooth water in all weathers, with a depth of
from two and three-quarter fathoms to four fathoms,
while Eastern Cove is capable of taking the largest
ships afloat. Finally, consider what would happen if an
enemy got possession of Kangaroo Island. From it
the invaders would be able to go out and waylay all
ships bound from the eastern States to the West,
England, Suez, and South Africa, and bring them
back to Nepean Bay as prizes, or sink them. Have
I not grounds for my contention that this island
should, at least, be visited by the authorities before
the base in South Australia is finally decided on?

“Tiaki” : I wrote a par in The Bulletin
recently about the affection of the octopus for human
people, and somebody rushed me with a demand for
authentic instances. Here’s a couple. During Christ-
mas week, a girl, 10 years of age, while paddling her
feet in the sea at Island Bay, near Wellington (M.L.),
had her leg pulled to such purpose by an octopus
that she shrieked like Freedom on an historical oc-
casion. Her father, who, fortunately, was close at
hand, rushed up just in time to prevent her being
hauled from the rock upon which she was seated, but
he was unable to dislodge the tentacles of the marine
devil, and, in his turn, had to shake up. the welkin
considerably. His outcries brought three ladies from
the beach, and by the combined struggles of four
people the child was freed. The other instance was
also in Wellington, and concerned a fisherman who
went into the water waist-deep to free his line. He
was immediately gripped by Beelzebub’s marine
agent, and anchored down. The tide and the fisher-
man’s hair were rapidly rising when a picnic party
butted into the proceedings with a boat-hook. The
octopus thereupon agreed, though reluctantly, that it
was no place for him ; that the time was not ripe,
and that the Constitution was in danger. These are
two cases to go on with.

“Matelot”: Writing from Cape Leeuwin
to The Bulletin (28/12/’ll), “Mallarapoonie” gives
a picture of the western outlying cape of the Aus-
tralian continent, that Dampier, the “prince of nauti-
cal description,” could not have excelled in its
vigorous terseness and simplicity of language, albeit
“Mallarapoonie” brings in the wreck of the Pericles,
in which some people lost their pyjamas and hat-
pins. One “survivor” of that catastrophe is now,
and has been for the past two years, engaged in
writing an account of the terrible adventures of his
wife and himself, and I am not interfering with the
copyright when I say that it is no less than the
dire truth that he “lost all his luggage, which was
uninsured and worth £4O, whilst some of the sailors
lost nothing”! “Mallarapoonie’s” vivid portrait of
the Leeuwin and its vicinity reminds me of another
headland with the same characteristics. This is the
•north point of the island of Eua, one of the Tongan
group. The cliff, one solid mass of grey, upheaved
coral rock, rises sheer to a height of 600 ft. or more,
and its base is swept by a furious current. The
summit is covered with a low, dense thicket scrub,
the breeding-place of the frigate and other sea-birds,
and the whole aspect is forbidding and depressing.
I once walked to the verge of the cliff, but no native
would accompany me. “It would be bad luck,” they
said, “to look over.” They told me a yarn that,
when Parker Brothers, of Sydney, had a sheep station
on Eua, in the early ’7o’s, 500 of the wool producers,
led by a ram, made their way to the edge of the
cliff, and placidly stepped over. I asked the Rev.
Shirley Baker, the Poo-Bah of Tonga, if it was true,
but he said, in effect, that it was a dashed fabrica-
tion. I let it go at that.

“Snell”: Suicidal tendencies were very
marked in Andrew Martin. For over 10 years he had
betrayed the symptoms. It took very little to
drive him to suicide; a few drinks, a cross word, a
bit of bad luck, a change in the weather, a sore toe,
or a casual disappointment—any of these was suffi-
cient. I never knew a man so self-destructive. The
fact that he contrived to live through it all must be
ascribed to his exceeding toughness. At the Two
Mates mine, Eagle, there was a large living hut for
the men. It had a range of bunks on one side like
store shelves. Here three shifts pigged in fraternal
discomfort. One Sunday night, after two dozen of
bottled beer had been broken open, and a few pounds
had been lost and won at euchre, Andrew Martin
suddenly arose from the table, with a fixed purpose
and a look of terrible resolution. “Here, I’m full o’
this” ; he said. “I’m sick of livin’—sick of every-
thin’. So long all!” “Gorn’ t’ kill yerself ag’in,
Andy ?” said Coleman, looking up froip his cards.
“I am,” Andrew replied with deathly calm. “This
time nothin’ stops me.” He fished a clothes line
from under Strack’s bunk. At the door he stood,
white but resolute. “Good-bye, boys. Godblesher!”
he said. He went out into the darkness, and the
absorption of bottled beer and the card argument
went on far into the night, and over into next day.
Towards two in the morning the door was violently
opened, and Andrew Martin appeared. It had been
raining, and he was wet through. The rope hung
about his neck in a noose, his face was white, his
eye impressive. “Gordammit!” he snorted, “Am
I t’ be left hangin’ there all night?”
“Dynamo”: Swarms of black beetles are
invading parts of Gippsland (Vic.) just now, and
where I am (Korumburra) they literally storm any
well-lit locality. Emptied a bucket-load out of the
power-house the other night, and was struck by the
fact that the beasts were covered with small, white
lice, something akin to the parasites that battle for
a crust upon the surface of the domestic fowl. From
observation, I find that these beetles do not live 5 more
than a couple of hours, and my curiosity would get
back to its normal heat if anybody could explain
whether the sudden death is caused by closer settle-
ment.
“J. 5.8.”: The information offered by
“E.W.O’D.” (B. 28/12/T1), about posts and post-
holes is as vague as a “Liberal” politician’s
description of the Socialistic tiger. The nature of
the country doesn’t matter so much as the quantity
of timber the fencing expert is game to chop off
the bottom of the post. If a 6ft. post is put into a
2ft. hole, 4ft. of post remains above the surface ; but
if 6in. of post is cut off with the axe the fencer
always keeps handy, an 18in. hole produces the
same result. The art of rapid fencing lies in a man’s
ability to plant the chips and discarded lumps of
post where the boss won’t find them.

“Wang”: “9-3-11” (B. 16/11/T1) is quite
right about the improvidence of the average cocky
in the good seasons. All the insistence that can be
brought to bear upon the casual cuss as to the neces-
sity of making provision for a droughty day, and all
the points and hints given on the preparing of ensi-
lage and the making of silos, appear (o be of com-
paratively no avail. The cocky agrees that the idea
is first-class, but says it is too expensive: for a poor
man. Perhaps the up-to-date, brick-built silo is too
expensive for the ordinary settler, but, if he wasn’t
such a shiftless, bat-eyed bloke, he’d know the value
of a hole in the ground for storing tucker for the
stock. It might not turn out the same quality of
tucker as that dished up from the elaborate artificial
contrivance, but it is not bad tucker, and, when
there is no other kind of tucker on the premises,
not bad tucker becomes dashed fine tucker. The
silos of my farming days were just holes in the
ground, a disused quarry-hole or a dried
water-hole; sometimes, even, a cocky was ener-
getic and far-sighted enough to dig a
hole. These were filled up with surplus grass, and
other fodder, and covered over with tree branches
and a layer of straw. On top of all a few tons of
earth was piled, to keep out the air. Some of the
finest ensilage I’ve seen was made from the huge,
variegated thistle, that prickly monster - that some-
times shoves itself 10ft. or more into the air, and
requires a sharp axe or billhook to hew it down. I
don’t know what the ensilage of a modern silo is
like. The stuff that was hooked out of the old-time
silos was rotten-looking tack, like huge masses of
wet tobacco. But the smell of it was sweet, and 1
never heard any complaints from the cow about its
taste, while, judging by the appearance of the ani-
mals, the nutritive quality was able to get a move
on in the right direction.
MONEY IN HIS BOOTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Not long since, a countryman who had placed bank-
notes in his boots for safety, and worn them out
in tramping the city streets, received £3 10s. in
gold from the Treasury for the tattered fragments
which, with diligence, he was able to extract. —News
item.
When with his axe Bill’s earned enough,
Straight down to town he scoots,
Pausing for half a jiff to stuff
Some bank notes in his boots.
The honest trees he knows rush past
The windows of his train,
Until he rattles in at last
To Sydney once again.
Fierce engines hiss like snakes at him,
And tram-cars snarl and flash,
As from the station, grimed and grim,
He does a country dash.
Small newsboys howl of blanky deeds;
The tangled traffic roars ;
Demented, here and there he speeds
And strangers’ aid implores.
At last a cabman sights his prey,
And drives him off with pride
Where for his bed a bob he’ll pay.
(The menu hangs outside).
Somewhere adjacent to the sky
They’ll stow suspicious Bill;
And cits will see as they pass by
His socks upon the sill.
For, when he’s sick of asphalt ways,
He seeks his high retreat,
Washes his socks, and through the haze
Looks down upon the street.
Barefooted, collarless, unbraced,
His old pipe drawing well,
He mumbles, without spite or haste,
“Now they can go ter H —1!”
His slow thoughts now on spielers run,
And uncles in Fiji,
And ev’ry sort of “crook” and “gun”
Who lives by trickery.
All day he braved the land-shark’s teetn,
And walked about alone,
His spare notes merely hid beneath
Twelve stone of meat and bone.
All day he guarded well his wealth,
And watched that no galoots
Kept him engaged, while one by stealth
Unlaced and robbed his boots.
But, safe on high, at sunset he
Indraws a thankful breath:
“Beat ’em again ! They said I’d be
Took down, as sure as death! ”
N.S.W.

“Matelot” : Re the giant deep-sea octo-
pus, the especial bonne bouche of the sperm whale
in the Pacific, and the resultant ambergris. In
August, last year, a native of Futuna (Horne Islands,
300 miles west from Samoa) picked up, whilst fish-
ing off Singavi Harbor, a lump of ambergris weigh-
ing 7ilb. Knowing what it was and something of
its value, he kept his discovery to himself, trusting
neither the local trader at Singavi nor the mission-
aries on the island. He made his way to Noumea in
the French cruiser liersaint, and sold the goods to
the skipper of a labor schooner for 1000 francs. The
skipper must have done well out of the deal, for am-
bergris, even in its crude state, impregnated thickly
with the steel-hard fragments of the bills of the giant
octopii, brings £5 per ounce in the London and
Continental markets. It is used as the basis for the
manufacture of high-class perfumes, having the ex-
traordinary power of developing the smells tenfold.
The writer has bought, handled and sold many lumps
of ambergris obtained from natives and traders in
the North and South Pacific, and found that in ap-
pearance and color it varied tremendously ; but such
great whaling authorities as Surgeons Bennett and

Beale agree that it is all the Jesuit of constipation
in the sperm whale—cetacean appendicitis, in fact.
In all the specimens that have been found, bits of
the black, parrot-like bill of the deep-sea octopus,
ranging up to 4in. or sin. in length, 3in. in width,
and lin. to 2in. thick, make the stuff resemble a
well-raisined plum duff. The big teeth of the mighty
cachalot, with his tremendous jaw-power, which
crushes a whaleboat like an egg-shell, makes short
work of this hideous and loathsome monster of the
ocean’s depths. But, like all high-livers, the sperm
whale pays the penalty. The ventral passage be-
comes blocked, and sometimes, unable to avoid the
obstruction, the whale runs ashore and dies. This
has been well proven. Ambergris, if a lighted match
is applied to it, burns freely, giving out a pale
yellow flame with a vanilla-like odor.

“T.V.T.”; The disc-plough has been a
potent factor in breaking up the scrub-lands of Eastern
Gippsland. With its advent, the “slasher” supplanted
the mattock, and vast areas of land, covered with ti-
tree and kindred scrub, that would otherwise require
years of labor to grub and prepare for the mouldboard
plough, have simply been slashed and burned and
then ripped up with the disc. Bullocks, or a trac-
tion engine, furnish the best motive power for
the disc-plough. Horses are too easily “staked” by
the little sharp stumps of ti-tree left by the slasher,
but a team of bulls will plod along all day without
injury in ground where the spikes are as freely dis-
tributed as stoves in Sheol.

“Boronia”: I don’t want to be hard on
anybody, but the Abos. who declare that jarrah piles
can defy Teredo Navalis, Esq., don’t know
the gentleman. For years Westralian
Governments have been replacing jarrah
piles, which have emerged second best
from an argument with him, as Premier
Scaddan admitted not long ago in the
Assembly. Now it is reported that scores
of jarrah piles in the Fremantle wharves
have been utterly destroyed by the marine
excavator. Similarly, Premier Ward, of
Maoriland, recently stated that piles of
the same religion, at the Wellington
wharves, had been eaten by the same
assailant, in from eight to 15 years. Of
course, these responsible officials may be
wrong, and the irresponsible Abos. cor-
rect; still, if there is any betting to be
done, I have a dark sort of a kind of
suspicion where my tuppence will be dis-
covered.
When Trivett counted up the Ma State’s
bores, the other day, he found 487 left, after elimi-
nating all that walked on legs—2ll of them public
bores and 276 private. They run in all shapes. On
Killara the officers struck a flow at 46ft.; at Boronga,
near Moree, the Government had to punch down
4341 ft. before it could get a wash. When the
Boronga flow was tapped it was worth having,
though; the gauge shows over a million gallons a
day. Around Walgett and Moree you can take the
biggest drink, without fear of emptying the barrel.
There are six taps on the stock routes around Moree
that spurt out over a million gallons a day apiece,
and four around Walgett do the same. In a general
way you never know whether a bore is going to boil
you or give you the cold shivers. When you hold
your billy oyer the Clifton spout, on the road be-
tween Milparinka and Wanaaring, you get your liquor
at 139 degrees, while at Warren it comes up at only
70 degrees. The drill hasn’t struck an absolute
blank in many places; mostly when it has failed
it has been because, for some mysterious reason, it
has got into salt water. Still, the percentage of fail-
ures is small: of the 211 bores which the State has
put down or suppressed, only 22 have had to be
written off as a bad job entirely, and they are mostly
out towards the droughty Barrier Range, where,
there being so little moisture on the surface, it was
well worth taking a risk to find out if there was a
trifle more in the innards,

“Dirk Swincott” : At one time or another
I’ve worked alongside almost every variety of the white
race, and have toiled as well with Chows and other dis-
colored institutions; but this last harvest I had the
queerest mate of all sprung upon me. The gentle-
man with the scrambled whiskers to whom I owed
allegiance sent over his daughter to do turn and
turn about with me at the winnower. I suppose
this kind of thing is usual in the district where
cow is queen; but, in the wheat country, I took it
as a low-down trick. My native chivalry prevented
me from letting my fair partner do any of the heavier
work. At least, it did for the first day; but when
I found the lady was my equal in strength, whether
of brawn or of language, I let her do her whack, and
was even ungallant enough at times to let her do a
whack and a quarter.

“F.C.”: I don’t want to reopen the dis-
cussion about shade trees which I started a while
back, but I badly want to insult the evil-disposed
individual who advised me to try the currajong. In
the natural simplicity of my heart I didn’t dream he
was pulling my leg; but I know better now. I put
in a row of trees—some sugar-gums, two scarlet
flowering gums, golden wattles, Cootamundra dittos,
and two currajongs, all being about 6in. high when
I got them, last June. The sugar gums are now be-
tween 18in. and 2ft. above the ground, and the
scarlet gums and wattles more than half as high;
but as for the. currajongs, one turned up its little
roots almost immediately; while the other, though
still alive, only puts forth a new shoot tentatively.
Its present height is what a tradesman would call
6in. bare. It is undoubtedly a fine shade tree —for
an ant or a small worm. But I wasn’t studying these
beasts; it was the house I wanted to shade. Even
John Weary couldn’t rest easily under a currajong
that had been planted less than 20 years.

P-F. : Does anybody know anything
about aboriginal “albinos” ? Queensland papers of
50 years ago mention a tribe of these freaks —
“men with conical-shaped heads, wholly devoid of
hair except a small tuft at the lowest point of the
the cerebellum, just over the back of the neck.”
Kanaka albinos are not uncommon, but the myalls
abovementioned apparently formed a distinct clan,
and were not merely individuals with eccentric
characteristics.

“Exile” writes from St. Louis, U.S.A. -
it o A .T rican lawyer” (B. 3/8/’ll) says “the
United States is, above all, the place of homes,” and
extols the work done in the beautification of the
cities.
1 have been three years in the States, and declare
that the lawyer tells but half the truth. Wealth
has its homes, of course—as it has everything else,
the middle-class also has its homes. But the home
ot the worker is not on a level with the home of the
Australian worker—not by a long way.
Start from the bottom. The “nigger” doesn’t
count, of course. There are only 10 millions of
him, and he lives ’most any old way, as niggers
should. The “Dago” is not reckoned either. There
are millions of him doing all sorts of hard and
heavy work. His pay of from one dollar 25 cents
to one dollar 75 cents per day does not enable him
to have a home, so he has a kennel instead. Perhaps
he prefers the kennel. He’s just a “Dago,” anyway.
Then the man who gets from 17 dollars to 20 dollars'
weekly—what of his home? Externally, it is not
too bad, for it is built for show. No privacy, as we
know it in Australia—just row after row of flats,
mostly three rooms to each set of apartments ; back
yards in common; front scrap of lawn, ditto. No
room for children—and none wanted, as a rule.
Hobbies for the worker out of the question. How’s
that for a home?
Then there are the “roomers.” These are not
single men and women, but married couples who
rent a single room. They infest the city in thou-
sands. They have no home—eat and sleep in the
one room; and the child is absent, which perhaps
is best for the child.
The American knows how to lavish money on the
“boulevards,” where the wealthy live; but just
round the corner, near the homes of the worker, the
streets are very often unpaved and undrained to an
extent not known in any Australian city. A com-
mon sight is a big red notice stuck on the front door
of these dwellings: “Scarlet fever —keep out”;
“Diphtheria—keen out,” etc. These tell the tale,
and the death-rate keeps well up. The United States
alone of all the civilised peoples publishes no vital
statistics. Just as well. They wouldn’t look good.
I don’t know New York. Perhaps it is all the
lawyer says. But St. Louis, the fourth city of the
States, I do know. It isn’t beautiful, and it isn’t
being beautified, unless one refers only to the
lovely boulevards lined with the homes of the fortu-
nate wealthy. Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, Balla-
rat, and many others—-I know them all. American
cities are not beautiful as they are beautiful; and
never will be while graft is as general as it is now.
The American pays far more in city taxes than
the Australian does, and gets far less for his money.
Says the lawyer: “Not all of this is finished, but
all of it is planned.” The American is the “boss”
planner of this earth. I’ve lost count of the number
of “plans” that have been put out for the beautifica-
tion of St. Louis since I have lived in it. Not one
of them has got past the “planning,” and never will.
The people don’t expect that they should. They feel
good when they read big head-lines in the daily
paper: “St. Louis to have a River Drive.” There
won’t be a drive, and we all know there won’t
be one. But it gives the American a chance to let
off hot air, and then he is happy.
♦
N.S.W. Land Appeal Court, a tribunal ap-
pointed to untangle some of the webs into
which the Land Laws drop the cocky, is
now composed of three ex-Under Secretaries
of the Lands Department. Originally, the
Court consisted of one high legal expert
(Judge Rogers), one red-tape expert,
and one man skilled in the things of the
bush. The legal expert and the man from
the land have dropped out, and two more
Under Secretaries have been promoted to
the Court to join their brother Under Secre-
tary. Lessees and intending lessees grow
increasingly uneasy at this, as many of the
cases are appeals from the decisions of the
Lands Departments; and they are not at all
comforted when they remember that lately
the retiring Commissioner Brandis —the
real land expert—was very frequently in a
minority when appeals were being decided,
the majority being the two ex-civil servants.
If the idea now is to merely register the de-
partmental view, then it seems a waste of
money to have a Court at all.
“Boh”: A girl who is in the habit of whip-
ping up charity entertainments confided her
woes to me the other day. Here is her wail :“Yes,
first of all I rush round and collect a crowd
of likely-looking children. I rehearse them
till I have a voice like an oyster-vendor or
‘rabbit-o,’ and the people next door
grumble and threaten to move. Then, when
the time comes for getting the costumes, the
performers start to revolushe. They can’t
make them, and they can’t get them, and
‘they aren’t going in’—as if it was a cold
bath ! Every boy-Jack and girl-Jinny
wants to be a principal, and I am fre-
quently informed “Mother says I am to be
a contralto !” The boys get jealous of one
another and refuse to sing; so I find myself
with a half-dumb company, and get des-
perate. The family, which has been watch-
ing me all along with silent contempt, seizes
the chance to tell me that it serves me
right. By threats and pleadings I get my
team going again, and the night of the show
crawls down the calendar towards us like a
death adder. When the fatal moment has
arrived, and the hall caretaker has a good
grip on the curtain ropes, the kiddies look
so nice and docile that I feel a brute for
having been stuffy with them. A wild
skirmish the last second before curtain-rise
makes me see red again, and I start them
on the opening chorus with set teeth. As
the curtain goes up, I shake my fist from
the wings and observe bitterly: ‘After this
show we part for ever!’ Rather to my sur-
prise, the parents and friends in front get
almost hysterical with enthusiasm at seeing
the family on the stage, and the affair is a
great success. Being called before the cur-
tain, I suddenly remember that I have
given my last spare hairpin to one girl and
my shoes to another. They are hurriedly
dragged off, and I hastily resume my pro-
perty prior to taking the call. At going-
home time I am embraced by all the kiddies
in turn, and they leave the precincts of the
hall plaintively inquiring, ‘How about the
next practice?’ This, fellow taxpayers, is
how we partly provide for the sick and
afflicted.”
