THE CAT: A THEOLOGICAL PROBLEM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sing ho! Sing hey! The holy man
Who lives next door to me,
His name is on the “District Plan,”
He is a stalwart Wes-ley-an,
And full of pietee.
I used to keep the church front door,
A godly man was I.
I practically never swore—
But that was long ago, before
My neigbor came anigh.
He hath a cat of morals loose,
A beast of Stygian hue,
And it is Thomas’s nightly use
Outside my room to raise the deuce
The weary watches through.
And I have tested every trick
Experience hath taught;
The poisoned chop, the whirling brick,
The rabbit trap, the loaded stick —
All, all have come to naught i
Now, if my soul be cast away
For all the oaths I swear,
Can any theologian say
Shall Thomas’ owner have to pay
II is fair and honest share ?
Let all the horrid tale be told.
The inky outcast brute
Of late hath often made him bold
To raid my cherished chickenfold,
With strategy astute.
And I forsake my father’s kirk,
And, with a pagan hoof,
I break the Sabbath. Hard at work
I plan and sweat the cat to burke
With chicken-coops of proof.
My sinful soul is sick and sore.
Can Mitre, Stole, or Hat
My pristine innocence restore,
Or must I stumble more and more,
Sing ho ! The man I was before,
Sing hey! The saint who lives next door,
Sing hey! Sing ho! The Cat!
VICTORIA,
BRONSON ELLIOTT.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Died at Castlemaine (Vic.), after step-
ping lightly along the journalistic track for
54 years, James Robertson, aged 88. Robert-
son, a Scot from Glasgow, arrived in Mel-
bourne in 1852, and soon drifted up to
Mount Alexander, as Castlemaine was then
called. There he joined the local Mail,
and, getting on to the editorial treadmill,
worked it for the best part of half a cen-
tury—latterly as part proprietor. It was
only during the last couple of years that he
handed over the dry bones of his job to a
younger practitioner.
Sank at his moorings, in his 70th year,
Henry Newton, who for 30 years was em-
ployed in the maritime division of the
N.S.W. Public Service. He was a pilot in
the old windjammer days, when the clippers
in full sail would beat up both Port Jackson
and Newcastle,. and rose to be Superinten-
dent of Navigation. If there is any particu-
larly snug anchorage for genial old salts on
the Jasper Sea, Henry Newton is quite safe
to bring up there.

QUIZUS : “ Tell me, who are these that are
condemned to walk along such a terrible

ANSWERUS : “No! They are aldermen."
“Bails”: Tom Pawley, manager of the
English cricket team in Australia, manages
the Kent county club. Appointed after a
disastrous season in 1897, he brought in 200
new members at one swoop, and went on
hand over hand until he had raised the roll
from under 1500 to over 4000. He managed
the Kent tour in America in 1903; and
Burnup, the captain, declared that it was
“owing to Pawley’s excellent arrangements
that the thing went through without a
hitch.” Before taking on a county he was
lion. sec. of the Tunbridge Club, and saved
its ground by collecting £2637 in less than
three months. In earlier days he did some-
thing as a fast bowler, and once took three
wickets for 11 runs against Sussex.

Thomas McKay threw in the towel at
Napier (M.L.) last week after 103 rounds
with Time, unless the calculation was
wrong, which it probably was. He lived
under six British sovereigns, which equals
120 shillings, or 480 thrippences, represent-
ing 480 pints of good, bad or indifferent
beer; but T. McKay never shook his whisk-
ers in the froth of a solitary one of them.
“Rad” speaks a word: —
It is diverting to note the deadly seriousness with
which the Australian dailies treat their cables con-
cerning Winston Churchill’s proposed visit to Belfast.
Churchill is, of course, working up the agitation for
all he is worth. Barring a few semi-demented
Orangemen (who are, innocently, his most valuable
allies in the matter), it is obvious that no one is tak-
ing him and his approaching daredevilry seriously—
as witness Stead’s elaborately ironical suggestion that
Winston’s colleague Birrell should prohibit him from
speaking, in the interests of law and order. Churchill
is a born showman. The limelight is the breath of
life to him. It will be one of the golden hours of his
crowded existence when he drives slowly through
Belfast, with hundreds of police guarding his car-
riage, and an immense mob screaming cries of love
and admiration and fear and hatred in his ears. As
an instance of Churchill’s demagogish passion for the
spectacular element that is afforded by vast crowds,
he was booked, during last general elections, to speak
at. Crewe (England). The local party organisers de-
ceived the populace as to the spot where he was to
arrive, with the well-meaning idea of saving him
worry. As a result, he proceeded to the Town Hall
through deserted streets. “Where’s the crowd?” be
demanded passionately. The organisers explained
what they had done. “Well, I’m d —d if I speak,”
said Churchill, and, despite their anguished pleadings,
to that resolution he stuck. There is no doubt he
has had Ulster Hall engaged for his meeting with the
deliberate design of causing a shindy. Fear is a sen-
sation he does not know, and he does his best plat-
form work in the face of opposition. No one will be
more gratified than Churchill if his coming arouses
such a pandemonium in Belfast as the sectarianism-
crazed old place hasn’t known in its history.
Mary Gaunt, the Australian —daughter of
old Judge Gaunt, of Victoria, and sister of a
naval captain or two —has been making
some confessions in London. For her first
book, “Dave’s Sweetheart,” written in 1893,
she got £5O from the London publisher.
Then she married a doctor and gave up
writing; but, being left a young widow, she
had to earn her own living, so turned to the
inkpot again. Her first year in London was
bitter; she wrote a story every week, but
nearly all of them came
back rejected, and she
earned only £l5. Then she
was commissioned to write
a book on West Africa, suc-
ceeded, and has since made
that country her hunting-
ground, with excellent re-
sults. Her next book will
be a volume of personal ad-
ventures, with the title of
“Alone in West Africa.”
Australia has lost Thomas
Hardy, an Adelaide patri-
arch, who had done more
than most other men to
carl-y out the patriarchal
duty of making glad the
hearts of men with wine
and oil. A Devonshire boy,
he was in S. Aus. by 1850,
and took service with John
Reynell, reputed the earli-
est winemaker there. A
little later, he made some
money in the Victorian dig-
gings, and put it all into
the rich soil of the Torrens
valley, and the ideal wine-
growing slopes of the Ade-
laide hills. For over half a
century he had been ex-
porting wine; he started the
first city wine-bar, and had
much to do with demolish-
ing the idea —now extinct,
but long fostered by the im-
porter—that it was not the
Thing to offer your friends
“colonial” wine. At S.
Australia’s Jubilee Exhibi-
tion, he won a big trophy,
given by E. T. Smith, for
the exhibitor who had “done
most to establish new in-
dustries suitable to S.A.”
He deserved it. The Hardy
business is now owned by a co. R. B.
Hardy, the veteran’s only surviving son, is
its manager in Sydney.
William Knox, of the Hat, who dropped
out of Federal politics on account of his
health, had a father alive until last week.
Knox, senior, was nearer 90 than SO when
he died, so there is reason to expect that son
Bill’s liat will be long in the land if it takes
care of itself. A great thing in this world
is to be able to afford to take care of one-
self. Old James Copeland, who passed out
at Warragul (Vic.), the other day, aged 96,
was one time a partner in a Melbourne soft-
goods firm, but noticing that his health was
beginning to fail, he took up a selection at
Warragul. and lived a healthful life there
for a further 32 years.
A merciless winter having smothered
Canada and frostbitten it in 4,000,000
places, Pie Commissioner Gorge Feed has
now a chance to dish the Canadians, and
ladle out shiploads of immigrants by furi-
ously advertising. “No Blizzards in Aus-
tralia ! We don’t Know a Snow Plough
from an Egg Whisk! All Our Ice Is
Carted!” Someone should pursue and wake
Gorge up at whatever banquet he is sleeping
at, and ask him what course he intends tak-
ing. And the odds are that Gorge will mur-
mur : “Why, the next one! What is it—
entrees or sweets?”

THE UPPER ONE: “Peace! Of course I loves

thanks— an" I’m sure you'll find the other
[?]ellers wants it much more'n I do! ”
Bert Levy is a versatile sort of citizen.
His vaudeville show represents but one of
many outlets for his abounding vitality. Every
week that he is in New York he writes and
profusely illustrates for the Morning Tele-
graph a full-page dealing with the theatre.
He has a standing contract to write and
illustrate special articles for the New York
Herald. (These articles are syndicated
and appear in some hundreds of papers all
over the United States.) His personal
notes _ concerning notabilities he has met
on his travels are rushed by the Ameri-
can papers in much the same way that T. P.
O’Connor’s goods of the same sort are rushed
in England. _ In between times he is by way
of being an inventor and mechanician. For
instance, _ the contrivance with which he
throws his drawings in a magnified form on
the screen, during his present Tivoli turn,
was designed by him, and every detail of it
, —barring the lenses —was made with his
own hands in his own workshop. Inciden-
tally, he is an aurist. For 20 years, owing
to an attack of typhoid, he has been practi-
stone deaf. Most of the big ear special-
ists in Europe and America have had a go
at his trouble, and retired defeated. Ulti-
mately. he though out and built a sort of
sounding board, which he wears in either
ear, and which enables him to hear as well
as any man. Small wonder that even in the
Pork City of Chicago, this Australian artist
is hailed as a pure-bred hustler.
, With the retirement of Tame Parata from
M.L. Parliament goes the smasher of many
a threatened stonewall. Tame was a strong
follower of old-man Seddon. Sometimes in
the early hours of the morning, when things
were going askew with the party, Seddon
would put up the old rangitira. Imme-
diately Tame rose to l'torero (through an in-
terpreter) members would show a large de-
sire to get home to bed; and then business
began to move. After Seddon glided over
the border Tame Parata fell into political
oblivion: and now returns, almost unnoticed,
to his whare and his old kiwi mat.
“Lex” :
Barrister Milner Stephen, who will be Deputy Lord
Mayor while Allen Taylor is taking his rest cure, is
probably the youngest-looking man for his age in
Australia. Dunno exactly what his age is, but he
looks about 30 years less than he is, whatever he is.
(This appearance of youthfulness is, by the way,
one of the most marked characteristics of the
numerous Stephens of N. S. Wales. They hardly ever
begin to look even elderly till long after they are
dead.) Milner Stephen is a good lawyer, a solid
worker and a reliable person all round. It is a beau-
tiful and edifying thing to see him wandering
through his constituency or bailiwick, or whatever
one calls that portion of the earth which is repre-
sented or ruled, or what not by an alderman. Wool-
loomooloo is where he reigns mostly, and there are
few of the local wharf laborers’ paws that he hasn’t
wrung, in the way of business, in his time. An in-
digenous superstition credits him with having person-
ally examined everv drain, kerbing and gutter in his
principality, with a view to the unearthment of de-
fects, and the swift rectification of the same.
There is a good article on “Aus-
tralian Life in the Forties” in De-
cember Cornhill, written by the
widow of Professor Huxley, the
famous fighting scientist— “Dar-
win’s Bull-dog,” as he was called
Mrs. Huxley, who is now 86
reached Sydney just before Christ-
mas, 1848, after a stormy voyage
of nearly six months from Eng-
land. At that time she was Miss
Heathorn, and with her parents
and sisters she journeyed per bul-
lock-dray to a farm in the Wol-
longong district. After a time
she joined a married sister in Syd-
ney. While she was on that visit
R.M.S. Rattlesnake arrived to
survey the Australian coasts.
Young Huxley was on board as
assistant surgeon. Miss Heathorn
met him at a Sydney dance; they
became engaged, and “a long anil
weary” wait was followed by “the
crowning gift of 40 years of happy
wedded life.”
In his lately-published autobi-
ography, Charles Gavan Duffy
gloats over the deposition of
O’Shanassy as Premier of Vic-
toria. By that time they were
ferocious enemies. Yet O’Shan,
had been foremost in welcoming
Duffy to Australia, and was a
prime agent in raising the £2OOO
which bought Dulfy his residence at Haw-
thorn. They became Ministers of State toge-
ther, O’Shan, as Premier, Duffy as Minister
for Lands, while Dick Ireland w T as Attorney-
General. With his Land Act of 1862, Duffy
was sincerely desirous of promoting settlement.
He thought Ireland cunningly frustrated it,
putting in loopholes to facilitate the awful
dummyism that resulted. O’Shanassy is
not likely to have had a hand in this. The
simple explanation of the row was that two
Irish lions roared along one small strip of
beach, and in time each began to think the
other a bellowing excrescence.
The well-gilded Ramaciotti, who added to
his heap of money while he was in the
J. C. Williamson firm, is spending some of
it in a way that ought to be greatly to the
advantage of Australia. He has been an en-
thusiastic Defence man for years, and
worked up to the rank of Lieut.-Colonel;
and, when he quitted the J.C.W. business
and sped out of Australia, it was chiefly
with the object of learning more about the
military business. Arriving in England in
June, he put in three weeks at Aldershot.
Passing over to Italy, he manoeuvred for
a fortnight with the Italian army, then did
Staff work for a month in Rome, and, when
last heard from, was attached to the Ist
Bersaglieri. In the spring he proposes to
go through their manoeuvres with the French
and Swiss armies. “Fairly hard work,” he
writes out to another Defence enthusiast,
“but enjoyable, and of extreme value.”
ANOTHER GLOOMY OUTLOOK.
Miss Ororoke was proceeding to her home when she
was attacked by a man, who attempted to snatch her
handbag. The girl, who is an athletic young woman,
promptly knocked the man down, jumped upon him,
and tore his coat off his back. Her dismayed assail-
ant fled before the police arrived on the seen e.— News
item.
THE TOUGH : “This is the new woman fl-
yer. It ain't safe these days. Why, they'll
be doin' a man out of ’is own perfession."
John Yerran opened the S. Aus. political
campaign with a volley at Wallaroo. He
took a very comprehensive view of the situa-
tion, and 1400 persons took a very wide
view of him. They couldn’t do otherwise,
for John was never built for speed, and
Office has so deepened his chest that it
reaches nearly to his knees. He isn’t quite
the rough diamond of earlier days. But
there is plenty of the old John left, where-
fore nobody was surprised when, sitting
down after over two hours of carefully pre-
pared speech, he pulled a vast apple from
his pocket and chewed it leisurely, while the
usual vote of thanks was being discussed.
STARVATION OF THE BODY.
The fact that indigestion and constipation are
fruitful causes of organic diseases cannot be widely
known, or these only too common complaints would
not be neglected, as they are in nine cases out of 10.
“It is only indigestion’’ is a common remark, and
few realise that indigestion might be termed “starva-
tion of the body,” for food that is not digested is
not assimilated by the blood, and the body is not
properly nourished. Chamberlain’s Tablets will cure
the most obstinate ease of indigestion, and many a
long sufferer owes his present good health to a course
of Chamberlain’s Tablets.*
A HOME-BIRD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Labouchere was the possessor of a beautiful and
historic residence on the banks of the Thames —Pope’s
famous villa at Twickenham —and there he delighted
to have his friends. —S. M. Herald (18 /!/’ l2).
Some people “have” one on the turf,
And some on ’Change, or where
Pale lawyers wrangle ’neath a surf-
Like mass of frizzy hair.
Each grimy office in the town
Contains its little room
Where people have been “taken down,”
And plunged in rage and gloom.
One man, however, Labouchere,
Declined, ’twould seem, to roam.
He spurned convention’s icy stare
And “had” his friends at home.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.
A JOB FOR MURRAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wanted a man to do nothing. Experience unneces-
sary.—Advt. from Philadelphia (U.H.) Record.
Echoing Premier Weary,
“Why work?” I daily query,
“O brothers, do let others do the graft and make
the fuss;
The calm life and inactive,
I find the more attractive,
The worrying and scurrying folks often miss the
’bus !”
The ad. that I have quoted
No doubt was duly noted
By weary John —a cheery John ’twill make him as he
reads ;
He talked about retiring,
A good long rest requiring,
With nought to do, this ought to do —this job should
suit his needs.
VICTORIA.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
A prodigious amount of piffle is being
printed about the Commonwealth Bank and the sav-
ings business. The usual way of putting it is that
one of th’e new bank’s wicked functions will be “to
absorb the business of the savings banks of the re-
spective States.” “There is,” says the S. M. Herald,
whose wild language is typical, “no possible excuse
for the Commonwealth endeavoring to interfere with
State business in this respect.” This regard for
“State business” is very touching, especially when it
comes from those who, month after month, recently
fought the proposal to transfer to the State what is
known as the Barrack-street Bank in N. S. Wales —
a concern which doesn’t belong to the State, though
the State was expected to prop it up when some years
ago it looked like falling. The Savings Bank busi-
ness is, in fact, no more State business than the fire-
insurance business belongs to the fire offices already
in existence; yet, so far from throwing bricks at
new insurance concerns which have appeared, the
papers have smiled on them, and, patting them on
the back with one hand, have held out the other fist
for an advt. And though there are Comic Bits and
Funny Cuts in plenty, the Sydney Telegraph didn’t
recently say that the penny snippet business be-
longed to those who were already in it; on the con-
trary, it launched its own penny snippet. If the
Savings Bank business belongs to anybody in par-
ticular, it belongs to the people who have the best
facilities for carrying it on, and as it isn’t denied
that those facilities lie in the post office, obviously
the authority which owns the post office is, prima
facie, the authority with the best claim to the busi-
ness. Prime Minister Fisher knows, of course, what
the real trouble is. The State Governments are not
worrying about the poor depositors; they are worry-
ing about the poor Treasurers who have been in the
hahit of dipping into the Savings Bank till whenever
other people wouldn’t lend them the money, for a
financial jag. The Savings Banks have been to them
the bottle on the shelf, to which these Sarah Gamps
could put their lips whenever they felt disposed.
Fisher went so far as to recognise that little weak-
ness, and undertook to keep the bottle supplied, for
a term of years at least; but the Sarah Gamps have
developed a large appetite, and nothing will satisfy
them but the complete command of the cellar. That
is the whole trouble; the alleged regard for the poor
depositor is mere bluff.

Six months ago many of the shareholders turned
sourly away from the chairman’s speech. They had
been expecting a plum, in the shape of a new issue,
at either par or a very small premium, and they
didn’t get it. Probably the long-headed and exper-
ienced old gentlemen whose words carry most weight
said: “Wait till we see what the harvest is.” The
wool clip and the wheat harvest were not a failure,
anyway, so the plum is now to be handed out. The
present capital is £1,500.000 ; this is to be increased
to £1,750,000 by the issue of 20,000 new shares at
par—that is, £l2 10s. As Commercial of Sydney
shares have for many months stood at somewhere
about £32, this looks like a bonus of £l9 10s. per
new share; or, as six of the present shares will carry
one of the new issue, £3 ss. per old share. But that
isn’t quite the right way to put it, because for at
least a couple of years it has been obvious that, un-
less the deposits slumped very much, prudence would
demand a new issue. Hence, the shares have had an
inflated price. The position may be put this way:—
Six shares at £32 = £192. One ditto at £l2 10s.
Total for seven, £204 10s. Dividend on seven shares
at 10 p.c. per annum =£B 15s. =£4 ss. 6d. p.c.
on the purchase price. So the investor who has been
paying £32 for the shares gets only a shade over ii
p.c. on his money, notwithstanding that the plum
looks large and luscious. It is very doubtful whether
4i p.c. is a fair return to get from any bank stock.
It certainly isn’t, unless there is a reasonable expecta-
tion of more bonuses. And in that connection the
history of the past few years is not a reliable guide.
It is true that five years ago the capital was qnly
£1,000,000, and it is now proposed to put it up to
£1,750,000; but it was 10 years before the capital
was raised from £600,000 to £1,000,000, and for
the 10 years preceding that no new capital had been
got in at all. Many things, no doubt, influence a
bank in putting up its capital; but the chief thing
is the amount of public money (deposits, etc.) at
risk. To give creditors adequate protection, Aus-
tralian bankers, as a rule, think it unwise to have a
capital ( plus reserves) very much less than one-
seventh of the total of the liabilities. Owing to the
big Australian harvests of wool, wheat and mineral
of the last few years, people have been pouring money
into the banks. When bad seasons come, not only
will the inflow stop, but there will be an actual out-
flow. In 1890 the total average deposits of the Com-
mercial of Sydney in N. S. Wales for the last quarter
were £9,515,138, and of all the banks £35,460,118.
For the corresponding quarter of 1900 —10 years
later —the figures had shrunk to £9,158,321 and
£31,533,476. If this country could go on piling up
deposits year after year without a break, this bank
might also be expected to go on making rapid in-
crease after increase of its capital. But the thing
would be a miracle if it happened, and right in the
teeth of past experience.
Looking back over 10 years’ history, these extra-
ordinary results show up:—
Liabilities.

The reduction in the notes and bills item is due to
the displacement of all other notes by those of the
Commonwealth. The bank has strengthened its posi-
tion greatly. Compare the 46 p.c. increase in ad-
vances with the 93 p.c. increase in liquid assets.
This bank, at any rate, seems to have no illusions on
the subject of the present abundance of money; it
seems to realise that, even if it can keep up the level
of its deposits—which is at least uncertain —the time
will come when some of its best customers will be
turning up for an overdraft instead of with a fixed
deposit. Meantime it keeps just over 50 p.c. of its
deposit money and other debts in a liquid state—
just in case it should be wanted.

The very solid increase in advances is doubtless due
in a large measure to the cutting up of big estates
and the financing of new occupiers—not very risky
business, seeing that farming land always has a value,
and that at least 15 p.c. of the purchase money has
usually to be put up within a few months, so that at
most there is only 85 p.c. owing. Deposits and all
other liabilities total £22,360,602; so the bank has
£7 6s. of the public’s money for every £1 of its
own. The issue of the new capital will bring it
within the standard 7 to 1. Profits calculated on
capital and reserves combined don’t reach the dizzy
heights shown by some other very prosperous banks:

The officers’ provident fund gets another £2OOO for
this last half-year, and that has been taken off the
profits. Of what remains, 74 p.c. is paid away iii
divs., leaving 26 p.c. available to lay by. In a time
of abnormal prosperity and high prices, 26 p.c. is on
the small side. For it is in such times that the bad
debts are made. Theoretically, a bank shouldn’t ever
make any; it ought always to have 20s. of security
for every £1 of advance. But Australia is a country
of pretty violent fluctuations. In the first place, it
depends so largely upon “the great primary indus-
tries,” and the great primary industries are as un-
certain as a prima donna’s temper. In 1899 the
N. S. Wales wool clip was worth £10,068,400, and
in 1900 it was worth only £7,676,800. In 1907 it
was worth £17,185,100, and in 1908 only
£12,680,000. As for wheat, the Commonwealth
production in 1900-1 was 48,353,402 bushels, and in
1902-3 only 12,378,068 bushels ; in 1903-4 74,149,634
bushels, and in 1907-8 only 44,655,673 bushels. A
country that depends mainly on wool clips and wheat
harvests, subject to such bumps as that, can’t fail to
have a bumpy existence itself. To intensify the ups
and downs the wool and wheat harvests are almost
invariably both good or both bad. And when they
are both good, and things are naturally inclined to
boom owing to the abundance of money, the Govern-
ments invariably rush out and borrow large sums —
often of foreign money—and scatter the coin, and
thus make the boom froth higher than ever. When
the 90 million bushel harvest suddenly slumps to one
of 15 millions, and Cohen turns a cold eye on further
borrowing, the bank’s 20s. security has a vicious
habit of being worth about 10s., unless it has been
carefully selected.
Fortunately, there is very little evidence that Aus-
tralian banks don’t realise this; and probably there
isn’t one of them that isn’t putting a bit up its
sleeve. All of them know that something is going to
fall presently; none of them know exactly what is
going to fall. So the wise ones are stuffing away
what may be called boom profits to provide for con-
tingeheies, in the hope that they presently won’t have
to make losses good out of visible reserves. But how
much any bank is stuffing away nobody knows who
isn’t in that bank’s inmost secrets. One can’t even
be sure that every bank is doing it. All that the in-
vestor can be quite sure about is that the present con-
ditions are such that Australian banks, unless they
are handled with extreme caution, are liable to be
laying the foundations for much bad debt. They
may, or again they may not, be making internal pro-
vision to meet the contingency.
Before the bank crisis, in the early ’9o’s, the Vic-
toria had a capital of £600,000; on reconstruction,
pref. capital, to the extent of £416,760, was issued
in part-payment of the debt to the depositors. The
present capital is as much as £1,478,010 ; and that
increase, of course, greatly .strengthens the position
of all creditors. The total liabilities are £7,563,308,
which means about £4 6s. for every £1 of capital
and reserves —a very safe proportion. Naturally,
having so comparatively little public money at risk
in proportion to capital and reserves, the profits cal-
culated on capital and reserves are not large. This
is the recent record: —

half-years.

Ten years ago this little bank had £222,562 of de-
posits and £289,351 of advances; to-day, after a
run of good seasons, it has £251,354 of deposits and
£317,254 of advances, so the growth isn’t anything
to cheer about. It hasn’t any branches, and confines
itself wholly to Ballarat. Somebody said it was all
right to put your whole setting of eggs in one
basket provided you watched that basket. But
watching won’t do much good: you need to hold the
basket, and that’s just what you can’t always do.
For instance, all the watching in the world wouldn’t
have put gold into the exhausted Ballarat and Cres-
wick mines; and that is chiefly what is the matter
with Ballarat—that and the big-landlord disease. No
doubt, having the whole thing under its nose, the
bank can pretty well steer clear of bad debts; so if
there isn’t much in the way of profits, there shouldn’t
be much in the way of risks. Capital is £85,000,
and on that and reserves combined the earnings
have lately moved like this: —
June, 1908 .. £5 2 8 p.c. June, 1910 .. £5 4 0 p.c.

June, 1909.. 5 3 2 „ June, 1911.. 5 4 6,,
Dec., 1909 .. 6 3 6,, Dec., 1911.. 5 5 9 „
The bank holds 40 p.c. of all its liabilities in
liquid assets; and in every way, so far as one can
judge on figures, the position is exceptionally safe —
and exceptionally unexciting.

To understand the present position it is always neces-
sary to go back and rake over the ancient muck-heap
a little. When the old Queensland booming gang
had brought this bank to the verge of collapse, it
used the borrowing resources of the State to try to
prop the institution up ; in other words, when the
bank couldn’t borrow any more itself on satisfactory
terms, the State did the borrowing, and passed the
money on to the bank—nearly two millions of it.
Pursuant to a revised reconstruction scheme adopted
in 1897, both the State and private depositors under-
took to forego interest on one-fourth of their de-
posits ; the private depositors, moreover, agreed to
accept payment of their fourth out of future profits.
This fourth was accordingly written off, and appears
nowhere in the accounts —not even as a contingent
liability, though it certainly is one. William Kidston
made a further re-arrangement with the bank when
he was Premier, taking promissory notes for the
whole of the debt to the State. The bank had the
right to prepay these p.ns., and there are only four
that haven’t been disposed of, payable 1918-19-20-
21. The entire position, so far as the old debt is
concerned, is as follows: —

A further 6d. will be paid next March. This will be
4s. provided in six years. In each of the past two
half-years £30,000 has been appropriated, and the
management evidently reckons upon being able to at
least keep that up, and £60,000 a year is about
Is. 2d. in the £. After the next 6d. is paid, there
will remain 14s. to pay; and if £30,000 can be ap-
propriated each half-year, the whole debt will be
wiped out in 12 years. The future must tell whether
that much or more can be spared for the purpose.
The bank’s new deposits, being a first charge, are
so well protected that there has evidently been no
difficulty in getting new money for new advances.
This shows the chief movements in 10 years:—

As good a feature as any is the complete disappear-
ance of the item, “Private ledger accounts.” These
were always understood to be very dubious assets in-
deed ; but either they have been written down so hard
out of profits, or they have recovered so much in
value, that they have now been disposed of without
further loss. If they have been written down, it
means that the profits thus hitherto employed are
available now for other purposes ; while, if they have
improved in value, it speaks very hopefully for all
the old assets. The deposits haven’t grown much in
bulk during the year, but that is only because the
Government deposit account has been drawn upon for
just over half a million ; and the same circumstance
explains most of the shrinkage in liquid assets. Yet
even now the liquid assets are extraordinarily strong;
they amount to 36 p.c. of the entire liabilities, in-
cluding the interminable stock (old private de-
posits). The bank could do a million more in ad-
vances if the right .securities were available. This re-
straint looks well.
If Queensland does even fairly well, and if no poli-
tical or other accident happens, the time will come
when Q.N. shareholders will be in clover. The capi-
tal is only £413,484, in £5 shares, paid to £3;
and, though there is a preferential capital of
£3,091,669, the latter carries only 34 p.c. This
pref. capital will, of course, justify the bank in tak-
ing and using far more deposit money than it other-
wise could prudently do; already the new deposits,
etc., amount to over £ll for every £1 of the share
capital. Certainly, in the event of that money, or
part of the interminable three millions odd, being
lost, the reserves and share capital will be first
drawn upon to make it good ; and there is a big risk
in that. But, in return for taking that risk, all the
surplus profits will belong to the shareholders.

Compared with other trustee cos., this pioneer in the
business has a very big capital; its lot is £90,000,
while the two important concerns doing similar busi-
ness in N. S. Wales have been getting along very well
on £25,000 apiece, with a big amount uncalled. The
size of the paid capital doesn’t seem to affect the
volume of a trustee Co.’s business very much; pro-
bably not one client in a. dozen Who isn’t also a share-
holder could say what amount the shareholders have
dropped into the till as a guarantee that the co.
won’t skip to Callao, or rhisread its instructions, and
so lose trust money, or do any other thing that an
honest and competent trustee shouldn’t do. But
though it apparently doesn’t make much difference to
the business profits whether the paid'- capital is
£90,000 or £25,000, it makes a lot of difference to
the rate of dividend when those profits come to be cut
up ; and that explains why this co., in spite of the
fact that it has a bigger value of estates in hand
than any other trustee co. in Australia, pays only 7
p.c., while others no better conducted pay 10 p.c.
In consideration of its big capital or guarantee fund*
it might be justified in dividing a big whack of its
profits; but it doesn’t do anything at all extravagant
in that way; and there is every reason to believe also
that it doesn’t draw too heavily on its corpus com-
missions. It is through those commissions that a
trustee co. can put on its profits almost whatever face
it pleases—for a brief time, of course. The annual
commission of 2$ p.c. on the income from a £30,000
estate only brings in about £37 10s. a year; but
the 24 p.c. chargeable once on the gross capital value
of the estate means £750. The co. that drops that
£750 into profit as soon as the estate comes into its
hands, will bulge the profits for that term ; but the
chances are that the £37 10s. a year, which it will
get thereafter, won’t pay the actual costs of admin-
istration. Scarcely a trustee co. tells how it deals
with the £750; and that destroys most of the value
of such cos.’ accounts. The only serviceable thing to
do is to consider the results over a fairly long span
of years. A general survey of this co. shows that,
while profits are irregular, they are at the least well
maintained; and a 7 p.c, div. or more has been
earned in every half-year in the past decade. Ten
years ago the total value of the trust estates in hand
was £6,569,543; it is now £9,314,730. Though
the profits vary, they have lately averaged 2s. sd. p.c.
of the capital value of the estates. The average pro-
fits nowadays should, therefore, be about £3400
p.a. better than those of 10 years back. Moreover,
though with a slump in property values the corpus
commission would also shrink, the volume of trustee
business done by this and other professional trustee
cos. may be reasonably expected to grow steadily.

This co. owns the fine premises crowned by a
bronze lady who for years past has been hailing a
ferry at Circular Quay without getting any for’arder
with the business. The buildings, including the pub
tucked away at the rear, stand in the books at
£69,750. They have- stood at £70,000 for so long
that the change comes as a shock. Property there-
abouts certainly hasn’t been wilting of late; but pro-
bably the co. concludes that even Hawkesbury sand-
stone won’t last for ever, and that it is a fair thing
to strike £250 off the value for a start. At any
rate, the item has come down that much ; and, though
£250 worth may have been sold, it is far more likely
that that much has been written off profits as a set-
off against the natural decay of the asset. The capi-
tal is £30,000; and, as reserves amount to only
£18,753, it is plahi that somebody else’s money is
also in the premises. The necessary £20,000 or so
to make up the £69,750, is represented by deben-
tures. Considering the very fair size of the reserves,
the profits are extremely moderate; but the Exchange
isn’t wholly a money-grubber, and, anyway, the
security is just about as unimpeachable as city real
estate knows how to be.

Melbourne is invited to put up the modest
£lO,OOO required by the promoters of the Photo.
Vista Film Production Co. The vendor syndicate has
already started the business of producing Australian
scenic and other films for home and foreign con-
sumption, and it makes out a very good case for ex-
pecting a big increase in business. Australia greedily
takes the scenic films of other countries, and drama-
tic films with a foreign setting, and the promoters
seem to be on safe ground when they assume that
other countries will do the same if offered Australian
films. The proposition is speculative, but is well
worth considering.

The Derwent and Tamar Insurance Co.,
doing 1 fire and marine business, has now permitted
itself to be swallowed by a British co. with a brass-
nlate in these parts. The co. is the oldest in Australasia,
having been formed in 1838, and it has throughout
maintained its head-office in Hobart. For some time
oSt it has shown very little enterprise, and its pre-
miums are actually less now than 10 years
ago. Still its business has been profitable, and a 16
pTc. dividend has been paid of late. However, it is
old and weary, and when a co. comes along and
offers it £2 16s in cash, it seems that it has so
much of the miser in it that it can’t resist the chance
to hear the coin jingle. Yet, if its assets are what
it says they are, it is giving the business away, in-
asmuch as £2 16s. per share will oniy mean
£l4O 000; and in October last its surplus of assets
over liabilities was £132,341, after providing for
the current dividend. Altogether it is a poor bar-
gain - *.
Mechanical concentration is generally
wasteful; but, with the flotation process added, there
isn’t very much thrown on to the dumps now at
Broken Hill. The ore fed into the Sulphide Corpora-
tion’s lead mill last year averaged ll.loz. silver, 16.3
p.c. lead, and 17.3 p.c. zinc. After it had gone
through the lead mill, and what was left had passed
on to the zinc mill, the residues were worth 2.30 z.
silver, 2.3 p.c. lead, and 4.6 p.c. zinc. This shows it
in another way:—
Silver, Lead, Zinc,
oz. tons. tons.
In Ore .. .. 2,530,075 37,046 ' 39,666
In Residues .. 260,469 2,626 5,245
Extracted 2,269,606 34,420 34,421
Unfortunately, this doesn’t mean that the co. gets
paid full price for all the “extracted” metal. Thus,
there were 3842 tons of zinc in the lead concentrates
(8.8 p.c. zinc) ; and it is no good there. And in the
zinc concentrates there were: —
Total Contents. Assay Value.
Silver .. .. 1,050,546 oz. 14’7 oz.
Lead .. .. 7,782 tons 10'9p.c.
Zinc .. .. 30,579 „ 42'7 „
And you can’t get full value for any of those metals,
because you can’t get them all out. A de-leading
process is now working on the zinc concentrates;
and that makes the third stage in the long process of
metal-extraction. First, the ore goes into the lead
mill, which divides it into two parts—(l) silver-lead
concentrates; (2) zinc “tailings.” The silver-lead
concentrates go on to the lead-smelting furnaces; the
resulting silver-lead bullion goes on to the refinery,
where the silver and the lead are separated. The zinc
“tailings” go on direct to the zinc mill, whose feature
is oil-acid flotation; and here again there is a separa-
tion into two products—(l) zinc concentrates ; (2)
residues. The contents of the zinc concentrates were
mentioned above. Being so rich in lead, it wouldn’t
be profitable to send them straight to the zinc fur-
naces or retorts; so there is a third stage—the de-
leading process. That once more separates the con-
centrates into two products—(l) lead concentrates,
low in zinc; (2) zinc concentrates, low in lead. The
lead concentrates go to the lead furnaces; the zinc
to the spelter works. It is a long and elaborate busi-
ness; but as much as possible'of it is done automati-
cally. Costs at the Sulphide Corporation work out
as follows:
Per ton.
s. d.
Mining .. .. .. • • 12 11
Development .. .. • • 14
Lead concentration .. .. .. 6 5
Zinc concentration . . . . . . 7 1
The zinc concentration is per ton of “tailings” fed
into that mill—not per ton of original ore. The
bulk of the silver-lead smelting is done in Australia;
but this co. now exports its zinc concentrates, and
Europe has the highly profitable job of producing the
metallic spelter. #
The Ore Concentration Co., which owns
the Elmore flotation patents, held its annual meeting
in London lately, and naturally all the talk was about
the litigation with the Sulphide and Zinc Corpora-
tions. Things haven’t gone well, so far, with the El-
more people; “but,” said the chairman, “if we lose,
we shall only have lost under the one special patent
which we have claimed infringement of, and will still
be in a position to start a new suit under other
patetits.” Other shareholders agreed that “the only
policy to adopt was to back up our patents to the
very last penny we have” ; so Costs is evidently in
for a good time.
* * * *
The Grampians Dredging Co., yyhich oper-
ates at Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.), has so far suffered
from bad luck or something. Dredging began in
February, 1911. Scarcity of water was the first
trouble; the second was a flood, in which the dredge
was washed out. Floating operations occupied about
a month, since when dredging has gone on spasmodi-
cally, with frequent intervals for repairs to dredge
and dam, the latter having a nasty habit of breaking
away at awkward times. These troubles have resulted
in numerous changes, but not much better luck.
Gundagai, one of the Ma State’s most
tantalising fields, is again dangling a baited hook
in front of the public’s eyes. The jeweller’s shop
this time is in the Long Tunnel, into which Rice and
party pushed their way a couple of years ago to
clean out and open up some old workings. The find
was made at 280 ft.; 86 tons from the rich patch
gave 3070 z., and then a few dishes of quartz dollied
2720 z. The usual yarn is told that this extraordin-
ary development clearly proves that the mine is im-
proving with depth. What it does prove is that
Gundagai is the same old Gundagai that it was
when the Prince of Wales Co. gave it a long* trial.
“Underlay” : The one N.S.W. field where
mining is regularly done for platinum is at Fifield.
Here a lead has been worked in patches for a length
of over two miles, and a width of as much as 200 ft.
When this lead was first worked for platinum in 1892,
the metal was sold locally for from 14s. to 16s. per
oz. Two and a half years ago it brought £2 17s. 6d.
per oz. ; then another market was found, and the
price rose to £3 15s. per oz. Twelve months ago
another buyer offered £4 if the miners would guar-
antee him up to lOOoz. They agreed, and completed
the verbal contract, although they were offered more
money meanwhile. Last year the price steadily rose,
until Christmas week saw it up to £5 7s. 6d., and
during' the holidays the local buyer offered *.6,
which is the present price. Whether the Russian
output has been decreasing during the last year or
two, thus affecting the market, I don t know, but the
fact that buyers are competing for the output is sig-
nificant. During the last few years an average of
about 15 men have been making a living out of the
lead. The sinking is from 40ft. to 60ft., and the
washdirt averages about 6in. thick. It is puddled
by machine, at a cost of 3s. 6d. per load, the yields
going from 3oz. to Boz. per mill of 12 loads lhe
general run of it is about 4oz. per mill. The bottom
is very good driving. The miners take it in benches,
gouging under the wash about 2ft., and then gad-
ding and picking the stuff down. After the opening-
up work has been done, one man will break hall a
load per day. It was in 1889 that the lead was
prospected by Connolly Bros, and Smith, who worked
for gold with Government aid, cutting the lead in
three different places with shafts and drives, they
obtained good prospects of platinum; but, taking it
for native silver in minute quantities, they threw it
away. It was not until three years later that Smith,
having found out what the white metal was, came
back and started work on the lead, which is now
called Platina. For a distance of over two miles
platinum and gold are to be found at or near the
surface, with here and there streaks of wash, but
these deposits are only occasionally payable, though
some very good patches have been obtained, there
are dredging possibilities here. Also, the individual
miner has a chance, not only along the old lead, but
further out; for there is tin to be had as well as the
rarer metals.
» * * -
If the Ivyloe (N.S.W.) report means
what it says, the mill is doing amazingly good work.
Thus: “At the ore-dressing mill regular work was re-
sumed on Monday, January 8, and the mill ran 119
hours, treating 248 tons of 5.5 p.c. ore. From the
picking belt four tons of prill, assaying 20.1 p.c.,
were removed to the bagging sheds; 39 tons 4ewt.
of jig concentrates, containing 8 tons 2cwt. oi
copper, and 19 tons lOcwt. of flotation concentrates,
containing 4 tons 9cwt. of copper, were bagged tor
despatch.” Ore treated, 248 tons, worth 5.5 p.c.;
that means 13 tons 13cwt. metal. Recovery was 16cwt.,
plus 8 tons 2cwt., plus 4 tons 9cwt.; total, 13 tons
7ewt. If this plant can get 13 tons 7cwt. out of a
possible 13 tons 13cwt., and do it at a reasonable
cost, it ought to turn the eyes of every poor copper
gouger in Australia towards the oil-acid flotation
process. *

Bullfinch Prop. (W.A.) has finally de-
cided to treat its ore by wet crushing, fine grinding,
filtering, etc., and to drive the whole show with
electric motors. Most of the required plant has been
ordered, and, all doing well, Doolette and Co. hope to
have it running in August. Another lev%l (the
third) has been started at 310 ft., and as rich ore was
found on the 210 ft. level in every spot where the sul-
phides were exposed, it is confidently expected that
the lode at 310 ft. will prove all right. The public
which dropped its bundle, the Government which built
the railway, the unhinged individuals who gave fancy
prices for town lots—in fact, everyone except those
who specked and are still “out” of the shares, fer-
vently hopes so. # *
In a report just to hand, Cosmopolitan
Prop. (W.A.) sighs over its reduced circumstances,
and expresses a hope that its luck will turn some
dav. A few years ago this co. was very affluent, ana
between 1900 and 1906 paid £129,000 in dividends.
Then the mine went wrong at depth, and the evi-
dence is all against it ever getting right again.
Work on the co.’s account was discontinued some
time ago, and portions of the property were let on
tribute. Since then about £4500 has been raked in
from the treatment of old residues and from tnbut-
ing, but most of it has been mopped up in office
exes., etc. This sort of thing will probably continue
until the exes, get into arrear, when no doubt the co.
will lie down and die. # *
Everything that could be done was done
to make shareholders cheerful at the Kalgurli meet-
ing ; but, although the chairman did his best, the
mine is in so unsatisfactory a state in the bottom
that he did not succeed. Still, the property will
figure high up on the producing list for years, but
the shrivelling of dividends, which started a year
ago, looks like continuing. #
Keen disappointment is expressed in the
annual report of the London, Australian and General
Exploration, Ltd., at the wretched results obtained
from the co.’s Australian holdings, owing, it is alleged,
to the difficulty of securing suitable labor and the
high cost of working. The Lancefield and Northern
mines (W.A.) are mentioned as having suffered
acutely. During the year the former yielded
£140,578, and the latter £98,652, but in each case
no surplus remained after providing for development
and capital expenditure. Many Australian properties
were considered, but were turned down after exten-
sive examination. As a result of fortunate specula-
tions elsewhere—at Maikop and Porcupine—the co.
shows a surplus for the year of £27,246, after pro-
viding for management and exploration. Having re-
gard for the general fall in market prices during the
last six months, provision has been made for depre-
ciation to the extent of £25,000. Present cash re-
sources (including the uncalled liability on certain
shares) are declared to equal the market valuation
of the co.’s scrip.

£lB,OOO per month) should not be maintained dur-

° * * * *
Ingliston Extended (W.A.) states that
last year’s operations resulted in a surplus of £4730.
Depreciation of plant, etc., has been deducted from
this, leaving a balance of £3272. The debentures
outstanding at June 30, 1910, totalled £3500, but
all of this has since been repaid. The overdraft has
also been wiped off, and the co.’s property at Meeka-
tharra is now free from debt. In order to provide
funds for additional machinery and development, the
capital was lately increased by the creation of 5000
additional £1 shares, all subscribed for at par.

The mines that scored the best dividends
at Charters Towers last year were New Brilliant
Freehold, 6s. per share (£45,000); East Mexican,
ss. 6d. (£33,962); Mills’ United, 2s. (£30,000).
All these three were working the Day Dawn forma-
tion —which, having so many branches, and cross-
veins, is really a network. Only 20 p.c. of the field s
dividends for the year came from outside the Day
Dawn The rival Brilliant is, however, now coming
rapidly to the front again, the most important de-
velopments being the run of good stone in the Bril-
liant Deeps, and the improvement in the Brilliant
Extended’s No. 20 eastern hanging-wall level—the
latter a healthy bit of news, because the Extended,
as a pioneer of deep levels at 2850 ft., has hitherto
had noor luck.
Mount Elliott (Q.) strike is off, and, as
far as appearances go, so is the Hampden amalgama-
tion. It is doubtful whether either Hampden or
Elliott is very anxious to have the tape-measure run
over its extractable reserves, Elliott especially. That
amalgamation on fair terms would be a good thing
is, however, pretty clearly proved by the fact that
Hampden has given up the poor job of turning its
rich ore into low-grade matte, and, instead, is ship-
ping the stuff direct to the Elliott furnaces.
Thompson’s Oaks (N.Q.) has 24 acres
and 150,000 issued shares, so the 24 acres have a stiff
job in front of them. In sinking the main shaft a
reef was passed through, and a trial lot of 100 tons
will go through a neighbor’s battery within the next
week or two. That crushing will be worth watching,
though it ought to be accompanied by an assurance
that the stuff hasn’t been picked at all. The reef
was 4ft. wide where met, but it varies considerably
both in size and value. At the bottom of an under-
lay winze, put down on the lode, the face was 2ft.
6in. wide, and in that 14ft. values have run from
over £5O down to about £5 a ton. The co. has a
few hundreds in hand, and the crushing ought to
make the financial position easy for a while.
Ballarat, during the December quarter, put
up a poor record, with 51870 z. won and £l4OO paid
in divs. Men employed have dwindled down to 800,
almost all of whom work in Ballarat East. Out of
436 stampers, only 227 are falling, and the worst is
there is no immediate prospect of a change. Ballarat
is always careful not to give the tonnage dealt with,
thinking the figures will frighten speculators away,
whereas it is the absence of such facts that keeps sen-
sible men aloof from the field.
New Langi Logan, Ararat (Vic.), is not
out of the verdant stage —work is in the green rock
that precedes green wash, and generally co-ordinates
with a green market. Present strongest symptoms
are a tendency to delirium over the result of wash
bores. The public is ready to swallow almost any-
thing the gentle market manipulator may suggest.
At such times it is little use administering caution
tablets of the sort that King has given to British
Broken Hill optimists. The first Langi Logan
bore, by running through green rock into a blank,
ought to have reduced the fever, especially as the
yarn had gone round that it would rip into rich
wash. This miss meant sending the under level ahead
200 ft., putting in a cross drive, and providing locks
to reduce the risk of a burst of water and slum.
Notwithstanding that cold douche, the faithful still
expect a £2 market for shares on a prospect of gold
from bores. The mine would then stand in the mar-
ket at £200,000. For a claim on a lead into which
a pick has never been put, this is a tough price,
especially as there is that loan of £6OOO or £BOOO
to be repaid to the North Langi Logan, with 12 or
18 months of calls ahead to meet costs of pumping
and entering the wash. #
“Wash good and water light,” was
the report concerning the No. 1 bore of the Cath-
cart Central (Vic.), and now No. 2 has punched into
light wash, giving colors of gold, with “water
fair.” This switch-on of the water bugbear has
switched off the market enthusiasm for the time
being, especially as nobody knows exactly what good
wash” means. The co. hit some rubbly quartz in its
first bore, and looked for a leaven of the same
material in its No. 2, but had to go without any
self-raising flour of the kind. Cathcart has declared
another shilling div., but as it has only £2200 left
on current account and deposit, there won t be much
stiffening for Centrals from that quarter till manager
Bryant starts panelling at No. 3 shaft.
* * * *
Every winter used to see All Nations a
happy little gamble to cheer every fireside in Wood’s
Point (Vic.). Now there is no relief from call-pay-
ing, spring, summer, winter or autumn. Six months
have just been spent in testing Lawson’s reefs, be-
tween the All Nations and Lawson’s tunnels; but
rising, winzing and driving only show that b°th the
shoot of gold and the reef are narrow and poor at
depth as shoots and reefs so often are at Wood s
Point. Only a third of the shares on the register are
alive to face the responsibility of testing the eastern
workings, where some decent prospects help to keep
the spark of life just flickering.* *
New Moon, Bendigo, is in a cloudy sky.
The present co. began to pay regular divs. in 1892,
six years after it took things over from the old co.,
and it hasn’t been out of the list in any year since. Its
best record was in 1894, when £2 a share was paid;
last year, mainly through the gleanings of tributors,
it managed 3s. On a capital of £12,000 the co. has
paid £353,400; and it’s things like that that send
Bendigo’s hand to its pocket on call days. Ihe bot-
tom workings, down to 2425 ft., hold out no bright
promises, and it is to the spurs in the upper work-
ings and the Paddy’s Gully line that the sharehomers
are looking now. Fortunately the co. has about
£3500 tucked by, and there’s always a trifle to be
done for the public at the battery.
So far as the Paddy’s Gully line is concerned, New
Moon doesn’t get much encouragement from its stable
mate, New Argus. “Operations down to 480 ft. have
not uncovered anything payable. There is a fine
show of reef country,” says old battler George H.
Green, the chairman, “but it is wanting in the essen-
tial quality.” So the gold nowadays has to come out
of the shareholders’ pockets—and the “sale of shares
that figures in the accounts tells of tired shareholders,
in spite of the co.’s record of £126,000 in divs.
After a muck-needed stope filling and
timbering campaign, Mount Lyell is gradually get-
ting into its old stride. It is estimated that the rise
in wages will cost £20,000 a year, or (say) Is. per
ton if the works are run up to their full capacity.
Costs may thus climb to 21s. per ton of crudes; but
even so, assuming that for years ahead the average
recovery is to be two units of fine copper, together
with ss. worth of precious metals, the total, with
copper at £6O, will be 245. for copper and ss. for
precious metals = 295. per ton, won at a cost of 21s.
A 2 p.c. average will make blister cost £52 lUs. ,
but the metal contents will total £72 10s., leaving
a comfortable margin of £2O per ton of blister.
Mount Bischoff’s output for the last quar-
ter of the year was 280 tons of tin oxide. The divs.
declared for the last three months mount up to
£lB,OOO, so the profit was over £64 per ton ot
oxide —-which is more than the metal brought for
many years. The co. is in clover while the tin
boom lasts; but the market is tricky, and the quicker
those 3,000,000 tons of ore in reserve are rushed out
the better. »

M.L.G.: You say this paper is incon-
sistent because, having once attacked the Citizens for
feeding the Ordinary branch at the expense of the In-
dustrial, it now regrets that the National Mutual
missed its chance to establish an Industrial branch to
feed its Ordinary business. There’s no inconsistency
about it. This paper doesn’t now suggest that the
National Mutual should have fed its Ordinary branch
at the expense of the Industrial policy-holders. What
it does say is that it is easier to get Industrial policy-
holders than Ordinary policy-holders, and that, hav-
ing got a man to take an Industrial policy, and keep
it for a while, an office is in a good position to pass
him on to the Ordinary branch. In short, an Indus-
trial branch, though it gives poor results in itself, is
of value to the office and the community as an in-
surance kindergarten; for which reason The Bulle-
tin has affirmed that no office should be permitted to
do Industrial business unless it also has an Ordinary
branch, to the superior advantages of which it should
be obliged to draw attention on every document
issued to the Industrial policy-holder H.A.W.:

Professor Richards, in the revised edition of his
celebrated edition of “Ore Dressing,” 1909, devotes
14 pages to the Wilfley concentrating table, and,
among other things, remarks (p. 1465): “The first
table sold was installed in the Puzzle Mill, at
Breckenridge, Colorado. Since then over 12,000
have been sold. This machine has revolutionised
ore-dressing, and as such is a phenomenal success.
The original form and forerunner of all modern
shaking tables with longitudinal riffles. .. .has been
greatly improved.” And further on (p. 1478) re-
marks: “One feature of the Wilfley table to which
especial attention should be called is its ability to
separate sands from slimes while making the ordinary
concentration. This separation is more complete
and thorough than can be made by any classifier,
and should be taken advantage of in designing
mills.” In the last extensive additions made to the
treatment plant at the Golden Horseshoe Mine,
W.A., a large number of Wilfley Table Concentra-
tors were installed. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, Limited, South Melbourne, have the sole
manufacturing rights.*

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, Januaby 22, 1912.
My Dear Arini, —
The peaceful Japanese invasion of this
hamlet was accompanied by volleys of light
refreshments and deafening rounds of soft-
soap. We are some miles away from the
Jap wave which trickled over Thursday
Island a few years ago. A man who admits
he dodged some of last week’s heathen
gatherings tells me that anyone who’s been
in a land where the early-rising Jap is on
top, lias lost his enthusiasm for Nippon and
its flowers and its heavenly kimono. “In
Thursday Island, a few years ago (I only
passed through that variegated spot by the
way), the Japs, who then formed the bulk
of the population took all the sidewalk, when
there was a sidewalk, and the occasional
white man had to make way for them or
fight them. Perhaps the rougher-type pagan
is nearer to his national traditions than
the polished Asiatic, cased in modern naval
frills. But the Jap who used to edge the
European off the path up north, bore a
strong family likeness to the unsmiling bar-
barians who soaked the night-clothes of the
Queen of Korea in kerosene. A wood-heap
and a lighted match finished that misunder-
standing.”
When the Jap cruisers, Aso and Soya,
were here about two years ago, they gave
away paper flowers, and other equally-suit-
able symbols of their eternal friendship, to
the womenkind of this city. They also
rigged up various side-shows, at their after-
noon tea and saki party on the Jap flagship.
Their recent visit was garnished with the
same Asiatic arts. The single-stick perform-
ance—a bamboo cousin to fencing with the
foils on —had a glint of. heathen humor shed
upon it by a report in the daily press. This
brown feat of arms occurred between two
Japs at the Royal Naval House. When
the mock combat was over, a Jap officer-
doubtless Petty Officer Malaprop—proudly
remarked: “The soul of the Samurai was
contracted by means of this sport in ancient
times.”
Mildred Fry, M.A., and her sister Edith,
B.A. (of Manly), signalled “All’s well”
from Adelaide on their way to the Land of
Grey Skies and Wintry Winds. Mildred,
who s still on the sunny side of 30, snatched
, de S r( ;e at the precocious age of 18.
She s going to represent the secondary
schools of N.S.W. at the coming congress
of pedagogues in London. Edith, the
younger, 8.A., will plank her easel down in
foreign parts during the larger half of her
year, abroad, and chase Art in a Parisian
studio. Landscape and still life are the
Manly girl’s chief weapons of attack.
Mrs. Edgeworth David, of this town, is
picking up, in Hobart, some careless strands
of health that went astray last year, and
records good results. Daughter Molly, who’s
been sampling Britain’s rainfall and an
Edinburgh Sabbath, with brisk jaunts
through Continental languages, flutters
back to the Sydney home nest in March.
Rid Sophia, of great fame in Tarawera
(M.L.), lived long enough to brood in gloomy
foreboding over the death of her son.
He made his sad, dark brown exit on his
way with the unlucky Maori troupe to Eng-
land, and the brown tattooed mind, full of
superstitious fears, became “pourri,” or des-
pondent at this bad omen. Poor old pic-
turesque Sophia never really enjoyed her
dried shark again. Between her and her
younger, sharper rival, guide Maggie Papa-
kura, there was no relationship, but an
ancient hostility and a veiled cat-chorus of
anger.
Five years or so ago, Sophia, even then as
straight as a flagpole, tramped with me over
the smouldering fires of Whakarewarewa
Her tall, gaunt figure, dark, chiselled fea-
tures, sombre eyes, and frankly Maori out-
look, were picturesque remnants of the past
She cared nothing for the drop of white
blood in her veins, and was pure Maori in
her sympathies. She served up the legends
of her tribe (with heavy embroidery), and
let loose a ton or two of scathing comment
on the Maori who “pretended to be a
pakeha” (white man). How much of the
Arawa fist, which distributed black eyes at
the Maori welcome home the other day is
mixed up with the departed Sophia’s ven-
detta, deponent knoweth not.
James Ashton, ex-Minister for Lands in
the Wade. Government, is off to Maoriland
±or , a .l\ olida y cruise amongst its statutes,
and will particularly poke into land laws
and similar complications.
~ A w ® ek ? a S°’ Harling Point and
thereabouts had a severe rush of matri-
monial engagements along its water-lapped
shores. Now Point Piper dashes into the

promontory, reiterates the glad tidings of

A slip of a girl, Dorothy aßeckett Terrell
who lives in the Great Fog, recently caught
the judge’s eye and cantered off with a £250
prize. Cassell’s publishing firm held out
this glittering bait to the writer of the best
serial story for girls. The winner has a
splash of ink in her heredity, for her great-
grandfather was Gilbert a’Beckett, one of
the founders of London Punch. The story,
“Sister-in-Chief,” which is now meandering
through the Girls’ Realm, is handled with
skill for a youngster, though the inevitable
English vicar’s family (large, high-spirited
and inclined to kick over the traces of the
Shorter Cathechism) forms the peg of the rather
conventional drapery. The girl’s success in
getting away with the solid sum of £250 in
the early days, when she is only an inkling,
so to speak, rather than a full-grown inky-
wayfarer, has brought her forward with • a
rush, as a young person on a good wicket.
Sir John Nutting, now in Australia seek-
ing health, and looking up the captain-son
who is on the staff of Governor Denman, is
in the beer business, being head of the firm
of E. and J. Burke, who bottle Guinness’s
stout for export. Sydney has long been con-
nected roundaboutly with the same brand, the
late Chief Justice Darley having been a
grandson of the boss of the Guinness firm.
A wedding that happened at Christ
Church, in Moss Vale, the other day, recalled
the earliest moments of the high-toned dis-
trict. The bride was Miss Eileen Estella,
youngest daughter of the late P. H. Throsby,
of Redbraes, a descendant of that first Mrs.
Throsby, who, as a little girl Broughton,
was rescued from the Maoris a hundred years
since by Alexander Berry, after the his-
toric massacre. The ’groom, who scarcely
counts in these matters, was A. J. S. Lind-
say, of Adelaide.
Mr. Henry Osborne, whose engagement to
Miss Marjorie Lord was announced the other-
day, has retired to a hospital to have his
appendix removed. First his heart, now his
appendix! The carelessness of the modern
young man is amazing.
Mrs. Arthur Macarthur was swept out to
sea, at Manly, last week, and was towed
ashore ultimately in an apparently
drowned condition. The business of restor-
ing animation was forthwith entered upon
by the local life-saving club. It is a moot
question whether it is better to be drowned
outright, or to be saved and brought to on a
beach which is seldom inhabited by less than
two or three thousand people.
The Rainauds, who were burned out of
La Corniche recently, have trailed sadly
back to their old stand at Bayview. All
their fascinating French plate, cutlery, etc.,
went up in smoke on that disastrous occa-
sion. In fact, practically nothing was left
to them barring their ability to cook. The
Bayview place is where Lord Dudley and a
party of his, had to wait two hours one
Sunday, owing to the vast crowd of feeders
that monopolised the luncheon tables.
From a male acquaintance:—
All the late Henry Labouchere’s vast wealth goes
to his only child, the Princess Rudini. I remember
her as Dora Labouchere, a small, precocious child,
years ago, at the Bellevue, Cadenabbia. Labby visited
the Italian lakes every summer before passing on to
Milan and Florence. People used to say that his
daughter was the only human being he cared about.
Anyhow, he let her do and say what she liked.
Those were the days of table d’hote meals for all
hands, rich and poor, the expensive d la carte
system not having penetrated outside cities like
Paris, Vienna, etc. Labby and his child sat opposite
us. In the heat and fray of dinner one night a stout
American woman grabbed a passing waiter, and
clamored for “lemonard gazooze.” The man ap-
peared bewildered. “She means ‘limonade gazeuse,’
waiter,” said the Labouchere infant kindly, in the
shrill tones of childhood. Labby was delighted, but
the lady from the U.S. looked as though she would
have liked to reward the child’s well-meant effort
with a hearty slap. Miss Labouchere’s prince turned
out the worst of bad eggs, and she has lived apart
from him for years. One sees her referred to in the
London papers, by the way, as “beautiful.” It is
absurd. She is short and stocky, and the possessor
of an indifferent complexion—the antithesis of the
Princess of Suburbia’s dreams. Her strong suit has
always been her wit, social sense and'brains—which
latter she gets from both sides of her family. Hen-
rietta Hodson, her mother, was esteemed by good
judges to be a brilliant woman as well as a useful
actress. However, these things didn’t prevent her
marriage with Labby being a rank failure.
“Maorilander” drops in with a romance of
the ink-pot:—
Veteran Henry Brett, of the Auckland Star,
started many years ago as a comp, on a small news-
paper effusion in Hastings (Eng.). Then he turned
his face to the Maori Isles, and did some smart work
as a shipping reporter on an Auckland evening
paper. After this, Brett and C. M. Reid, once a
Presbyterian parson, and later a leader writer on a
Sydney daily, joined together as newspaper pro-
prietors. Reid had a pen with a “bite” in it, and
the partners were not in accord in this matter, for
Brett was not a hard biter by nature. They agreed
to separate. Each put in a tender for the proprie-
torship of the Auckland Star. Brett’s offer was ac-
cepted, and after that he sailed along in smooth seas
to affluence. Reid struck a less promising location,
and pulled his journalistic boat against the stream
without getting to the gilded harbor that Brett had
attained so easily.
Organist Lilian Frost sends a coo-ee from
the southern fringe of Australasia, on the
Ancient Way to London. The Sydney
musician’s fingers will skip over the key's
of various organs during her stay in the
overcrowded city of Bullville. She will
also extend a white caressing hand to the
music pipes that peal in sundry Parisian
churches.
Is Andrew Higginson getting weary of
stage love-making, or did the weather-beaten
“Miss Gibbs” finally knock him down and
jump on his love trills? The Matinee Girl
no longer furiously applauds the Higginson
saunter, and remains unmoved when he
kisses the back hair of Olive Godwin in “The
Quaker Girl.”
Jessie Lonnen, the new arrival, who takes
the part of Phoebe in “The Quaker Girl,”
is a fascinating minx, for no very obvious
reason. A tall and rather lean slab of a
girl, a trifle stiff in her knee action, she
grins one’s attention and won’t let it go.
Blanche Browne is another puss who hangs
on to the better feelings of the audience, for
some occult reason not connected with
her voice, or any special claim to beauty.
“It’s just charm!” as Maggie says in J. M.
Barrie’s “What Every Woman Knows,” and
you’ve got to swallow it and ask no ques-
tions.
Ivy Schilling flashes short skirts of irides-
cent tangerine above a pair of substantial
legs in the Champagne Dance in “The
Quaker Girl.” In this smart sketch, Leslie
Holland, who has to catch the handsome
Schilling, earns his bread in the sweat of his
brow, these summer days, for the lady
passes through the air with the solid whir
of a Christmas hamper.
No fatalities have been recorded as the
result of the Manly water carnival on Sat-
urday night. This certificate to the patience
and good-humor of an Australian crowd,
when it finds itself in a tight corner, can
only be appreciated by those who safely
passed from the overburdened ferry boats to
the densely-packed Esplanade. At 5 p.m.
Manly was filling up to its last inch of kerb-
stone. Sydney seemed to be emptying its
human cqntents into the Corso. The over-
flow got away from the crush and dashed
into the surf, and the crowd on the beach
had considerably more than 4d. worth of
ecstatic enjoyment out of this item. At
seven o’clock there was breathing room only
on the Esplanade.
Thirty, thousand, or thereabouts, is the
semi-official estimate of the influx, and it is
probably under-stated. We all know what a
40,000 crowd looks like at a Test match.
Myriads of children swarmed over the edge
of the adult wave which surged from shore
to shore, and spidery bare legs, well tanned
by sun and wind, attached to small and
furiously-active bodies, crawled and twirled
over every pebble, boulder, or mere human
corn that could give a foothold. Many of
them were obviously Manly inhabitants,
these bare-headed, healthy little imps—a
sporting mixture of goats and grasshoppers
in their genius for balancing themselves in
the atmosphere. They were not a public
nuisance. They merely added to the gaiety
of tne Town of a Thousand Lights. “Who
owns this?” asked a voice in a herring-
barrel squeeze. “This” was a little human
male shrimp of three or so, tied up with a
large red belt. “Please, he belongs to me,”
answered an agitated treble. “Me” who was
quite five, collared her property, and wrig-
gled off, amid the 60,000 legs of the multi-
tude.
The chance to pay Is. each for breathing
space on a jetty, or 6d. apiece for chairs on
the Esplanade, was rushed by the early ar-
rivals. Next time Manly breaks out in
Chinese lanterns, its municipality ought to
steal 20,000 chairs, and rake in the same
number of sixpences. Lines of lanterns,
swung across the house fronts, a score or
two of decorative row boats, an illuminated
shark effigy emitting fearsome noises, a
band, also emitting loud noises, troubadours
under Mr. 11. Brewer, skimming across the
water, and the rather thin pipe of Manly’s
Venetian choir, anchored to a heavy-weight
pontoon, went towards the making of a good-
looking carnival.
Aid. C. D. Paterson, W. Heaton and Adam
Ogilvy pushed this evening party into the
public eye, with strenuous help from Mr. K.
V. Holmes, Mr. Shemwell and Mr. D. Hogan.
A house decoration committee (Mr. V.
Green, hon. sec.), a water trimming con-
spiracy (Mr. W. H. Walker, hon. sec.), and
a programme and collection squadron (almost
everybody more or less hon. sec.), organ-
ised minpr details. Why the ferry boats,
packed to the last inch of standing room on
their upper decks, didn’t sit down suddenly
on their sides, when there was a rush for
the illuminated view area, is only one among
the many miracles of the Manly water
escape.
When a Saturday afternoon crowd swal-
lows ozone at Bondi or Manly, it: generally
parts in the middle, or some other convenient
portion of its anatomy. Part of it stands
on the wet sand, where the creeping tide laps
the legs of the small fry, and the wooden'
buckets of infantine visitors wobble on the
shallow water. The rest of it gathers to-
gether in the surf, or fills itself with sand-
wiches and week-end pastry. The breaker
ladies who looked most shipshape at Manly
the other afternoon, were neatly strapped in
at the waist with leather belts, and closely
upholstered in their lower limbs with black
stockings and sandal shoes. The loosely-
made type of woman, with mountain-
ous curves, is sometimes rashly importunate
in the matter of her choice of sea frills.
Like a Greek chorus—but quieter in its re-
frain —the crowd (chiefly sac suit and straw
hat) flung carefully edited comments along
the line of dripping mermaids who skipped
the yellow sands on the track to the dress-
ing sheds on Saturday. The red kerchief
cap, with ears stuck up in front, is the
surf belle’s best-looking head-gear, when
she throws herself into the arms of a Manly
breaker.
Ethel Irving, in a stage box at a matinee
the other day, looked very weary after the
sobbing emotions of “Dame Nature” on the
previous night. She is rather thin and or-
dinary, as one sees her out of the collar, and
she consistently turned the back of a tired
black feather toque to the audience, while
she rested from her weeping. Ethel Irving’s
success as an actress —the most perfect in
her special line of feminine character
sketches this town has seen for many years
—is a triumph of mind over matter.
The Crown-street Women’s Hospital,
which is hanging out signals of distress in
the matter of ward space, will hold its an-
nual general meeting on Monday afternoon,
January 29. Visitors will be allowed to
patrol the premises, which palpitate with
the pressure of sick humanity, from 2.30
p.m. on that day.
In a lean week, chiefly filled with nor'-
easter and the far-off swish of the Asiatic
invasion, an English magazine, with an al-
leged Australian story, fell across my path.
The writer evidently knows his subject—as
they know Australia who live in Bloomsbury
or St. Paul’s Churchyard. “A Bushranger’s
Daughter” shows internal evidences of some
study of the Kelly Gang, or other light lit-
erature of our continent. “Bushranging
ran in her family In those days (1850),
in Australia, a chance word, or a vague
hint, and a man might swing for it The
most innocent persons slept with a pistol
under their pillows,” etc. The heroine, by
the way, who is a Thames Embankment re-
flection of Kate Kelly, rides a thoroughbred
horse back and forwards to the bushranger’s
eyrie in the “Strathgoolong Ranges” (pos-
sibly “Strathbogie”) at midnight, as a regu-
lar pastime. She finally jumps a chasm
which the artist has obligingly placed in an
English forest. “Black men,” and a hope-
lessly imbecile police force, cive this story,
apparently by an inexperienced pen, the
“tang” which the untravelled Londoner
firmly believes is Australian life.
As a pioneer, Parson Sam Marsden was a
member of the scanty settlement in
N.S.W., when the nineteenth century was
young. Like those of much greater men,
Marsden’s life story is a bone which con-
tinues to be picked with some acrimony. In
the S. M. Herald, the other day, some let-
ters written by Marsden (1790-1814), re
his sheep and other live-stock ventures, ap-
peared in connection with one of the interm-
inable controversies that still rage round the
farmer-parson’s rather piebald character.
They travelled from Farsley (near Leeds,
England), but are quite familiar here. I
fancy all or most of these letters appear in
Hassall’s reminiscences. Whether Mars-
den bred sheep for patriotism or profit (or
for both excellent reasons) was less interest-
ing to the assigned servants of his time than
his fixed idea that the devil could be flogged
out of a man. Provided the man was in that
station of life which made him floggable,
the fierce old preacher always assumed that
the devil was in him, and that it was his
duty to get it out. A first-class Inquisition
was lost through Marsden being born out of
due date.
May your shadow grow less, Arini, for the
days of the plump are full of sorrow, and
the slim sihouette reigns in the land.
