MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, January 22, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
State Governor Fuller did the opening of
the Premiers’ Conference, at State Parlia-
ment House, on Wednesday. His frock coat
was decorated with a red rose, -and lie wore a
solitary gold-laced Gentleman Help behind
his chair. His speech was quite fierce in
spots. One spot was where he got this off:
“There is, perhaps, no more difficult Consti-
tution to work in the civilised world than the
Constitution under which we live in Aus-
tralia.” Which sounded rather like the wail
of one overshadowed. However, he ex-
pressed the hope that the wrangles about the
unification of the railway gauge would
bring forth fruit; also, that the Murray
waters would not be allowed to pour them-
selves into the sea much longer. While the
speakers droned and stumbled, the women
aloft' (who had been invited to the tea-party)
yawned and criticised hats and heads. The
badly-needed refreshments were handed
round in the members’ dining rooms. Mrs.
John Murray, in white cloth, acted as
hostess. Lady Fuller, who had motored
down from Macedon. wore crffpe de Chine.
Auntie Phipps was in yellow, to match the
petrol waggon, in wnich they sprinted home
again immediately the party was over. The
Gaud Mayoress, with horseshoes on her hat,
hurried away at the same time to an oyster
opening. Byron Moore watched the proceed-
ings in company with a daughter who had on
a matronly hat. Mrs. Secretary Harkness,
from Sydney, was etched in smart black and
white stripes. Mrs. Holman, another Sydney-
sider, hung a brocade chair cover round hex-
plump shoulders. Mrs. Shields, looking very
impressive in black, had her daughter with
hex-. Mrs. Pearce, who has a strong leaning
towards politics, invaded the crow-like groups
of men garbed daintily in blue. Informal
plans were made in all corners for the enter-
tainment of the visitors. Next evening the
men-folk dined at Parliament Plouse with
Fuller, and buttressed the sacred (I nearly
wrote “scared”) cause of State Frights witlx
much good food.
The return trip of the Linlithgow pair
through Yankeeland does not seem to have
benefited the lady. She has never been able
to travel beyond Liverpool since crossing the
Atlantic. The bouquets which were to have
been thrown at the newly-married peer at
the belated coming-of-age celebrations wilted
long ago, and the joyous uproar of the ten-
antry has been postponed until the Mar-
chioness is herself again.
The Campbells, of Murray Downs, have
had no less than three deaths in five months.
The first to go was the grand old dame, Mrs.
Charlie. She was followed very soon by son-
in-law Johnstone, who squatted near Bal-
larat. This week comes the sad news that his
widow (she was Mary Campbell before her
mai-i-iage) has also passed away. Two
young girleens survive their parents, and
will have money to burn.
Kathleen Oliver, who was one of the shin-
ing lights amongst the scientific research
pupils of the University, has taken up the
study of domestic economy. The tall, clever
girl was quietly married last week in Sydney
to a former fellow-student. Most of the
bride’s family expressed to the Ma State for
the wedding. The pair are looking happily
forward to a life on the land in Westi-alia,
where they have taken up a tract.
Nowadays every society miss yearns to be
able to “shove” her own motor; and the driv-
ing tests seem to grow daily more severe.
One majestic matron, whose 18-year-old is
qualifying for her driver’s license, was horri-
fied on hearing that the law specifies a drive
through the city with a policeman. “My
daughter driving with a policeman! The
idea!” Still policemen are very steady and
reliable.
Wally Moore, a gilded young man of this
city, is at present suffering from a fine col-
lection of bruises. He and a chum took
Dad’s finest motor for an evening spin. They
collided with a cab, smashed the motor, ac-
quired black eyes and blue and black patches,
and had their lovely clothes cut to pieces.
They extricated themselves, and took refuge,
at two in the morning, with a sympathetic
grandmother, who bound up their wounds
and cheered them on to the inevitable inter-
view with Dad.
Barrister Bonner has come up from his
Portsea cottage a rejuvenated man. A
severe operation put him out of horsehair
for a considerable time.
The two John Highett daughters had the
time of their lives at the Dux-bar celebra-
tions. One of their diversions was a state
promenade on the roof of an elephant, whose
immense countenance had been either
painted red, or was flushed as a result of
drink.
Mrs. Edwin Miller (sister of the Bowes
Kelly lady) walked down the gangway of the
mail steamer the other day, with her orphan
niece, Belle White, who was her companion
during the sojourn in Fogland. A detach-
ment of Kellys rushed over from the Fly-
speck to fall on the returned wanderers’
necks.
Kathleen Fitzgerald, daughter of the late
Surgeon Thomas, arrived home from her
Durbar trip by the Macedonia. She will
spend a busy time and much money collect-
ing happy rags for her bridal outfit. As Mrs.
Colonel Douglas, her future address will be
somewhere in India.
Miss Anne Payne, the octogenarian aunt
of M.L.C. Payne and his brothers, died at
Tooralc last week. She was an invalid for
the last few years of her long life.
Alan Newton, the youthful medico son of
Clerk-of-the-Assembly Newton, has been
taken on as lieutenant by Surgeon Fred
Bird.
Squatter Alan Currie, who married the
youngest daughter of the Albert Miller pair,
managed to let his motor-car turn turtle the
other day, whilst taking the air in the West-
ern district. All save a Grice niece, who
sustained slight concussion, emerged from
the smash unhurt.
Barrister Bill Furlonge, a cousin of Ted
Mitchell, K.C., spent the Christmas holidays
on the sick list. He only managed to strug-
gle off the painful document a few days ago.
Furlonge is remarkaple for a marvellous
memory. Also, he is remarkable for having
once been sternly reprimanded by the Wax-
cellency for addressing a female in court as
“My good woman.”
Rita Kiddle, back aeain in Melbourne, is
wearing black for her uncle, Lewis Kiddle,
who died the other day. She is getting full
accounts from her numerous friends of the
number and bitterness of the tears they shed
when the report of her death travelled out
from London.
The newly-acquired -wife of Solicitor J. C.
Stewart has quite tamed her new motor.
She is a qualified chaffeuse, and is licensed to
scatter pedestrians on her own.
The timber-yard Moores, who dwell in the
loftiest of St. Kilda-road palaces, are packing
their boxes for a March flitting. Papa,
mamma and the boys intend to make a two-
years orbit round the earth.
When people sell off, why do they leave
signed portraits of their friends in the silver
frames? At an auctioneer’s orgie, the other
day, I noticed a lady called “Maud” who had
been left for the highest bidder. The habit
or vice of presenting signed photographs will
die out if the ungraceful habit continues.
Paymaster-in-Chief Mannisty, who came
from Fogland, last summer, to join our
young navy, has quietly carried off a Sydney
bride —Miss Flo McCathie, of Pymble. The
pair are now honeymooning at the George
Hotel, St. Kilda, and hunting for. a Toorak
house in which to dump their elegant goods
and chattels. The bride has an enviable col-
lection of house linen. One piece is a won-
derful tablecloth covered with Australian
flowers and birds. The wedding was a quiet
seven o’clock affair, and happened in the
Pymble house. The bride wore a filmy din-
ner dress of rose point lace, and a family
party of about 20 subsequently sat down to
supper in a room decorated with pink carna-
tions and roses.
St. Kilda beach rejoices again in its ama-
teur Lady Godiva. The damsel is shapely,
young and fair, and she arrives early on her
horse, clad only in a bathing dress and a
long cloak, which is flung off at the water’s
edge. The shapely one then dashes into the
sea, until only the head of her mount is visi-
ble. After a good swim, she heads the steed
shorewards, and gallops up and down the
strand. When she is going full speed ahead,
with her hair streaming in the breeze, she
makes one of the best moving pictures on the
market.
The latest matrimonial engagement sig-
nalled is that of Carl Gillespie, of Northern
Westralia, to Myrtle Dillon, of Melbourne.
The fiancee is the first chick of the Dillon,
w-ho, with partner Burrows, made much
money out of lollies. Mrs. Dillon was one of
Melbourne’s belles years ago. She is a sister
of Dr. Moloney. Daughter Myrtle, who is
of the petite type, has just returned from a
scurry abroad. The fiance, who has his share
of nice looks, is the eldest son of Miller
James Gillespie, who has dropped grain for
sheep-breeding in the West.
The latest notable blue-blooded bankrupt,
“Captain the Hon. Gerald Oakley Cadogan,
Viscount Chelsea” (as the Cold Country
press hath it), offspring of Earl Cadogan,
did the thing on epic lines. For a long time,
Chelsea was a younger son, with only a
beggarly £7OO per annum on which to make
both ends meet. So he didn’t try to make
them meet, and his noble dad had to separate
himself from several thousands of pounds to
silence the howls of the creditors. In August,
1910, Chelsea succeeded his elder brother as
heir, and got a rise to £2500 a year. His
household expenses since June, 1910, are
set down by himself at £10,083. In addition,
he paid away £12,000 as interest on borrowed
coin; and “presents to friends and relatives”
cost him £lO,OOO. Of £lO,OOO which he shov-
elled into the jeweller’s hands, he explains
that £4500 represented goods given to Lady
Chelsea; the rest, presumably, was presented
to the aforementioned “relatives and friends.”
The gifts to friends can be understood. But
why should a man set fire to his cheque book
for relatives? Most of us would rather set
fire to the relatives.
Explorer Karl Kumm gave his wife’s
bridesmaids gold-mounted shark’s teeth. The
chewing apparatus of the sea scourge is a
peculiar—not to say, gruesome —wedding
gift. A lawyer bridegroom, of course, could
say that they were a souvenir of a deceased
brother.
The sale of the Ernest Brookes house-
gods provided some excitement. for society
and others last week. A pathetic feature of
the dispersal was the glumness of the Scotch
gardener, who saw his carefully-tended
nurslings divided up into lots for the. sacri-
fice. Camelot was thrown open for inspec-
tion days before the sale, and the cars of the
petrol classes stood before the gate for hours.
The tram-car orders rolled up in loads all
day long. The more expensive people have a
habit of attending these sales decked out in
their best chiffons —as though the passing of
the stylish portals, even on a purely mercen-
ap’ mission, demanded fete dress. The fur-
nishings w T ere French. A great clothes-
press, like a small house, of exquisitely in-
laid wood, was pronounced by an excited
female to be “Lou-iss Quince.”
In the Winter Garden, on Saturday night,
30 red-coats dined enthusiastically amongst
scarlet flowers, blood-red ribbons and much
bunting. It was “the Sixth” giving a send-off
to Colonel Courtney, who is departing to
Fogland on a work-and-play trip. Rupert
Bunny departs this month for the
same region, with his pocket book nicely
upholstered and a lighter sketch-portfolio.
Captain Nutting, whose dad visits these
shores to see how his small son is get-
ting on, can easily distance his brother aides
on the Denman staff with three distinct
brands of dog. The first is his yapping Aus-
tralian terrier; the second is his large pri-
vate car; and the third is what he puts on
for special occasions.
Someone with a keen sense of rumor says
that the debonnair Sep. Miller has settled
£15,000 on Muriel Henderson, his prospective
bride. The wedding, which is now almost
within hail, will be bridesmaided by Henry
Madden’s daughter.
The Tom Fitzgeralds have sold the Ber-
wick acres and baa lambs, and are coming
to live in suburban Oakleigh, which place is
handier than Berwick to the Royal Golf
Links.
The station folk are now rolling through
town on the way to the seaside. They don’t
shed any Bathurst burrs in the process. The
Melbournian notices a few strange cars
about the streets, and encounters smartly-
dressed strangers in the teashops—that is
all. The sheepy women are mostly pleasing
athletic figures, in good linen or tussore
tailorings, while the kiddies are very spruce
about the hair, and mostly wear bare legs
and sandals.
A rich but wary bachelor, who has been
the despair of marriage-making mothers for
many seasons, has been observed throwing
tender glances towards a widow. Unat-
tached girls moan at the unfairness of it.
The widow has had her allowance of hus-
band, and is wealthy into the bargain, whilst
the moaning ones have neither cash in hand
nor a money-maker in view.
One day last week, Gaud Mayor Davey,
accompanied by his Gaudess, performed the
formal “opening” of a nook in the Fitzroy
Gardens, where a new lakelet and lawn have
lately appeared under the direction of Guil-
foyle. The spot has been open for years, and,
to the naked eye, doesn’t look more open
than it was. Possibly the sleeper-out, in the
waste hours of the night, notices an added
ferocity in the wind that blows over the
“opened” spot; but no difference is apparent
by day. By the way, was there ever an
official opening of the sea by a Gaud Mayor?
If there was not, the open sea must still be
shut —at least, so far as the Best People are
concerned.
A good deal of bad luck has pursued the
crew of the drunken vessel in which Sinbad
set sail in search of the Valley of Diamonds.
First, there was the death of skipper Frank
Lincoln. Now Nell Finnis, the maiden with
the picture post-card teeth, who used to look
such a neat figure in a middy’s uniform, has
had to desert the vessel owing to nervous
breakdown, and will be docked in a private
hospital for a couple of weeks, at least. Her
principal-girl part has been handed over to
Miss Mary Morris Smith, the sweet-faced
Australian damsel who last year annexed
the first prize in West’s Beauty Competi-
tion. Emperor Fred Winn was also out of
the cast for a space last week owing to a
troublesome throat.
Comedian Bert Bailey, who is floating out
as “Bert Bailey, Limited,” has been stalking
Gregan McMahon’s Repertory people to fill
some gaps in his company. The new mimes
start business in March, at Sydney Palace.
Bailey has dramatised Steele Rudd’s “On
Our Selection,” and *Dad is to rage before
the footlights ere long.
Ada Crossley and Clara Butt are genuine
pals. Recently, when Ada was steaming
home from South Africa, her ship passed that
containing Clara and husband Rumford, then
on their way towards African audiences. The
pair exchanged wireless endearments. For
once, on meeting, they were unable to ex-
change and try on hats. When ladies can try
on hats by wire, science may consider that it
has really arrived.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
An echo of the days when we were war-mad, and
wore red-white-and-blue straws in our hair, comes in
the death of Bugler, at Angaston. The old horse was
given by Mr. J. C. F. Johnson to raise funds for the
war, and was auctioned over and over again by the
late Theodore Bruce —a champion in the hammer-
wielding line. “Abe” Shannon topped the list of
buyers at 100 guineas, and when we stopped shrieking
“Rule Britannia,” the noble steed, who by this time
looked as if he were going to bolt or founder or some-
thing, was permanently handed over and passed on to
Angaston.
Mr. W. H. ‘Squires, who died the other day in
England, and left a matter of £16,000 behind him,
was at one time in the Civil Service of this State.
His wife was one of the numerous Hughes clan.
This week sees a vast female exodus to Tasmania.
Some are going for purely frivolous reasons, others to
attend a Women’s Conference. In the .first category
is Miss Isabel Young, who teaches artistic dancing,
while Mrs. Makin and Mrs. Davies Thomas are among
those who have sterner business on hand.
The outgoing mail took away three members of
the Squires family—a small boy and girl who are to
be deposited at school, and an elder sister, Margaret,
who will keep an eye on her juniors during the
voyage.
Saturday’s cricket tragedy was made more awful
by a heat wave. Let us draw a veil or an umbrella
over the painful subject, and bury it sadly in the
freezing chamber.
Miss Maud Puddy, Mus. Bac., was to have given
a concert, in Vienna, early this month. She carries
our warmest wishes with her. Another S. Aus-
tralian who’s doing us proud is Miss Bessie Tyas.
Her father used to “Profess” at our University. She
sang at a concert at the iEolian Hall, with Mr.
Vernon Warner as a co-“star,” and the London
papers say nice things about her. Leila Doubledav’s
concert was also a big success. In fact, in a musical
manner of speech, we are keeping our end up.
Skuthorpe’s Buckjumpers betook themselves to the
Exhibition Oval on Saturday, and bucked and lassoed
like anything. As usual, one of the most absorbing
items was unrehearsed. The management had offered
a prize of a gold bracelet to the winner of an open
ladies’ race. Frenzied with enthusiasm, one of the
contestants laid detaining hands on a competitor
immediately ahead, and a smart bout, wherein hair
and hairpins flew like curses in a State Parliament,
ensued. The bloodshed was stopped ultimately by the
Law.
Strathalbyn won the polo match, at Mount Craw-
ford, last week, and so got well-nigh within grab-
bing distance of the Richard Smith Challenge Cup.
The Anal match (between Adelaide and Strathalbyn)
will be played at Plympton next week. The polo
world is chortling in that a number of young players
—Murrays and Angases and Stirlings and so forth —•
are coming on. The Time is thoroughly ripe for
such a happening, since many of the present-day
members of the teams are more fitted, physically, for
elephant-steering than for polo-plaving.
Obit on Wednesday, Miss Alice Morgan, daughter
of the late William Morgan, Knight, and sister of
Mrs. Arthur Waterhouse. Her mother died some two
years ago.
Dr. Bennett, who’s back from honeymooning in
Maoriland, filled in some of his time inquiring into
racing matters there. lie also gave evidence before
the N. S. Wales Totalisator Commission, and bought
half a dozen new horses.
As I write, another heat-wave is surging down on
this devoted hamlet. My thoughts stray from social
matters, and turn yearningly, insistently, to the sub-
ject of cold drinks. Fare thee well.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

POLITICAL
POINTS
THE MAN WHO ALWAYS MISSED
THE ’BUS.
“It seems that platforms were made merely to get
in by—not to remain on afterwards.” —Joe Cook,
sarcastically, on political promises.
And if some kind power had only recognised
the purpose of Joseph’s numerous little platforms
that now , alas! litter the political track , he
might have been permitted to board the car at
[?]i stcr e and enjoy a seat inside.
According to a Sydney evening paper:—
Liberal Ministers attend in a very resolute spirit.
They are going to fight, and fight hard against Federal
advancement. They will not yield one hair’s breadth.
They will not hear of the transcontinental railway
being built without the State’s bondholders being
consulted. According to the private arrangement
made some time ago between State Ministers, the
Federal line may be built as projected by the Fisher
Government, but the State Governments will not,
under present conditions, alter their lines to link up
with it. So that unless some compromise is arrived
at, the desert railway will finish at Port Augusta,
and there will have to be a break of gauge at that
place and another break in Melbourne before the half
circle can be made of the continent by rail.
Well, Port Augusta will be a big Federal
port when the railways from Perth and Port
Darwin meet there, especially if it has
short-line connections with Albany and Es-
perance Bay, and it will probably be able
to do without the recognition of “Liberal”
Premiers.
Private Enterprise is either a great ass or
a great prevaricator, and a “General Mer-
chant’s” recent statement to Melbourne
’Erald doesn’t settle the question. Accord-
ing to this yarn, the benevolent gentlemen
who import ales and stout are now
making no profit worth mentioning,
while the merchants who pass the
liquid goods on to hotels and grocers are
actually losing 3 per cent, on the value
of each transaction. Heaven knows just
why they commit this surprising folly—ap-
parently they must sell imported goods or
burst. The curious feature of the sad case
is that “General Merchant” seems to think
they deserve sympathy. And in a way they
do. Men who are prepared to lose money
rather than give their own country a job
can’t be ouite sane.
Inspired by the visit of the Jap training
ships to Sydney the S. M. Herald burst
forth on the need of “great national quali-
ties.” “What we really lack,” said the
Ghadbandish old rag, with as much smug-
ness as though it had never played the State
Fright game in its life, “is an infusion of
national qualities, and among the most ele-
mentary of them is a grasp of the serious-
ness of things, a fight against the enervation
of our easy circumstances, a determined
effort to achieve the nationhood of which we
boast sometimes without due reason.” By
way of inculcating “a grasp of the serious-
ness of things” the Herald published in the
same issue a large, polysyllabic leader on the
Adelaide .cricket match. The Telegraph
did likewise, and helped along our spirit of
nationhood by referring to the Australians
as “the colonial players.” The Common-
wealth will, no doubt, ultimately appreciate
that it _is no longer a colony, and that
cricket is not one of the serious things of
life where grown up people are concerned,
but the “Liberal” dailies are putting in some
fine work in the way of delaying that desir-
able consummation.
“Pressman”: Brave words in S. M. Her-
ald by one John Young, of Mosman, con-
cerning the proposal to resume Federal Gov-
ernment House, Sydney:—
Let another meeting be called early, at which I
hope to speak and. further in every way I can the
efforts of our influential men to place this important
matter on a basis satisfactory to the people. I shall
be glad to join again in another deputation to the
Government.
It was my lot to attend the last meeting of
protest against Holman’s alleged outrage.
It was one of the dreariest and most futile
affairs imaginable. Twenty-odd citizens,
mostly old ladies and deadbeats, sat uneasily
in one of the smaller
ante-chambers of the
Town Hall, while a
number of elderly
male Govern ment
House frequenters
harangued them pas-
sionately from behind
a rampart of deal
table and glass water
bottle. Viewed as a
dead failure, the in-
surrection was only
surpassed by a meet-
ing which occurred in
the same room, a
couple of years ago,
having for its
object the resuscita-
tion of Freetrade. It
is no use John Young,
or any other “Loyal-
ist,” clamoring . for
vast public meetings,
in regard to certain
questions. The pub-
lie is utterly* disinter-
ested, and, in consequence, it firmly declines
to meet.
“We are all Socialists in our saner
moments,” said Premier John Hurry, of
Victoria, the other day. Just so —all So-
cialists when we think, and Anti-Soshers
when we don’t; all Socialists when we are
not on the make, or contesting an election —
bar the cocky, who is only a Socialist when
he is on the make and wants low railway
freights, or free seed, or Government cool
stores, or an increased municipal subsidy.
THE HEARING OF NOISES IS THE
FIRST SYMPTOM OF INSANITY.
A hundred motor-cars buzz past, each one giving
forth some new variety of hideous sound. Boys with
hatefully shrill voices shriek race-cards with an ear-
piercing note. A traincar crossing an intersection
bangs its bell vilely for half a furlong before the
gripman's arm drops from sheer exhaustion.—Mel-
bourne Argus.
One of those people who gird at legisla-
tive interference with Private Enterprise
now wishes that the State had interfered
with him a little more strenuously. He
owns a weapon called a buzzer plane at his
Geelong factory, and the Factories’ officers
lately ordered him, among others, to have it
protected by a guard, for the safety of his
employees. He said a guard was quite un-
necessary, and he would sooner dismantle it
than hamper it by such a needless encum-
brance. He threw it out of gear for a
while, but, during the holidays, set it going
again, operating it himself. Before an hour
passed he was two fingers short. It will
have a vuard now.
The Yic. Government has at last informed
Melbourne Harbor Trust that its policy of
discriminating between goods shipped at
different ports is illegal. The Board’s only
reply is that it thought it was being patri-
otic, inasmuch as it charged least for Vic-
torian goods, and worked gradually up till
the farthest-out foreigner was hit five times
as hard. Probably the Trust had the best
intentions: the only drawback is that it
doesn’t happen to be its job to revise the
Customs tariff, or abrogate the Constitu-
tional principle of inter-State free trade.
For that matter, neither is it the Railway
Commissioners’ job; yet they go on doing
it, and the Commonwealth Inter-State Com-
mission—which the Constitution says “there
shall be,” on purpose to stop such tricks —
seems just as far off as ever.
That strange organisation, “The Society
of Free Workers,” held a meeting in Mel-
bourne the other night-, at which the secre-
tary complained of “the bitter and unwar-
ranted opposition” of some undiscerning
toilers, who were unable to see that the
objects of the society were similar to those
the unions aimed at. The only difference
was that the society meant to arrive “by
lawful means,” presumably by being affable
to everybody, no matter what happened.
Labor mostly gets what it wants by lawful
means; but now and then lawful means
break down just as badly as international
arbitration does. When that drear hour
strikes, _ the vague, shadowy society which
has arisen to share organised Labor’s loaf
will be free to drift out of the dispute. Still,
it is unable to understand why Labor re-
gards it with aversion. It didn’t help to get
the loaf, and it isn’t prepared to defend it,
but it is quite willing to share it.
“Locust” theorises:—
The question of how to dispose of N.S.W. Speaker
Willis now appears to have superseded the coon-fight
in the public mind. Willis, who started out to blaze
a very straight track, has got bushed, through over-
sensitiveness, or vanity, or something; and there is
a possibility that he may have to be moved. In
theory, the Assembly can pass a resolution deposing
the Speaker; but Willis has risen superior to all
theory up to now—as the Wade-Wood crowd knows
,to its cost, and he may decline to submit the motion.
The Ma State may then have a modern replica of the
scene in the Commons, in 1629, when Valentine and
Hollis and Speaker Lenthall in his chair, while they
read a motion which he had refused to put, and,
with locked doors, the key on the table of the House,
the “Black Rod” banging in vain for admittance,
the resolution was passed. If McGowen, on the
one side, and Wade, on the other, hold Willis down,
while a resolution deposing him is passed, a police-
man can do the rest —as the Speaker has proved.
But, m the first place, there is a lack of unanimity
about Wade and McGowen. In the second place, sup-
posing Willis lets out with both feet, and then
snatches the key and leaps for the door, he won’t
have nearly so many members to fight his way
through is in Lenthall’s case. Furthermore, Willis
may guess what is coming, and have the police ready.
Lenthall couldn’t. There were no police in his day.

series of experiments on the dogs it was found that

whilst in the hyjnan experiments the increase was

In the closing hours of the
Victorian Assembly Premier
Weary promised to consider
the grievances of the Vic. police.
A few of the things which the
general public reckons it is up
to John to do are (1) increase
the miserable pittance of the
men; (2) restore the pensions
which are now denied to the
younger men; (3) give a man
with genuine ability a chance
to rise at a quicker rate than a
stripe in 20 or 25 years; and
(4) remember that seven days
of work in the week is as bad
in the police force as in any
other line. There are other
needed reforms, but the above
would do to break the ice of
equity, so to speak.
Ex - Governor-General Dud-
ley has raised his voice in Eng-
land to the effect that seven
Legislatures of two Houses a-
piece are rather over the odds
for Australia with its popula-
tion of less than 5,000,000. It
is a very common remark, yet
Australia is in no way sur-
prising or unusual. Switzer-
land, for instance, with less
population than Australia and
less than the area of Tasmania,
has 23 Parliaments. Germany
has about 40; the United
States, 47; Canada, 10; Mex-
ico, 27; Brazil, Argentina, Ven-
ezuela and other Spanish-
American countries have each
got a heap. Australia, in fact,
has got a fairly large—though
not unusually large—stock of Legislatures
for its population, but for its size it has, so
few that they are hardly worth mentioning.
Suggested name for Percy Hunter’s new
scheme to further bloat Sydney—-the Mill-
stone Club.
THE DANGER OF
DEFICIENT BLOOD.
The System Lacks its Chief
Defence Against Disease,
Dr, Williams' Pink Pills have been
found a Sure and Speedy Means
of Replenishing the Blood Supply.
The gravest danger of Anaemia is the
fact that the system’s chief defence against
disease is broken down. Thin impure blood
is virtually an irritation to disease. The
blood works night and day to maintain the
health and strength of the body. Any de-
ficiency in the blood supply weakens na-
ture’s defence against disease. Anaemia is
the doctor’s name for lack of blood. Its
chief symptoms are paleness and lack of
energy. It attacks young girls; those re-
covering from acute illnesses, such as fevers,
and persons of advanced years. The only
cure for Anaemia is an increased blood
supply. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have been
found to increase the blood supply of
Anaemic people in an astonishing way.
After using them for a time, sufferers have
found the most distressing Anaemic symptoms
give place to red cheeks, good appetite,
strength and energy. The following case is
a typical example :
“I was going to business, and I think the
close confinement did not agree with my
health,” said Mrs. Florence Mackin, 64
Kent Street, North Richmond, Melbourne.
“Anyhow my strength began to fade, and m,y
blood turned more watery every day. When
I looked in the glass I noticed how white
my lips and gums were getting, and in my
fg.ce every bit of color faded. I began to
lose all taste for my food unless I could get
hold of anything acid or sharp tasting.
Every day I got more tired and languid. It
was as much as I could do to drag about,
especially on hot days. My head would
ache in the temples, just as if a hammer were
beating it, and when these attacks came I
could not bear to be bothered with any-
thing, the throbbing was so persistent. If I
went for a walk my heart would start beat-
ing twice as quick as it ought to, and I
would fairly lose my breath if I hurried,
and now and again I would get dizzy, faint
sort of turns, especially if I stooped. A
trial of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills gave every ■
satisfaction. After a while I could not help
noticing my blood was getting richer, for I
began to lose the waxy look and get color in
the gums and lips. My appetite began to
steadily improve, and my strength to return
till I felt thoroughly built up.”
It is well to remember that it was Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills, and not an imitation,
that cured Mrs. Mackin. Getting the
genuine is an important thing in a cure.
They are sold by most chemists and store-
keepers, or will be sent direct by the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia Ltd.,
Sydney, on receipt of price, 2s. 9d. per box,
six boxes 15s. 3d.
A SUBLIME SACRIFICE!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sir,—You correctly designate Federal Government
House as the “national guest chamber.” Constitu-
tionally, morally, and by prescriptive right, the place
is as much a residence of the King as Windsor
Castle, Balmoral Castle, or Dublin Castle... .Icono-
clasts have decided that this pleasantly-placed man-
sion shall no longer be an emblem of Monarchy.
They have, in effect, given the King notice....
It cannot be that they are going to allow this
great act of repudiation done in their name to pass;
to allow a handful of Goths to take from Csesar that
which is Caesar’s.... If so, then fidelity is dead, and
there is no good left in us. But this cannot be.
Let us believe that the torpor at present exhibited
is but the deep silence preeursing a storm. — J. Laker
Macmillan, in Sydney Telegraph.
What horror and terror hang over the Nation!
We welter in treason and tergiversation!
We writhe in the grip of some murderous villain!
And who is it warns us? Why Mr. Macmillan! .
Oh, speak not of Curtins, who perished untimely—
Of Arnold von Winkelried, dying sublimely—
Of Byron’s immortal, lone prisoner of C' illon —
Their woes are outdone by the griefs of Macmillan.
The wrath of the gods, and the terror of slaughter—
Heroic endurance through fire and through water—
These things have been faced in Humanity’s story;
Then, let us refuse not Macmillan due glory!
Australia! My country! Your path is uncertain,
And wars are approaching that you may be hurt in;
But, always remember, though foes should enslave
thee,
Macmillan at one time rushed forward to save thee!
When insolent persons, whose names do not matter,
Showed rudeness and treason —and chiefly the
latter —
Remember, Australia, the parties who scorned you
Were promptly denounced by the great man who
warned you!
When George and his Person and Throne were in
danger;
And, homeless, he wandered, an outcast and
stranger—
When Csesar was robbed and deprived of his coin,
say,
By the Goths and the Vandals of Glebe and Drum-
moyne, say!—
When, heedless of Windsor, Balmoral and Dublin —■
Those insolent persons with treason were bubblin’,
And squashing our grand Constitution with unction
By vilely depriving the King of his function:—
When rogues adventitious and weirdly fantastic
Brought shame to thy name by acts iconoclastic,
And outrage and infamy heaped o’er and o’er thee,
Who, then —O my country ! Who strove to restore
thee?
A curse, or a blight, such as falls on pertaters,
Afflict, and pursue, and destroy all low traitors!
But here’s to the triplice that none may dissever
The King and Macmillan and Bunkum for ever!
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.

GENTLEMAN JACK.
By DON DELANEY.
Illustrated. Price l/=, Post Free 1/1.
Bushranging- for revenge, not profit, is the keynote of “Gentleman Jack,” and those who are in
search of startling episodes and SDlendid situations should peruse this veritable romance of bush-
rangmg. “Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde” is not one whit more remarkable than this tale of the man
who js bushranger and lover by turns, and who creates that air of mystery throughout that is so dear
to the reader’s heart.
Altogether, a rattling story, apart from the fact that the doings of the notorious Ben Hall gang
are woven in with the main plot, and lend it a keen note of tragedy.
A book one is likely to read from cover to cover before leaving off.

The Outlaws ofWeddin Range
By AMBROSE PRATT. Illustrated by Lionel Lindsay.
Price 1/-, Post Free 1/1.
Mr. Ambrose Pratt’s work is so well and favorably known to our
Australian readers that there is no need to enlarge on it. In this new
book he very graphically describes the doings of the Ben Hall Gang of
Bushrangers, and goes to some trouble to give the true ring of authen-
ticity to his work. The many stirring episodes in Ben Hall’s life are
depicted with such skill, that while we are reading actual historical
happenings, the whole thing has an air of romance that some might
say was worthy of a better cause. Some hitherto unpublished parts
throw a new light on Hall’s character, and show how in those days
there were many police-made criminals.
Decidedly this is a book worth buying and keeping.
STELLA SOTHERN,
A. Tale of the Bush and Bohemia.
By IVAN ARCHER ROSENBLUM.
Illustrated by Harry Julius.
Price l/», Post Free 1/1.
The Bush and Bohemia to most minds are social extremes, and often mean innocence and the
opposite, and Stella Sothern’s experiences in both spheres are worth knowing. Mr. Rosenblum speaks
of things as he has seen them, and no one, after reading this book, will say he is far wrong. The
work is cleverly done, and is sure to bring the author into further prominence amongst our Austra-
lian writers of fiction.
But “Stella Sothern” is not all fiction ; rather it is fiction founded upon fact, and emphasises the
old adage that “truth is stranger than fiction.” The scenes in Bohemia—-Australian Bohemia at that
—-are quite unique, and should arouse a lot of intern t.
DAN KELLY—OUTLAW.
Being the Memoirs of Daniel
Kelly (brother of. Ned Kelly, leader
of the Kelly Gang). Edited by Ambrose Pratt. With four full-
Price Is., Postage id.
page illustrations by Percy Spence.
In the whole history of Australian Bushranging no gang was more notorious than that captained
by Ned Kelly. Various accounts of the doings of this gang have appeared, but none bears the hall-
mark of truth so pointedly as these leaves from a dead' man’s diary, as edited by Mr. Pratt, who has
presented the story so convincingly that, for tense interest and exciting events, it has few, if any,
equals.
Dan Kelly was the only member of the gang who escaped the clutches of the law, and his account
of the last stand at Glenrowan is a veritable human document.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The war-like declaration of President
McElhone, of the Board of Control, was delivered
with the object of forcing the vague voices which
were said to represent the feelings of “the players”
to materialise into personalities. It had its effect.
After a little hesitation, Clem Hill, Trumper, Arm-
strong, Ransford, Cotter and Carter scrambled out
from behind the newspaper fence, and demanded that
the Board of Control should back down, eat humble
pie, and abrogate its decision to keep absolute con-
trol of the 1912 tour to England. They did it, in a
long-winded and rambling letter which suggests that
the Six are a little worse at letter-writing than they
are at Test cricket on recent form. They demand to
be allowed to appoint one of the players as manager,
and that he shall be really manager. The Board may
send a man to look after its interests, but he must
be paid by the Board, and stand on the door-mat
with his hat in his hand, and take what information
the haughtv player-manager likes to throw his way.
But, most important of all, his salary and exes, must
not be charged against the takings of the tour. Not
one sixpence that might get into the pockets of the
players must be diverted on his account. And if the
Board doesn’t climb down, then the Six won’t go to
England.
There is only one answer. The names of the Six
must be eliminated from the list of possibles. With
the exception of Carter, not one of them will be
missed, on recent form. The colts, in the State
matches, made an infinitely better showing against
the English bowling than did the veterans in the
Test matches. Why these gentry should have got
themselves dubbed as “certainties” in the anonymous
campaign is hard to understand, except on the theory
that they are suffering from enlargement of the hat,
and really believe in their conceit that they cannot
be done without. As a matter of solemn fact, the
letter of the Six has cleared the air a lot. It has
made it possible to drop, without any signs of
cruelty, a number of gentlemen of somewhat avari-
cious nature who, on recent Test form, have fallen
from the rank of players to that of has-beens. As
the Greeks said. “They whom the gods would destroy
they first make mad.” Perhaps they had the same
sort of trouble before the Test match with Persia.
* * * *
Australia went down with an awful
crash in the third Test at Adelaide, and yet many
people heaved a sigh of relief that it was not as
bad as it promised to be. The Australian XI fell
like a crowd of schoolboys for 133 in the first in-
nings; and then the M.C.C. jumped at the Aus-
tralian bowling, and smote it to the horizon for
501. The Australian XI pulled itself together a
little, however, in the second attempt, and managed
to lift 476, leaving the English only 109 to get to
win The deficit was wipeu out by the accumula-
tion of 112 for the loss of three wickets. This
seven-wicket victory gave England two out of the
three Tests that have been played, and “the Ashes”
are in grave danger.
In the whole three of the Tests Clem Hill won
the toss, and thus had his choice throughout the
series, so the downfalls at Melbourne and Adelaide
cannot be charged to fate. The bowling records of
the third Test should be framed and hung in every
Association room for a warning how not to do it.
Cotter, five wickets for 146 (and he’s talking
strike!); Hordern, three for 175; Kelleway, two
for 54; Matthews, one for 96. Australian cricket
high-priests should set forth on the quest of the (
Holy Bowler! Immediately the XI had fallen a
hoarse roar for the return of M. A. Noble went up,
but it soon fell flat. Noble retired from representa-
tive cricket after getting badly hurt in the previous
bumping match with the Board of Control. A more
sensible idea would be to give some of the promis-
ing colts a show, and there is greater chance of
bright young players flashing into wondrous doings
than of retired veterans coming back at the age ot
37 and saving the country. As far as the
third Test is concerned, Australia apparently wants
a new team, lock, stock and barrel; but, speaking
by and large, it is the bowling that is weak; and
until the selection committee can unearth some
mighty trundlers, there doesn’t seem much chance ot
pulling the rubber out of the fire.
* * * *
“Adelaide”; Without going too much by
a single match, and without whipping the cat m any
way, it looks as if Australia hasn’t got any bowlers
worth relying on. Whitty did the work last year,
and, apparently, his appendicitis operation has dam-
aged him. Hordern seems to be dangerous only
while unknown; since the first Test he has been
played without much difficulty. Cotter may always
take some wickets —expensively, like all last
bowlers. Armstrong is no longer feared, and the rest
in sight are in the “useful” class. England s last
three totals speak for themselves: 219 for two wic-
kets; 501 and 112 for three wickets. On English
turf any of our bowlers may do well, but there are
two more Tests here to face. Writer would like to
see Hazlitt and Macartney go in. The lest m Ade-
laide was lost on the first day. It was about the
slowest day’s batting on record. It was also Aus-
tralia’s worst day for years. May there not be some
connection between those two facts? Anyway, that
day’s form was all wrong, and may be disregarded.
We have any quantity of batting, while England has
a distinct tail. All we can hope is to worry them
all out somehow — and hit up more runs than they do.
* * * *
An amazing statistician, with Barnes on
the brain, has supplied Melbourne Argus with a
table showing the remarkable influence ot Barnes in
all matches he has played in against the members ot
the Australian team engaged in the Third lest, in
elusive of the first innings of the match Irom
this laborious compilation it appears that Clem Hill,
Ransford, Bardsley and Trumper have been very sus-
ceptible to Barnes, owing their aggregate downfall
to his bowling 32 times out of 70. On the other
hand, Armstrong, Cotter, Carter, Kelleway and Hor-
dern have rarely been slain by Barnes possibly be
cause they lost their wickets before he could get at
them, or perhaps because they have nerves of a ch -
ferent sort, and one man’s Barnes is another man
poison. The blessed Barnes table boils down to
this: Barnes had taken 33£ p.c. ot the wickets (not
counting run outs) ; which boils down to the further
fact that the other English bowlers, as a rule, have
not been sufficiently deadly to prevent Barnes having
all these wickets to take. Either Barnes is a lot more
deadly than the other bowlers, or the other bowlers
are a lot less deadly than Barnes. You can read it
either way.
* * * *
“D.”: I hewed this out of an evening
paper last night: “Owing to his injured arm, Hor-
dern was unable to attend his business to-day. There
is some doubt about his playing in the Fourth Test.
He will give the selectors a definite decision in a
few days. Victor Trumper, who was injured in the
Third Test at Adelaide, has not yet returned to Syd-
ney. He is resting in Melbourne. There is no like-
lihood of his playing in next Friday’s inter-State
match on the Sydney Cricket Ground. Macartney
has now recovered from the injury to his head, and
Warren Bardsley’s injured ribs are giving him little
trouble.” And Ransford is a fifth cripple. Things
I would like to know are: (1) Can anyone cite me
five champion boxers who have been physically in-
capacitated in the last three months as a result of
following their profession? (2) Are the Wowsers
going to start a campaign against the grand old Eng-
lish sport of cricket, on the score of its dangers to
life and limb ; and if not, why not ?
* * * *
S. Aus. tried two lads in its last Sheffield
Shield match —Steele, captain of Prince Alfred Col-
lege, and Murray, captain of St. Peter’s team last
season. Steele’s average last season in B grade was
134.4, including one 231 not out. Murray (average,
over 50), is a tall fellow, standing nearly 6ft. 3m.,
and he makes use of his height. Against N.S.W. he
scored 48 and 36, and S.A. has visions of another
champion. He is a useful bowler, too, but did not
find his length against N.S.W.
* * * *
The end of the seventh round of First
Grade cricket in Sydney, on Saturday last, saw the
bat make rather a feeble showing, in spite of the fact
that three centuries were on the list. J. S. Taylor,
of Burwood, smote 182 against Sydney, and Law and
Scott, both of Petersham, pounded up 138 and 130
respectively against Paddington. But consider the
bowling figures, and how they run. A. Rough, of
Petersham, bagged four Paddington wickets for 12
runs. Kerim, the Syrian of Redfern, captured foui
Gordon wickets at a cost of 35 runs. Mailey, also of
Redfern, took the other five Gordon wickets for 47.
Follcard, the Balmain wonder, raked in seven Glebe
wickets for 71, in addition to carrying his bat for
60. Dowd, of Paddington, smashed three Petersham
sticks for 38. Stack, of University, collared nine
Middle Harbor men for 117 runs. Law, of Peter-
sham, downed three of Paddington for 40 runs.
Emery, of Redfern, took eight Gordon wickets for
107. Redfern was the star combination of the day,
and having declared with eight wickets down for
456, it demolished Gordon for 165 and 94, thus win-
ning by an inning's and 197 runs. Balmain, with 192
in the first innings, managed to get Glebe out for
172, and thus won by 20 on the first call. Sydney,
having bashed 394 for the loss of seven wickets, de-
cided to go for something great, and sent Burwood
in to bat. Burwood recorded 386 for the loss of seven
wickets, and time running out, the game ended in a
draw. University, having piled up 342, felt reason-
ably safe for a full point win, when Middle Harbor
replied with only 180. But the effort to get the enemy
out quickly in the second innings failed, and only
eight wickets had fallen for 130 when time ran out.
So University had to be content with a first innings
win by 162 runs. A man who played a daring game
was the captain of Petersham. Paddington had 122
on the board when Petersham went in, and ran up to
297 for the loss of six wickets. Then the Petersham
captain declared the innings closed. It was Pad-
dington’s evil day, and Petersham’s notable bowlers,
Rough and Law, got it down and fell upon it. The
side vanished for a miserable 74, and Petersham was
on top with an innings and 101 runs to spare. North
Sydney, with 381, proved too many for Waverley by
97 runs on the first time of asking. The cemetery
suburb made only 284.
* * * *
The sixth round of Melbourne Pennant
cricket, concluded on Saturday, was like its Pjn-
decessors, only more so —a lashing around of hefty
bat-wielders, a piling up of runs and a one-innings
j or a draw. The bowler that Australia, and
especially the Weary State, wants still dwells in
modest retirement. As for premiership prospects,
none of the clubs got damaged, though Northcote,
in putting up a paltry 286 against ’Wood s more
paltry 214, fared better than it deserved. P ran,
flying the 1911 pennant, and virtually the strongest
team in the competition, left some more of its
reputation in the dust by gathering in but 198 runs
to North’s 200. Richmond had beaten St. Kilda
when only five of its wickets were down, ultimately
closing the rout with 287 to Saints’ 181. The colli-
sion of East and South, though neither has better
than a dog’s chance of the pennant, was a consid-
erable and well-patronised struggle. With the aid
of 97 from Peter McAlister and 78 from ex-inter-
Stater E. Y. Carroll, East acquired 283. None of
Harry Trott’s men did much in reply save W. Scott,
but he made 220 off his own little stick, and when
the discussion ceased South’s total was 356, a con-
siderable feat against the bowling of Laver, McDon-
ald, Braid, Parsons and co. Fitzroy, with 123 from
Colin McKenzie and 121 from veteran Chris Kier-
nan, having put together 310 for five wickets on
Saturday week, declared, and gave Essendon a
whole afternoon to match the tally, or go under.
Though it lost the first three wickets for 13 runs,
the ’Dons rose to the occasion, and, With 58 from
Proud and 103 from Pat Shea, put up 239 before
stumps were drawn. It is good to close your in-
nings when you think you have the game safe, but
it is possible to be too previous at times, and Roy
very nearly did it. The Melbourne-Carlton dis-
turbance didn’t count in the scheme of things, but
was punctuated by a pleasing 124 from the bat of
Harry Sandford, a youth of 19,. with the finished
style and gay insouciance of manner of poor Harry
Graham. He just previously made 153 against St.
Kilda, and as he bats with a sort of reckless irrele-
vance, annoying to a bowler but joyous to a
spectator, it is to be hoped he will continue the
good work.

bowlers aren’t of much account when Barnes
and Foster are away. Both these two were “rest-

this year’s Vic. Rhodes scholar, put together 55,
while his uncle exactly equalled Liddicutt’s Geelong
score against the same team, viz., 129, before being
caueht and bowled by Iremonger. Whatever the
bonding may be all the batting talent of the Weary
State doesn’t dwell Melbourne. #

Valley, on Saturday, Alan Marshall smote mightily,

The tally included 13 hits worth six runs apiece ; and

Victoria has three colts well in the run-
ning for larger honors in the cricket roll M. Her-
ring, who crossed the path of the M.C.O. last week-
end, and scored 129 in a total of 318 his fifth
successive century this season; Liddicutt, who smote

* * * *
Swimming records are being chopped
mercilessly this season. In the 880yds. N.S.W. pro-
fessional championship, at Leichhardt (Sydney), last
week, O. Dickman won in llmin. 18sec.—19sec. bet-
ter than the world’s professional record belonging to
Englishman D. Billington. Dickman’s time is also
20 2-ssec. faster than amateur W. Longworth’s effort
in the N.S.W. championship a week or so ago ; and
the win was so easy that it appeared that even more
could have been clipped off the record without much
exertion. As showing the rush for the cash in League
swimming, the 50.yds. handicap, of £lO 10s., at the
same meeting, had an entry of 117 a fact which
speaks loudly for the love of the money rather than
the teapot.
* * * *
The Victorian invention for the improve-
ment of chassis suspension, mentioned in this page
a few weeks back, has proved itself sufficiently to
make money talk. An English company is being
formed, with a capital of £200,000, to operate the
British rights; the French have already been se-
cured, and the German will be taken over by a
sauerkraut combination of equal financial tonnage.
Boiled down, the invention is a device whereby the
vertical shocks are deflected to the direction of travel.
How it is , done this deponent knoweth not; but,
judging by the reports of men like Lamplough,
Napier, Remington and Maudsley, the blessed car
fitted with the new arrangement can jump the street
kerb, and do all sorts of bounding evolutions, with-
out the occupants feeling any inconvenience. Riding
on a chassis suspended according to the new revela-
tion is said to be like sailing along in a boat, no
matter how rough the road is. If it can stand the
wear and tear, and put up anything like a decent life,
then the pneumatic tyre on motor-cars is in its
last days. It is understood that Harry B. James,
of the Dunlqp Rubber Co., will shortly drive a car of
the new device from Adelaide to Melbourne, with a
view to finding out how it will stand wear and stress,
and his report will be eagerly awaited.
* * * *
The third contest between the 14-footers
of Westralia, Queensland and N. S. Wales took place
on the waters of Port Jackson on Saturday last. The
weather was ideal, the wind light and from the
south-east —the sort of wind that taxes the ingenuity
of the master-mariner to get the best out of it. At
the fall of the fifth flag seven boats were streaking
away, and of these two represented Westralia and
Queensland, while five upheld the cause of Ma. There
would have been six representatives of Ma, only St.
George, the holder of the title, carried away her mast
before the race started, and drifted a wreck on to
the lee shore of Clarke Island, fortunately without
loss of life. Waratah, of Queensland, and Etna, of
Westralia, were the last to get away. It soon
became evident that the Botany Bay boat,
Rene, was going to be among the placed
craft. Although flying no extras it danced away
from the fleet with the Queenslander—a wonderful
mass of canvas, top-sail, sninnaker and ring-tail; —
coming very fast indeed. There was some ambiguity
in the instructions regarding what constituted the
rounding mark, and an alternative having been men-
tioned in case of removal of the rounding mark,
some of the boats went round both of them, and
thereby lost a little time. The race was actually a
long stern chase of Rene by the Queensland ship,
the crew of which displayed wonderful skill in the
handling of canvas. Rene romped home with 33sec.
to spare from \varatah, and Sydney was in the third
place. It was a great race. Rene was so much the
faster boat that the splendid skill of the Queensland
crew could not wipe out the difference.
* * * *
The world’s best all-round cyclist, A. J.
Clarke, of Australia, is back again in his native land,
to ride with A. J. Davies in the Melbourne Six-Day
Race. His travels abroad have made him wider and
thicker than when he went away, and he brings with
him an American wife. Also, he is much more like
a Yankee than an Australian. Three times he has
finished second to Frank Kramer, the sprinting king,
but then Kramer is not the all-round man that
Clarke is. In addition to three times running second
in the sprint championship of America, Clarke has
won two six-day races at Madison Square Gardens,
and one in Germany (the first held there). He will
be in Melbourne till the end of February, and be seen
again on the track in Sydney in March.
* * * *
N.S.W. League of Wheelmen is able to
stand up again without props, last season’s efforts
having yielded a credit balance of £lOO. With
the life which the six-days’ race awakened in the
pastime, it seems possible that “boom” days may
yet return to N.S.W. professional cycling.
* * * *
Chief Justice Stout, of Maoriland, is a
supporter of the feeble Wowser fiction that the tote
on the racecourse only is a more widespread incentive
to betting than the metallician who lays odds every-
where. “He believes that if Australia abolished the
bookmaker, and replaced him by the totalisator,
gambling would not decrease, but increase.” This is
a “belief” that exists on the impossibility of pro-
ducing direct proof of its falseness. The takings of
the tote are bound to swell as the population of a
State increases, and the racing improves in honesty,
owing to the abolition of bookmakers. Turf gamb-
ling becomes more attractive because it is less harm-
ful. The losses of punters are much less, on the
average, under a tote system, than when the “books”
had the robbing of them. Whereas a large amount of
public capital has to be put through the machine
many times before it is all lost to the public, the same
amount is not infrequently swallowed by the books
at one gulp. South Australia offers trustworthy
figures re the gambling evil. If Robert Stout wants
information re the difference between the results of
racing before and after the legalising of the tote in
S.A. some 20-odd years ago, he can get it from the
police records. There was a big drop in the number
of prosecutions for embezzlement as soon as the
temptation to “shop” and credit betting was removed
by the substitution of the ready-money machine, on
racecourses only, for the bookmaker who gave credit
and plied his trade all over the place.
* * * *
“Snorter”: The maligned penciller doesn’t
always have it entirely his own way. Scrutinise, for
example, the following analysis of the six events
at Aspendale Park (Vic.) on a recent Saturday:—
Where
Number First
of Favorite
Runners. Winner. Finished.

2nd , 23 Ist ~ First
3rd ”, „ 24 2nd ,) Second
Steeplechase 10 Ist •> (evens) First
Park Handicap 9 2nd ~ Thud
Jumpers’Flat 17 Ist ~ First
The average number of runners for each paltry stake
was 17, yet in every case the winner had started
first or second favorite, and in no case was the
actual first favorite unplaced. Eighty of the horses
were treated as nonentities by the professional back-
ers. And they ran accordingly. Only in one race —
the Steeplechase—did a rank outsider get placed,
and when the fields were largest, the betting was the
surest guide to the results. Such occurrences lend an
air of verisimilitude to the banshee-like moans that
Isaac Mo. emits concerning his hard life, and the way
everyone’s hand is against him, and the miserably
scant hopes he has of expiring in a state of solvency.

meet.
The Kangaroo Rugby League footballers
got a taste of Fogland, last week, when a match
was abandoned owing to the playing-ground being
under snow. What a grand place England is for
the absent inhabitant —especially in winter! Of
the 33 matches played by the Kangaroos during their
wanderings, 27 have been won, four lost and two
drawn —a rather substantial record.
* * * *
Luck put up a great battle for Frank
Thorn at his second meeting with Billy Elliott, in
Melbourne, the other evening. When this brace
brought off their first mutual assault, Thorn secured
a victory on points, but a skinful of trouble went
with it, and it was reckoned he had nothing to cut
to waste when Elliott faced him again. The fight
produced 34 minutes of vivacious bruising, and then
Elliott collapsed. It seemed at first that Thorn had
fouled him out, but medical experts reported an
organic injury, the result of a twist, entirely Elliott’s
own doing. So Frank took the bundle, and went
home, and Billy went to the surgery. There are just
101 ways of getting licked in a fight. Elliott has
brought the 101st to light.
* * * *
The disturbance between Hugh Mehegan
and Yankee-Italian Picato, at Sydney Stadium, on
Saturday night, was a hard-fought affair. Practically
every round ended with Mehegan on top. Still,
although overmatched, Picato was always dangerous,
right up to the 16th round, when he fizzled out and
spent all his energy and skill in avoiding a knock-
out. He got through.
* * * *
Yankee fighter “Porky” Flynn (what a
name!) has had a bad time since he arrived in Aus-
tralia. A poisoned finger gave him much pain and
prevented him doing anything startling in the way
of putting a head on his brother pug, and final!v it
was found necessary to perform an amputation. The
bone has been removed half-way down to the second
joint, and “Porkv” will scrap with Bandsman Rice
on the night of February 3, at Sydney Stadium.
* * * *
Dago-Yankee bruiser Joe Grimm, who
created much amusement in Australia some years
ago, was supposed to be dead. But it seems he is
still swimming around, and, when last heard from,
was in Portland (U.S.A.) A flagpole snapped, and
the wreck landed on Grimm’s head. People rushed
to his assistance, but he merely scratched the place,
and remarked he had been hit much harder than that,
and walked off to have a banana.
* * * *
Jim Barry, the Chicago heavyweight at
present in Sydney seeking trouble, is, like Henry
Irving, a student of crime, and is generally regarded
as one of the best amateur detectives in the U.S.A.
The Pinkerton Private Police organisation has made
repeated efforts to get him on its staff, but Barry
clings stubbornly to the biffing life, on account of
its freedom and big money. Barry is a curious mix-
ture, with Irish, French and Indian blood in his
veins. He is a sort of human haggis.
■# * * *
“K.”: Re par. (B. 18/1/’l2) concerning
the thickness of the Amurkan nigger’s lid.
Johnson recentlv submitted to having an X-ray
photo, of his ceiling taken, and the results are stag-
gering. The common, or garden skull is from one-
eighth to, in extreme cases, a half-inch thick. John-
son’s dome varies from iin. to Jin. The time for
photographing an ordinary skull is five to 15sec.; but
it took the X-rays 5 § minutes to make a picture of
the champion pug.’s inside thinking apparatus. John-
son has a brain-box of exceptional size, and it is so
thickly armored that nothing short of a 14in. gun
would make an impression on it.
* * * *
“Bilbaker”: Boxing is having an up and
down time of it in England. Certain contests were
prevented by the authorities, and the “kidney punch”
declared illegal. But the limit was reached in a
match at Sheffield, between Alf Langford (said to be
a cousin of Sam’s) and Gunner Healy. In the eleventh
round the police inspector put the plug in, on the
ground that Langford was not boxing, only trying
for a “knock out.” Boxing contests are “off” now
in the cutlery city.

Government Notice.
Department of Defence,
Melbourne, 17th January, 1912.
Appointment of two compe-
tent MECHANISTS AND AVIA-
TORS.
APPLICATIONS from Expert Mechan-
ists and Aviators desirous of being appointed
to the Defence Department will be received
by:—
THE SECRETARY, DEPARTMENT
OF DEFENCE, VICTORIA BAR-
RACKS, MELBOURNE, or
THE HIGH COMMISSIONER FOR
AUSTRALIA, 72 VICTORIA
STREET, WESTMINSTER, S.W.,
up to and incusive of FRIDAY, the Ist
MARCH, 1912 (extended from Ist Febru-
ary, 1912).
Candidates must state: —Nature of quali-
fications and experience; Military ex-
perience (if any) ; Whether born or
domiciled in Australia; Age; Whether
married or single.
Salary to be at rate of £4OO per annum,
inclusive of all allowances except travelling.
The Commonwealth Government will accept
no liability for accidents.
The successful candidates will be appointed
for 12 months on probation, upon conclu-
sion of which, if their services have been
satisfactory, their appointments may be con-
firmed.
S. A. PETHEBRIDGE, Secretary.

BARNET GLASS MOTOR CYCLE TYRES
Long famous among Motor Cyclists for their general excellence,
B.G. Motor Cycle Tyres placed the seal on their fame m the recent
Sydney to Melbourne Reliability Trial,
when Mr. A. Maplestone rode
BARNET GLASS Studded Motor Cycle Tyres,
which, at the end of the journey, were found to be
in better condition than those of any other makes on
other machines. -
B.G. CYCLE TYRES
For long the choice of most experienced Cyclists, and
upon which most of the finest records in recent years;

“Exile”: I’ve just got home from the
Outer Darkness to God’s own country. At
Adelaide I bought a paper, and noticed that
seven bachelors of laws and 36 new doctors
had been indicted by Madden, C.J., at Mel-
bourne University. They are all learning
the wrong things: Our ship brought six
gas-engineers under contract to permanent
billets ; three of them are to get over £5OO a
year for seeing that coal is properly stoked
at one end and brought out as gas at the
other. Another highly-paid man goes to
Lithgow to test rails for the Government.
Surely we Australians can do all our own
gas-inspecting and rail-inspecting ourselves.
We don’t, by the way, seem to use the 36
doctors to inspect immigrants, as we had
four consumptives and one case of g.p. on
board, and they ai. .all likely to land, as the
medical inspection of immigrants is a huge
farce.
Listen to the hysteria of the cable news
distender as he tells of Calcutta’s final out-
break of “loyalty” to King George. This is
from the Melbourne Argus :
Preparations for the event had been in progress
for months. The native princes of 30 States had
brought their armies of retainers and their herds of
trained elephants and tuskers, camels, and dancing
horses. The .great line of color included 57 elephants
and tuskers, and 35 camels, all being richly capari-
soned.
In short, each prince brought an innumer-
able herd of one elephant and most of an-
other, and raised the dust as far as the re-
motest horizon with one camel and one-sixth
of another camel. As to the dancing horses,
their number isn’t given. But the Mahara-
jah of Jeypore used to be one of the horsiest
of India’s native nrinces, and when this
writer had the blessed privilege of inspect-
ing his stable, he had one dancing animal.
It was really, a pony, and it simply threw up
its hind legs when it was tickled and waved
its tail in an unimportant sort of way.
The Argentine correspondent of the S. M.
Herald told the old paper the other day
that labor was going to be very scarce dur-
ing the coming harvest, and wages would
probably be as much as 14s. per day. He
explained that this dreadful calamity was
due to the railway cos. chopping down wages
a while ago, and the consequent “numerous
departure of laborers from the country.”
Fat rarely has had a more annoying state-
ment thrown at him. Hitherto, the mono-
poly of leaving the country has always been
the pet possession of bloated capital. To
know that common labor can do the same
thing is most embarrassing. In fact, the
Argentine cockies who have to pay 14s. a
day might find it less beastly inconvenient if
sacred capital booked its passage by the first
lugger.
Australia is beginning, in a small way, to
follow the U.S.A. in the matter of outland-
ish new religions. The latest upheaval was
the inauguration of the Order of the Star of
the East, in Phillip-street, Sydney, near the
head office of the police. It burst into sud-
den life with a local membership of over 200
and a lot of outside support. It is a curious
sect, which believes in the coming of a Great
Teacher, and throws cold water on the Judg-
ment Day, explaining that all these intricate
matters of worthiness of immortality are
settled by a slow, cold process of evolution.
Like produces like, and the sum total of it
weighs down the balance, and the chance of
eternal life is lost, and the soul that has lost
it begins to degenerate, sliding horribly from
the man to the pig, from the pig to the rep-
tile, the reptile to the slug, the slug to some
baser form of semi-nervous life, and thence
to the inorganic. Thus a cabbage may be
an ex-king of bad character, jit is a sort of
revival of the Order of the Rosy Cross, the
members of which were known _ as Rosi-
crucians, who sought the Philosopher’s
Stone, and the secret of physical immortal-
ity. The modern crowd has abandoned the
idea of physical immortality, and postpones
the taking on of the eternal form to the next
stage of existence. These immortalities are
an amusing crowd, and people who almost
die of ennui on a wet Sunday contemplate
the tragedy of the shoreless ages of eternity
with the utmost equanimity.
By “Henri Singemoine” :
The world is full of cant, and every now and then
a fresh variety of the horror is born. For example,
of late, worthy old gentlemen and others have been
remarking thoughtfully that boxing is being “ruined
by the money element.” “Leave it alone and it will
kill itself,” they say sternly, with all the air of one
who has, after severe labor, resolved a huge sociolo-
gical puzzle. When they are asked why big money
should kill boxing any more than it has killed cricket
(where the cash question is more clamant now than
ever before) or football, or aeroplaning, or horse-
racing, or pigeon-shooting at Monte Carlo, or bill-
iards, or a thousand other sports where the par-
ticipants stand to rake in anything from £5OO to
£5OOO as a reward for their labors, the oldsters bury
themselves in the tomb of a dead silence. The reason
is that there is no answer available. One of the
manv differences between boxing as it exists to-day,
and boxing as it was half a century ago, lies in the
fact that where once it was the privilege of the
affluent few to witness a boxing bout, it is now pos-
sible for anyone to do so who has a few shillings to
spare. Naturally, the boxer earns more money, just
as the modern music-hall star, who plays weekly to
thousands at half. a dozen different houses, com-
mands 20 times as much as the Lion Comique who
diverted the patrons of one small hall with a seating
capacity of a couple of hundred.
The blither about the objectionableness of the
money-element as regards boxing, is, when it comes
to be considered in all its bearings, simply a move-
ment in the direction of underpaying the pugilist.
London Winning Post, which voices the sentiments
of the National Sporting Club, where the pug. has
always been sweated, came out frankly, the other
week, with a cry of longing for the good old era
when fighters accepted, with thanks, whatever was
offered to them; and it wound up with the aspiration
that the day of “mushroom promoters” would soon
end. There is about as much likelihood of this hap-
pening as there is of a reversion to Feudalism. The
wares of the skilled boxer have the same fixed value
in the world’s market as the novels of Marie Corelli
or the art of Little Tich. No amount of argument,
no frantic appeals to his better feelings will ever
make Jack Johnson extend himself in the hempen
square for a ten-pound note, a pot of beer, and the
privilege of having his vast and loathly paw shaken
by a peer—as his 18th century prototype used gladly
to do. , ,
But the black calamity .will box no less well and
honorably for that. It is indisputable that, for good
behavior, in and out of the Ring, the present-day
pug. fairly loses his brother of the bare-knuckle
period—when it was the fashion among Right-
thinkers to refer to pugilism as one of “old Eng-
land’s glories.” Also, the modern boxing crowds are
considerably more orderly and decent than the crowds
of yore, who, when the man that was winning didn’t
happen to have been backed by, them, mostly outed
him with a fusillade of bricks and bottles.
I hereby move that the snuffle about the sport of
boxing being “destroyed,” because a race of business
men has arisen who pay the performers what they
are worth, be leapt on with scorn whenever it makes
its appearance. As well say that the superb old
pastime of Devil-harassing is on the wane because
the champions or archbishops of the business make

The Inky Way.
That industrial agreement which the Australian
Journalists’ Association has made with the newspaper
proprietors is going to have a lot of effects. Up till
now, a very few of the big morning papers have
monopolised the best men, because they alone paid a
decent wage. Now, a “log” rate has been fixed for
all papers in the large cities, so the best men are
no longer forced to look only to a little group of
bosses. They can choose those which require the least
work for the standard wage, and those offices where the
conditions all round are most pleasant. Also, the
best newspaper staffs in future will not be found on
the morning papers, for very few men will work on
a morning daily when they can get almost the same
money on an afternoon rag. The evening papers,
recognising this, are hastening to secure the pick of
the men from the morning papers. Thus the morning
paper proprietaries are liable to presently find them-
selves with the fag ends of their own staffs and the
discards of the evening papers—unless they decide to
pay considerably above log rates, and accept reduced
profits.
* * * *
One of the curiosities of the Australian inky way
is the Nobden, which has been circulating among
the Danes, Swedes and Norwegians of Australia for
the past 15 years, and lately felt strong enough to
break out into a special Christmas number, with an
eye to a foreign circulation. The principal article,
well illustrated, was by Jens Lyng, the author of a
book or two, who says to the Scandinavian: “If
you’re thinking of immigrating, try Australia. It’s
as good as any country in the world and better than
most.” Ordinarily, the Nor den is a sort of fort-
nightly news-letter, very handy for the hardy Norse-
men to drop into the post to tell the old fjord how
the new world is treating Oscar and Olaf and Martha
and little Thyra.
* * * *
“Tired Pressman”: Prank Foster, M.H.R., is re-
ported to have interjected during the “signed
articles” debate on the Electoral Bill, “They are
hired assassins”—meaning the men who dig bread
and dripping with the pen-point. I’m fervently sick
of this sort of muck from Labor politicians. I’ve
heard it too often during a harried press career.
Come, now, what is Foster’s own position ? As a
Labor member he supports a Government which
supplies Tory newspapers with telephones, telegrams
and a postal service. If the “hired assassin” is in
Melbourne, and the paper whose opinions he puts
into words is in Sydney, that Government will cheer-
fully carry the message; and Foster supports it, and
supplies it with the authority necessary to do this
service.
* * * *
“L.F.H.” : T. H. S. Escott’s book on British journal-
ism, just published by Fisher Unwin (London), is far
and away the best and most exhaustive so far pub-
lished. His researches have carried him back far
beyond the invention of printing. The war corre-
spondent was the first journalist. He flourished in
the times of Thucydides, Xenophon, Julius
Caesar and Livy. During the Wars of the
Boses in England, there was an indefati-
gable news-collector named Fenn, who organ-
ised a staff of correspondents, and was thus enabled
to bring out a weekly manuscript journal, for which
he had a considerable number of regular subscribers.
Rivals followed in Fenn’s footsteps, and several
manuscript newspapers came into being. Also, lead-
ing families kept a “newsman” upon the domestic
staff. It was his duty, when they were away from
home or travelling, to keep them well informed as to
what was happening. But the undoubted father of
journalism, as we now understand the word, was
Daniel Defoe, best known as the author of “Robinson
Crusoe.” By his tremendous intellectual vigor he
made journalism a real political power with the
people at large. As Escott says, he “wanted for him-
self the positions of editor, publisher, political
writer, social and literary critic.” His paper circu-
lated in all the London coffee houses and taverns, and
was carried home to be read by the wives and
daughters. Defoe paved the way for Addison and
Steele, who were followed by Smollett, Wilkes, Leigh
Hunt and Coleridge.
- * * *
“Penman”: Here is a matter which I submit to
my fellow journeymen-journalists, with full confidence
of a unanimous verdict in my favor. I contracted to
write a lengthy article for £1 a column. The news-
paper proprietor thought so highly of it that he
“leaded” every line, and thus it made six columns.
My bill was for £6, but I could only get £3. “Oh,”
says the proprietor, “the leading is my business; it
has nothing to do with you. You didn’t expect it,
did you?” “I did not contract for £3, but for £1
a column, whatever it might make,” says I. But I
might as well have talked to the type. Sancho
Panza said you must take the boss’s verdict, and sit
down to dinner. But Sancho Panza didn’t know; quite
as much about trades unionism and the like as we do
to-day.
.* * » *
Dear Bulletin, —In view of the number of actor-
folk who burst into poetry in the “Poverty Point”
column, I beg to suggest that it be re-christened
“Pros, and Verse.” —Yours, Locust.
A SHORT WAY WITH EDITORS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Constantinople, Tuesday Evening.—lsmail Faik
Bey, a journalist, has been bastinadoed. for the pub-
lication of an article criticising the “overbearing atti-
tude” of the Committee of Union and Progress.
The father of Ismail, who is the editor of an An-
gora newspaper, has been arrested for publishing an
open letter of protest, being whipped senseless.
Similar acts of tyranny have occurred in other
parts of Turkey.
— Cable.
There isn’t any nonsense of a sentimental kind
About the Turk!
As a rule he’s very thorough when he really puts his
mind
To am' work!
Though he’s weak on sanitation,
Finance, medicine, astronomy—
He holds a lofty station
In political economy.
For he models all his methods on the Book of Deu-
teronomy 1
In Caucasian communities strange lenience is dis-
played
Towards the scribe
Who wields the scathing, scurril pen, and not the
flashing blade,
To flay his tribe!
At least, that’s been the custom
Observed until quite recently;
The scribe might fight like Rustem,
As long as he fought decently.
But the monster Costs devoured him if he ever wrote
malfeasantly !
But such tender, foolish methods never troubled
Abdul Bey-—-
The written word
He treated, like the levelled blow, in his old-
fashioned way,
When it occurred!
Editorial bravado,
In the shape of criticism,
Had to own the bastinado
A more biting witticism—
Abdul’s “ology” with scribes was: We can always
fit his “ism” !
When the Bugle of Angora (as the cables tell us)
wrote
Some hostile views
Of Abdul, he remarked: “We’ll of this person make
a note —
Off with his shoes I”
Whereat they placed that editor
In most distressing attitude,
Who yelled: “I never said it !”—or
“It was but harmless platitude!”
His last words, ere he fainted, were: “Was ever
such ingratitude f”
