LIMITED,
The Big Block, BRISBANE.
IN BANANA LAND
The garrison at Thursday Island —the
small, white force that is supposed to guard
the waterway from the North to the
eastern coast cities of Australia —
bubbled over the other day, and the
office of the little Patriot nearly got boiled
in the process. One of the garrison troops
had been lost on the Cape York mainland,
and had apparently died of thirst or starva-
tion ; whereupon the Patriot was foolish
enough to remark that “the loss of a pros-
pective farm-burner doesn’t matter very
much one way or the other —nowadays.”
Evidently the Patriot hasn’t yet observed
that, so far as Australia is concerned, it is
the other fellow who is likely to do the
farm-burning, and that the Australian de-
fence-man’s job is to keep him from doing
it.
Some 16 or 17 years ago, Queensland
threw one of its best prospective assets —its
Brisbane tramway rights—to. an English
company, and it has been filled ever since
with the cussedness of great joy. The Bris-
bane trams are inadequate, they are not
cheap, and they are not excessively com-
fortable. And manager Badger is some-
thing like N.S.W. Railway Commissioner
Johnson. Just now there is trouble on the
Brisbane trams. A union of employees had
been formed, and the union devised a badge
which the unionists proceeded to wear. Why
the badge should have irritated Badger, any
more than a blue ribbon or a flower, doesn’t
appear; It wasn’t illegal. It didn’t over-
load the cars, and if the extra weight was a
matter of any importance, Badger might
have issued an order that the men who wore
the article should have their hair cut short,
to counterbalance the burden. The fact that
a number of orderly people took an interest
in noting who wore and who didn’t wear the
badge should rather have cheered up Bad-
ger. He should have been glad to see the
streets crowded, that being a matter which
would lead to business. If the crowds be-
came obstreperous that was a matter for the
police—not for Badger. A wise man doesn’t
go looking for trouble, unless there is money
in it. But the Brisbane tramway boss or-
dered that the wearing of badges should
cease, just as he might have ordered the men
to leave off shirts or boots, and started to
plunge round and exhibit himself as the
Strong Man of the situation, if a thing like
that could be called a situation. The result,
so far, is not exactly a lock-out or a strike,
but a very-much-dislocated service, the loss
of a, great many fares, a deal of walking ex-
ercise on the part of the community, and a
very important Badger occupying a position
of uncertain legality. Of course a man
can’t help being Badger if he is born that
way, but a lot of people might prefer to be
J obson.
Brisbane paper :—-
By the Kuranda, which left Innisfail (Geraldton)
for Townsville on Thursday, were 16 Chinese saloon
passengers, and 19 Europeans in the same class.
Down south, whare he is a comparatively
rare fowl, Ah Chow is a deferential creature
who crawls into the dark corner, or
hides on the back perch; but give
him the stiffening that comes with num-
bers, and Ah Chow has no use for any
dark corners or back perches. It’s the
foleign white devil, in the steward’s uniform,
that is the dirt then. Still, the foleign white
devil of Geraldton hasn’t any cause to com-
plain ; for Geraldton is the town where the
foleign white devil long ago contracted the
habit of taking off his hat to the magnificent
yellow person who keeps two joss-houses
going within the one small township.

Stores in Australasia. Ladies recommend
them. Be sure you get the genuine with the
signature, Martin, Chemist, Southampton,*
The Clerical Slog.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a recent boxing bout at Longreach, the Rev.
Hulton Sams acted as referee ; Father Lane officiated
as timekeeper, and the Rev. C. Hicks as 10 seconds
check. When the Rev. Sams gave his decision for
a draw, some of the audience boo-hooed, and the
referee thereupon invited any who disagreed to step
into the ring with him. No one complied. —News
item.
A time there was when the Church would snort and
Methody leaders sniff
At them who step in the fistic ring and follow the
sinful biff;
But now the cleric will don the gloves and offer a
churchly cheek
To them who smite with a fist of might and bash on
a foeman’s beak.
“Step into the ring with me,
Just put on the gloves,” says he ;
“And the Church will biff till the Flesh is stiff,”
says the Reverend Referee.
If Satan shakes —as> the old hymn says —whenever
he turns and sees
The humblest saint in a prayerful fit drop down on
his contrite knees,
What would he do—and his demon crew —if parson
addressed him thus:
“Rut up. your props in a manly way, and try a few
rounds with us ;
Step into the ling with me,
Just put on the gloves and see
How the Church will biff till the Devil is stiff,” says
the Reverend Referee.
The times march on, and the Church has found that
she must get marching, too,
And train her men for the old “good fight” that
waits for the preacher true;
And whether she slog at the curse of grog, or go for
Apollyon’s tip,
It's Ho! Look out for the parson stout, and ’ware
of his grievous grip!
“Step into the ring with me ;
Just put on the gloves,” says lie,
“And the Church will biff till the world is stiff,”
says the Reverend Referee!
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, January 20, 1912.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The parsons still wax fat, and the maker
of wedding rings waneth not. On Monday,
at All Saints’, Miss Ruby Hall discarded
her naihe for that of D’Arcy Winten, and
flitted from the church by motor (driven by
the groom), towards the Northern Rivers,
where the honey-swoon is suspected of lurk-
ing. Everything in the drapery line was
blue, except Miss Winten (one of the maids)
and she was buttercup.
On Wednesday, popular Cecil O’Neil
Brenan was shepherded to St. James’s
Church, Enoggera, by his two best pals, Mr.
Dixon and Mr. Nigel Shand, and there swore
off bachelorhood, with the entire approbation
of Miss Sue Ward, daughter of Duncan of
that ilk. Sister Mollie Brenan and a little
Miss Ward (at present of the kingdom of
fiapperdom) egged on the bride, who was har-
nessed in satin and lace.
On Thursday, St. Stephen’s Cathedral was
packed with perspiring humanity (mostly
A LEGITIMATE' GRIEVANCE.
BUCOLIC BOWLER (to batsman who for the third time has swiped him for four): “Here,
I say , blokie, let up. You're spoilin' my average!"
self-invited), anxious to see the Beirne-
Hooper union. The bride, who is the eldest
daughter of kindly T. C. Beirne, wore a
gown that caused the tenth commandment to
be obliterated and rendered of no account.
It was fabricated of satin, and lavishly
trimmed or rhymed with exquisite Duchesse
point. The veil was also of Duchesse point,
and the general result was all that the most
calculating bride could do to plant the roots
of envy in sister woman. Four little Beirnes,
in white net with blue-edged frills, kept the
crowd from treading ou the bride; and
Messrs. Charley Bowley and Fred Connah
saw to it that the groom (who was dressed
in dark bagging, or tweed, or something,,
trimmed with white linen and crowned with
head whiskers) did not repent at the last
minute. Afterwards, he was led southward
to your city by his new boss. Amongst the
paternal gifts were a house and a motor-
car.
Still more engagements are rumored, so
evidently Cupid works more than the legal
eight hours a day, or else his marksmanship
is improving. One is'little Miss Grace Mor-
gan to Norman Jeffries, a person with a large
banking account, who hails from the North.
Another is pretty Rita Murpliy, daughter of
the Hon. Peter, to J. Filhelly, at one time an
ardent follower of Rugby.
Things have been very quiet of late'at the
Government Shanty, but his Ex. goes to
Blackall on Monday, and if he paints the
place red I’ll let you know next week.
Agent-General Robinson and his missus
have presented to Premier Denham’s missus
a Queensland opal necklet. Incidentally)
the Robinsons have been visiting a few of
the big homesteads during the past few days.
The Frank Needhams, at Canning Downs,
and the Cressbrook McConnels were on - the
list of eligibles.
“Tommy” Jones, the cheery old clergyman
who is so beloved of all classes and denom-
inations, has had a bout with the pneu-
monia devil, and, at the time of writing,
seems to have gained a certain victory. At
latest, the referee was steadily counting out
the enemy.
Another popular Brisbaneite, Mrs. (Dr.)
Hopkins, has been ou the sick list. She se-
verely strained herself while attempting to
restore a drowned man at Southport during
the year-end holidays, and was for some
time in hospital. She is so far recovered
now that she is putting the finishing touches
to her health in her own home.
A friend of mine, whose wash-up repre-
sentative walks out with the envoy of the
meat hidalgo, tells in tragic tones that
1000 butchers are not only ready, but eager,
to strike in sympathy with the troubled
tramway men. Thank Heaven, I haven t
heard of a Chinese Gardeners’ Union, so
“eabbagee” and the aesthetic turnip may still
grace our tables. At time of writing there
was no information as to what action the
Industrial Union of Imported , Governors
proposes to take; ’but the worst is feared.
The Q.T.C. races were well attended on
Friday, by the Fleeced and the Fleecers.
The glad rags were again of the boiled frock
order, and summery hats of the floral type
shaded the heads of our women.
Local gossip received a severe upper cut
the other day. A well-known man departed
on a business trip to America, and pitiful
tales of his cruel treatment of his weaker
half were sent the rounds before his ship had
slid down the rim of the horizon. Unfortu-
nately, his grass widow got to hear the news,
and in her indignation wound the Law up
to full 'cock. Now the sympathetic scandal-
hawkers are sorry their sympathy was mis-
placed.
Yours affectionately.
AUSTRALIA JANE.
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of- communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.
D.D. : By your account the “average
Australian” is “one of the most blatant and ignor-
ant persons to be found anywhere.” But unless, you
know something yourself you can hardly tell what. is
ignorance and what is not. How much, for in-
stance, do you know? Write it all out on half a
sheet of notepaper. .. .L. 5.8.: (1) A square mile is
an area of 640 acres in any shape. ,A mile square is
640 acres contained within equal lines, with right
angled corners. Also, while a square mile and a
mile square are both 640 acres in extent the resem-
blance ends there. Two square miles are 1280 acres
while two miles square are 2560 acres. (2) It is a
mineral. (3) Merely a matter of opinion.... Ivory:
Please don’t send us parodies in summer. It is too
hard work thinking up sufficiently insulting remarks
to welcome them with Endealite: It sounds
musical, but the meaning is hard to arrive at. It
reminds us of those tender lines which the curate
read to the weeping ladies at the Dorcas meeting:—
Sweet lady mine who glitterest in shimmer of sum-
mer athwart the lawn,
Ganst tell me which is bitterest —the glamor of eve
or the glimmer of dawn ?
... .Rejected: You say that we “know and have
always known that Protection duties have been not
only the bulwark but the very soil and manure that
has grown the landlord,” etc. Is that why the land-
lord is such an amazing institution in Freetrade
Britain? According to the Statesman’s Year Book
there were, in 1909, 509,171 farms in England,
Wales and Scotland, and of these 447,108 were
rented from some loafing landlord or another. On
the other hand, in Protectionist France nearly 60
per cent., and in Protectionist Germany 86 per cent.
of the farmers own the land they cultivate

MOSQUITOES, GIN AND SNAKES.
One hot sultry night in December
I wearily went to my bed
Mosquitoes provoked my ill-temper
As on me they feasted and fed.
My body they freely did puncture
Till large purple pimples arose
Causing a passionate erupture
To shatter my hopes of repose.

say hasn’t seen them, his medical adviser having for-
bidden excitement; and Vincent telegraphs that he is
glad he was away when they came. Low declines to
give an opinion,- on the -ground that he is a Maori-
lander and doesn’t wish to cause ill-feeling between
the two countries.

Melbourne suburbs have been puzzled by a
big machine which is set down at street
corners and carefully watched, though it
does nothing but squat and linger and loaf,
either by day or early night. It is a fathom
high, 18ft. long, and very intricate-looking,
like a compound of printing press, traction
engine and diamond drill. It only works
from midnight to 5 a.m., and its job is plan-
ing the tramway rails smooth where they
begin to show splinters and whiskers. In
fact, it gives old rails a deceptive aspect
of youth—they are thinner, certainly, but
quite serviceable. And it saves the tramway
monopoly quite a mountain of money.

IN THE SUPREME COURT OF NEW
ZEALAND, WELLINGTON DIS-
TRICT.
IN THE MATTER of The Ad-
ministration Act 1908
and
IN THE MATTER of The Public
Trust Office Act 1908
and
IN THE MATTER of the Will of
THOMAS LANG DON late of
Christchurch in the Provincial
District of Canterbury, But-
cher, deceased.
Before His Honor The Chief Justice
Saturday the 18th day of November, 1911.
UPON READING the petition of the
Public Trustee of the Dominion of* New
Zealand and the affidavit verifying the same
respectively filed in this Court AND upon
hearing Mr. J. W. Macdonald Counsel for
The Public Trustee IT IS ORDERED that
The Public Trustee be and he is hereby
authorised on the assumption that ALBERT
FRANCIS LANGDON son of the above-
named deceased is dead to distribute his
reserved share under the aforesaid Will as
if the said ALBERT FRANCIS LANG-
DON had died intestate and unmarried.
By the Court,
(The Seal of The G. S. CLARK,
Supreme Court— Deputy Registrar.
New Zealand.)

ABRIDGED PROSPECTUS of
The Photo Vista Film Production Company
To be Incorporated tinder the Companies Act igio. Limited
CAPITAL: £20,000 in 20,000 Shares of £1 each.
3,500 Shares, fully paid up, to be allotted to the Vendors (The Photo Vista
Film Production Syndicate) as part consideration for the business of
the Syndicate. '
10,000 Ordinary Shares are to be offered to the public for subscription, payable
2/6 per share on application; 2/6 per share on allotment; 5/- per share
within three months, and balance as by calls not exceeding
2/6/ per month.
6,50 J Shares will be held in reserve for future issue if required.
Provisional Directors :
The Hon. THOMAS LANGDON, M.L.A., Kerferd-road, Albert Park.
FRANK BUCK HURST, Esq., Flinders-street, Melbourne, Chemist.
Solicitors:
Messrs. FORD, ASPINWALL and DE GRUCHY, 128 Queen-street, Melbourne,.
Bankers:

A. O. LEWIS, A.I.A.V. (Wm. Medhurst Taylor & Co.), 21 Queen-st., Melbourne,.
OBJECTS.
The Company is being formed to acquire from the Vendors, on the terms herein set out, the
together with ail the.assets thereof, now being carried on by the Photo Vista Film Syndicate; to carry
on that business and extend same, including the manufacture, sale, purchase, lease and other dealings
with cinematograph negatives, films and apparatus, and also, if thought advisable, to establish in various
parts of Australia moving picture theatres.
No better opportunity than the present is likely to present itself for entering into this business. The
demand for films exceeds the supply. Picture theatres are paying well, and, many of them are returning,
extremely handsome: profits. The whole field of educative cinematography is practically unexplored.
When one remembers the avidity with which either frankly scenic films of foreign cotintries or films,
showing dramatic subjects With a background of foreign countries, are received by picture audiences
here, it is fair to assume that films produced in this country under Similar conditions will receive a
similar reception in other countries. The picture film production business in Victoria is in its absolute
infancy, ana the promoters of the Vendors’ Syndicate felt so-confident that there was a splendid opening
i to establish such a business that they inaugurated a Syndicate and commenced to establish the business.
The Commonwealth of Australia and Dominion of New Zealand are the happv hunting ground of
wealthy tourists. Their unsurpassed beauty spots are unknown even tti xfianv ,o*f our own people. As
subjects of cinematography tney contain scenic treasures . practically untouched. The Promoters are
convinced that, with the development of the production of films of Australian scenes, a new era will be
opened in cinematography, and that just at a time when perfect .on of these scenes can be reached by the
wonders of color cinematography. Pathe Frcres have expressed their willingness not only to act as dis-
tributors for films on usual business terms, but to purchase outrignt films where thev are subjects suitable
for export.
With the pioneering work done the Company will take over the business, which is a going concern.
What has been accomplished can be judged from the following extract from Pattie s Weekly, under date
30th November, 1911: —
“The Photo Vista Company have just produced the first Australian Comedy produced in
“Australia. The story is indescribably funny, and the situations original and unique. The whole
“work is Australian throughout, and is now in the bands of Pathe Freres for inspection. The
“same company have also completed a fine drama, entitled ‘A Miner’s Luck,’ to be released in
“due course. Progress with the new venture has been so satisfactory that arrangements are now'
being made to release at least one drama or comedy, weekly. Mr. Chas. Wheeler (seventeen years
“with Bland Holt) will be the producer, and some fine" stories are being' put in hand.”
The rights of Mr. Ambrose Pratt’s “Outlaws of Weddin Range” have been acquired from that gentle-
man by the Syndicate in consideration of 100 shares and 7 V per cent, royalty.
The Manager of the Syndicate will enter into a long engagement with the Conmany as Manager, and
will undertake to devote his whole time and attention to the business of the Company, and also will make
suitable portion of his property above mentioned, at Beaconsfield, available to the Company for film produc-
tion on a long lease aka merely nominal rental.
Mr. Wheeler will take charge of the stage production; and generally act as Stage Manager.
The Vendors only ask for so much as will purchase the assets in hand at a valuation. For their
work, expenditure and losses in founding the concern, training the staff of artists and assistants, and
obtaining the expert experience they now have, they are prepared to accept shares on the foregoing
conditions.
In accordance with the Companies Act 1910 of Victoria the following information is embodied in
the :
(a) The draft Memorandum of Association showing the names, descriptions and addresses of such
persons as have up to the present signed such Memorandum and the number of shares up to the
present subscribed for by them appears at the end hereof and form part of this Prospectus.
The only founders or management or deferred shares are the above-mentioned 3500 shares to be
issued to the Vendors.

and the amount payable on -application and allotment respectively is 2s. 6d.

otherwise than in cash is the above-mentioned 3500 shares only.

STOMACH TROUBLE
A Carrier’s Suffering from Nausea, Belching, Heartburn,
Headache, etc.—The Penalty of Rough Living.
jkj
ĵk
Mr. James Green, a carrier, of St. George,
via Dailey, Queensland, tells of his suffering
from terrible stomach trouble, and his
cure by the great standard remedy, Bile
Beans, for the benefit of fellow sufferers,
as follows:—“Being a resident of the
bush,” says Mr. Green, “and following
the occupation of a carrier, I have often
been forced to camp under my waggon, and
live on hard fare such as corned beef, soda
bread, etc., and this, combined with the
scarcity of green vegetables, had the effect
of upsetting my stomach. In addition to
this, and to make matters worse, I was
particularly partial to strong tea, and
the inevitable result was bad digestion, which
greatly played up with my health. My sleep
became disturbed, and I was troubled with
nausea, heartburn, wind, belching, occasional
headaches, poor appetite, fulness after
meals, and constipation. Knowing Bile
Beans to be the standard remedy for all these
ailments. I procured a box, and they proved
an efficient remedy—toning up my stomach
and system, cleansing the bowels, and giving
me immediate relief. The wind, nausea, headache, and other nasty synip-
toms from which I suffered, have entirely left me, my freedom from which ij
solely due to Bile Beans. Bile Beans are a handy, useful medicine for those
of my vocation to carry about, as they take up little room, an d are easy to take. ”

Live and Let Live.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ihe Live-and-Let-Live Hotel, Forest Creek, Vic.,
has been closed by the Victorian Licenses Reduction
Hoard; compensation £l9s.— News item.
“ T IVE-and-Let-Live!” I knew that
| J Pub;
It was a little shanty built
Where still the Chow takes pan and tub,
To wash for ‘‘colors” in the silt,
For oft I’ve walked for miles to tilt
The cool pint glass upon its bar,
And hear old tales of gold and guilt,
So different from the days that are!
They built it in the Roaring Time,
When every gully yielded gold,
When Forest Creek was in its prime,
Noisy and dissolute and bold ;
When all the diggers, young and old,
Lived as they pleased, nor reckoned then
The Wowser’s nasal voice would scold
The joys and sins of other men.
It was a digger’s motto, that —-
A pleasant creed the world might well
I’aste nowadays within its hat,
And silence thus the Wowser’s yell;
No motto for the folks who smell
And snuffle in another’s yard,
But meant much trouble to dispel:
‘Live-and-let-live” makes life less hard.
Twas there the digger flung his ounce
Upon the bar, and called for wine
Which- —since the truth we can’t renounce —
Was not ambrosial, not divine;
Keats had not found its flavor fine,
For rum and nicotine are rank ;
Who cared? It was an anodyne:
‘Live and let live!” they cried, and drank.
I used to find it lonely, save
> For one old fossicker who sat
Peering (I guess) towards his grave
Beneath an old and battered hat;
And while we smoked and drank and spat,
And offered mutual compliment,
We two, in many a friendly chat,
Learned well what “Live-and-let-live” meant.
For I was young, and he was old;
And I had silver, he had none;
My strength was selling, his was sold ;
My race was starting, his was run ;
/nP es we swa PP e d, and yarns we spun
(There’s some of his unwritten still),
And when at last his time was done,
I paid his undertaker’s bill.
To-day, the Law stalks round, a beast
Biting at all loose ends of life;
It takes the liquor from the feast,
It bids the lover seek a wife.
But in the days of lawless strife
Men had a good old rule that’s gone:
The blackguard dropped his foolish knife:
“Life and let live!”—and things went on.
Live and Let Live! Poor little pub,
With memories we can’t forget,
You die decaying—there’s the rub !
But Wowsers might have spared you yet.
VICTORIA.
M.
Bruisers in Babylon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BABYLON the Beautiful! City of
Many Waters!
I saw it rise from the baby waves
of its harbor through which our ship slowly
threaded its way, a fragment ripped from
Paradise and cast upon earth to show what
beauty dwells in Elysium. Spire, dome and
mighty piles reared blackly against the rose-
tinted sky ; in the foreground a castle with
the Lion banner floating from its central
tower, and a forest of masts and funnels
scattered along the line where the green
glinting water ended.
This was Babylon, the place to which I
had come to make my fortune, regardless of
whose blood and sweat it should be crystal-
lised from.
All day I wandered round the strange
and to me enchanted city. Along sweltering
streets, through deep arcades where a wilder-
ness of pot plants and ferns dripped water
and made a cool oasis; past gorgeous shop
windows filled with the merchandise of
every country on earth but that to which
they belonged ; in and out of splendid bars
where gaily dressed women served long cool
drinks, and laughed and chatted as though
thev enjoyed it; till in the evening I once
more found myself back in the balconied
house at Potts Point and talking to a
young man in a Tussore suit and Panama
hat. On his still handsome face the mark of
the Beast had not obliterated the grace of
youth. He took a kindly interest in me be-
cause I was a stranger, and led me upstairs
to where his balcony, like an eyrie at the
top of the many-storeyed house, gave a bird’s
eye view of mingled gardens and ship-
covered waters.
In the west a long blue ridge already
turning to purple. It might have been hills
or haze.
“Over there,” he said, as he brought out
whisky and soda with glasses held beneath
his arm, “is the most beautiful river on
earth. And over there, beyond the city,
bush land filled with flannel daisies and
little swamps all aflame with Christmas
bells, that look like tiny lancers riding
through the tall grasses. And there,”
pointing to where the water reflected the
wondrously tinted maize green sky, “is a
sight you can see only in our own city!”
And I believe he was right. If ever there
was an Elysium it was this landlocked har-
bor, its isles and capes silhouetted in the
deepening gloom, and dotted over with glid-
ing ferryboats, their colored lights gleaming
like great clusters of gems against the dark-
ening waters.
“I’d like to take you round, but there’s
a light on to-night, and I’m on the press. If
youd care to have a look at it I shall be
glad to take you! There goes the dinner-
bell.”
He led the way downstairs to a dining-
room in the basement, where the mingled
sounds of the street floated in through wide
open windows, and women sat round a table
bright with flowers and electroplate. They
wore loose wrappers that showed details of
their beautiful bodies in a way that the
heavier stuff garments of my own country
had never done.
“It is the first time I have ever been in a
great city!” I explained, “so please excuse
any gaucheness. I have read of ancient
Rome, of Byzantium, Venice, Paris,. Madrid
and London; but never of anything like
this. It takes me off my feet.”
He smiled faintly.
“I’d give all I possess to have your ex-
perience,” he said softly. “I was born here,
and grew up among it all, and cannot re-
member a time when it filled me with won-
der. I wish I could. It would be great.
Have you ever been to a prize fight?”
“No —but I’ve seen timber-getters fight-
ing. and they are said to be pretty tough.”
“They are —but that was a primitive scrap
with bare knuckles. These fellows you will
look upon to-night are the best friends in
the world, but they fight for the largest
share of a purse, and when they hear the
gong, the savage derision and more savage
glee at the sight of blood, they will become
for the time being wild beasts. They are
heavy-weights, hard hitters, scientifically
trained to inflict and endure punishment.
At first you will be disgusted, and then you
will begin to get interested, and long before
the fight is over you will be whooping as
wildly as the rest, and shouting to the vic-
tor to ‘finish’ the man who is obviously
fighting a losing battle.”
“Sounds like wild beast pleasure.”
“It is, it is,” he agreed, passing some pas-
sion fruit. “And the shocking part of it is
that the more wild beast-like it is the better
we enjoy it. Babylon makes wild beasts of
us all.”
We sat on an electric car that blazed
with light, and roared through streets filled
with men accompanied by women whose
hats were walking gardens, whose breasts
showed voluptuous outlines through the thin
silk that hardly hid the glow of their bodies.
Everywhere the loveliness of woman was
only half-hidden, and they flaunted their
charms in the eye, and were proud of them.
“Pagans everyone!” I murmured.
He nodded. The car came to a standstill
in a street that blazed with bewildering
amethystine globes of light. It was crowded
with men, all hurrying in one direction.
“Where on earth are they all going?” I
asked.
“To the fight, every mother’s son of them
—hurry up or we won’t get a seat.”
He gripped me by the arm and pushed
and drew me in and out among the crowd
till we passed through an iron gate where
men with three days’ growth on their chins
stood by in a row and glowered enviously at
the crowd pouring in to see the battle.
Down a passage, up a staircase, and into
a great circular pit in which tiers of seats
rose from the floor to the roof in the midst
of which a great open space showed the
stars swinging low in a purple ocean.
Clouds of smoke rose from hundreds of
cigars, cigarettes and pipes, and floated
through the open roof. Right in the centre
of the huge building was a roped-off square,
with padded corner-posts. That was the
arena.
The hum of voices was indescribable, and
men were shouting to one another across
the gulf above the arena. Every now and
again in some distant seat men would arise
and call out something quite indistinguish-
able, wave their hands in angry gesticula-
tion, and then subside once more.
I looked round the throng. There were
men in the front seats in evening dress,
there were others in silken suits, and some
who wore silken vests, but the light
Panama hat was everywhere. High up,
sweltering in the hot air near the roof, were
men and boys in their shirtsleeves, all chat-
tering, all smoking.
And the faces! They rise before me yet,
even now when Babylon has grown com-
monplace, and the lovely women attract no
longer, and the love of money has excluded
all other emotions. Those fighting faces!
Great square jaws, firm, strong noses,
shaven jowls, and columnar necks. And
always the half-contemptuous, semi-cynical
expression of the eyes.
A tall, weedy young man rose up and
bawled:—
“Anybody want to lay five to four! Any-
body offering five to four? For heving’s
sike don’t all speak at wanst!”
Nobody took any notice." Just below two
men seemed to be considering and discussing
something. Then both shook their heads, sat
back and looked at one another. One’s hand
stole to his waistcoat pocket, the other
pulled out a pocket-book, and both handed
some money to a third man, who counted it
carefully, and put it in his inside pocket.
Again the weedy young man rose up and
made remarks at the apdience generally.
Another tall form sprang up five rows of
seats away and shouted something to him.
The. weed listened, and then, making an in-
sulting remark, sat down again.
The pressman with me, or, rather, with
whom I was, smiled.
“I’ll lay five to four on the nigger!” he
hissed across the seats to the weed, who
scrambled and pushed till he came along-
side, and they passed their money to me to
hold. Then the weed went back and franti-
cally offered the same odds.
“That fellow knows something, but not
enough. The nigger is hard up, and a syn-
dicate of bookmakers bought him, giving
him a price twice the value of the prize to
lie down and be counted out at the end of
the 19th round; so they are all plunging
wildly on the white man.”
“WeH, if you know this, why did you back
the black man: \ou have laid your money
on the negro.”
“Yes; but Thorley Thornville, the plunger,
has rebought the nigger. Made it worth his
while to win, and the other fellow, never
dreaming of a double shuffle, will be off his ,
guard and get knocked out. That is sport—
in Babylon.”
Up through a trap door climbed the
pugilists, and the house sprang to its feet and
cheered wildly. Both pugs stood in the arena
and waggled their heads delightedly. The
difference was startling. The black man was
over six feet tall, and beautifully built,
everything but his face and head statuesque
• —they were ophidian. The white man was
shorter, squarer built, and stronger far.
Moreover, he had the powerful jaw. clear
eyes and finer head of the dominant race.
The negro was arrayed in trunks only, the
white man wore knickers of white cloth,
and above them his body was brown as ivory,
but a clear, transparent ivory, through which
the blood glowed fitfully.
The fat, podgy man who was to referee

BURDENS LIFTED FROM BAD BACKS.
WEARY is the back that bears the burden of kidney ills. There's no rest or peace for the man or woman who has a bad back. The distress begins
in early morning. You feel lame and not refreshed. It's hard to get out of bed. It hurts to stoop to tie your shoes. All day the ache keeps
up. Any sudden movement sends sharp twinges through the back. It is torture to stoop and straighten. At night the sufferer retires to toss and
twist and groan. Backache is kidney ache —a throbbing dull aching in the kidneys. Plasters or liniments won t do. You must get at the cause, inside.
Mr. R. Spring, Ross Street, Parramatta, says:—“l make

ache Kidney Pills. Off and on for many years I suffered from

Five years later Mr. Spring says:—“The above statement

the fight leaned languidly over the ropes,
giving instructions in short, decisive sen-
tences, and fragments about “clean break”
and “hitting in holts” were heard intermit-
tently. The gloves of dark brown Were sud-
denly thrown into the ring. The black man
chose his, and then the white picked up the
others and walked to the corner. The
grim-faced seconds adjusted them, tied the
strings and suddenly scurried out of the
ring.
A sudden silence seemed to fairly crash
down on the audience. The voice of the
referee requested “gentlemen” to put out all
“smokes,” and then there was a brief pause
of terrific suspense.
A gong sounded—a single tap, as it were.
The fighters arose, advanced to the centre,
shook hands, backed away, and then ap-
proached each other warily. After the roar
and hum the shuffling of their feet sounded
strangely loud in the heavy stillness.
Round they shuffled, each watching for an
opening. Then the white man stepped in
lightly, knocked the black fists away, and
smote the negro in the chest.
The pent-up excitement broke with a roar.
Through it the two men whirled round the
ring, ducking, jabbing, fending. Biff, shuffle,
smack, biff!
The crowd rose en masse. The black man
was down on his knees, his gloved hands
resting on the padded floor.
Torrents of abuse flowed over him. Men
rushed to the ropes and howled to him to
get up and fight, threatening to murder him
if he did not. And above all the roar of
the referee’s voice could be heard : two, four,
five. . . .eight”
And then one last and loudest shout to get
up seemed to suddenly lift the black man to
his feet. The white rushed in and smote
hard. The black took it on the glove, and,
stepping back to avoid the right that swung
out viciously, poised himself for a moment.
His long, black left shot out with a graceful,
lance-like motion, and caught the white
man in the face. White’s blows lost their
aim, he flung up his arms, and, pitching for-
ward, caught the black man round the shoul-
ders and hung on.
I saw his features blurred with blood, over
the red stain his clear blue eyes gleamed
with a murderous light. The black shoved
him off, and he backed away, wiping his eyes
with his glove. The black approached him
warily. The white moved round cautiously.
The black right came out, tentatively, allur-
ingly. The white never heeded it, he was
watching the black man’s eye. And .the
black seemed to creep nearer and nearer with
a stealthy, cat-like movement, feeling his way
with his right fist. His left was drawn
back ready to strike.
The white man ducked in, the left shot
over his shoulder, the whirling white arms
seemed to hurl the brown, gloved fists against
the towering black body, which reeled and
rocked, and then shot over the floor with a
slithering motion, fell heavily against the
ropes, and slipped down to the floor.
“Wan —two —three, foor”
He sprang up, and, instead of backing
away, rushed at his opponent. They met
with a crash, black and white firms were
mixed up together, struggling, hitting, swing-
ing, and then gripped together they rocked
round the ring. Blood streamed from both
faces, showing darkly against the black skin,
luridly against the pink pelt of the white
man. The referee scrambled into the ring,
the audience screeched and howled, and one
excited spectator jumped on to a seat and
hit a tall man in the face, knocking him right
over into the next row of seats. -
Over all the din came the resonant gong,
and referee and seconds tore the combatants
apart and dragged them to their corners,
sprayed water over them, held flasks to their
lips, and fanned them wildly with towels.
The audience subsided like a sullen sea.
Again the gong, and once more they
walked warily round each other. White
tried to rush in, but the long, lance-like left
of black met him full in the face and drove
him back. He tried to duck under —the same
left shot out once more, and then the white
rushed round, feinting, for an opening, tried
to beat down the black guard, and again
stopped the left, which came out always
with the same graceful, piston-like move-
ment.
“He’s beat, he’s beat! Black, black,
black, black !” howled the audience, keeping
time with their feet.
“White, white, white, white! —kill ther
cow !” came the counter roar. Far up, near
the ceiling, a thin, lanky youth was shouting
to white exactly how to do the killing. But
white, with his blue eyes full of blood-lust
and maniacal fury, heard nothing, saw no-
thing, only the ophidian head and its battered
face that he yearned to shatter to pulp, and
could not. He showered his blows like
lightning, aiming every one for the long, black
jaw, that backed and backed away. His
whirling arms met and turned aside the
thrusts of that lance-like left. Blows fell
over his shoulders and chest; he never felt
them, and, struggling closer, struck and
stiuck to kill.
Round and round the roped square they
raged, fighting, fending, the black dancing
away, the white in full pursuit. The audi-
ence felt the strain, the shouting died down,
and a fierce desire for a .sudden ending to
the strenuous, exhausting affair swept
through the crowded hall.
The gong clanged.
The bookmakers were crowding to the
ropes, with scared and anxious faces. This
black brute had either gone mad, and forgot-,
ten the arrangement, or he had sold them.
They shouted unintelligible things to him.
He lay still, with his eyes closed, drinking
in the air from the waving towels. They
swirled round to the corner where the
seconds’ empty chairs made room, and tried
to talk to him. The audience howled and
hooted, and men foaming with fury begged to
their Maker to say had He ever seen any-
thing so barefaced.
“They’re squaring him —they’re squarin’
’im !” they shouted.
For myself I was tired. I had felt every
blow struck. I was sore all over. I felt
that unless it ended soon I should die of
sheer exhaustion.
“Cla-n-n-ng-g!”
They met with a crash. Both reeled back-
wards .and slipped down. Up again and
away round the ring, the black getting home
with two of the piston-like strokes of his
beautiful left, but reeling like a ship in a
gale from a right-handed swing that had
caught him over the heart as he whirled
round. The white man closed in on him,
pinning him to the corner, pressing in close
upon him, boring closer and closer to deliver
the coup de grace.
Again that sudden silence of intense, agon-
ised anxiety. v
The left shot through his guard, and made
impact on his face. He staggered, it shot
out again, he struck feebly, it glided over
his left and smote him on the chin, and down
he went in a heap.
The droning voice of the referee had
reached nine when he struggled up. I sud-
denly felt myself jerked from behind, and
realised that I. too, had risen involuntarily.
I sat down hastily, without taking my eyes
off the pugilists.
The white stood up and met the savage at-
tack of the black with a vicious uppercut
that sent him reeling. They both fell, and lay
still.
The seconds started to scramble through
the ropes, when the combatants struggled to
their feet, and rushed limply at one another.
There was a roar of excitement from the
audience, the men swayed about, and over
the noise came the shrill shriek of the negro
in agony, and with a desperate effort he
shook the white man off, fell down on his
hands and knees, and struggled towards the
referee. After him scrambled the dazed
white, hitting at him as he crawled, with
helpless, powerless blows.
The audience was on its feet roaring in-
anely, the bookmakers were rushing wildly
towards the referee, who was in the ring,
with his hand on the negro’s head, howling
something quite unintelligible. The white’s
seconds dragged him away, and disap-
peared down the trap door. The crowd
started to surge for the doors, we were
swept along, when the weed suddenly ap-
peared and demanded his money back.
“Let’s get the referee’s decision!” said the
pressman, struggling against the torrent help-
lessly. The flood of men and youths swept
us apart, and, after what seemed a frightful
interval, I found myself in the courtyard,
with the Weed holding my sleeve. A man
was posting up a notice: —
“BLACK WON ON A FOUL!”
Then the pressman caught me by the arm
' from somewhere and steered me through the
torrent into a side stream through glass
doors, and brought me to anchor at a bar.
“What made black screech so?” I in-
quired, sinking down into the cushioned
lounge.
“White bit him!” smiled the pressman,
tilting the nether end of his glass heaven-
wards, and taking an observation at the
roof through the base of it.
We wandered homeward through a park of
century-old trees, and breathed in the per-
fume-laden puffs of faint, warm wind. _ Lo-
custs and other cicadw made sweet music in
the trees. Past great, silent ships lying at
iron-gated wharves, up steep steps to the
house, and on to the balcony from which the
jewelled ferry boats could be seen wander-
ing on the invisible water.
And so ended my first day among the wild
beasts of Babylon.
C. A. JEFFRIES.
The Winner.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SHEEMSH’ me,” began the man in the
middle, didactically.
The tall, lean, brown person on his
right reached across and made a dive among
the water-biscuits. He seized three, and con-
veyed them to his mouth in a bunch.
“Too mush weigh’, Shmish !” he urged in a
muffled roar, from among the biscuits.
“Sheemsh’ me” —insisted the first speaker.
“Well, well, well, Joe, if y’arsh me,” said
the rest of the party, a man in a green-rib-
boned strawdecker, with socks to match, low-
cut shoes and a clean-shaven, red jaw, show-
ing the fatness of some 40 summers.
“Sheemsh’ me,” came from beneath the
drooping moustache of the persistent Smith.
Joe exhaled a shower of crumbled biscuit,
and broke in hastily. “Dish’ance too far.
Shmish! Too mush weigh’! Far too mush
weigh’!”
Smith took his empty glass and rapped
angrily upon the bar. The drink merchant
came along.
“Whisky an’ slio’!” said Joe.
“Whi’!” murmured the green-socked one,
lazily, leaning against the polished railing.
The booze stevedore supplied them, and
waited for Smith’s order. Smith rapped
hard again with his empty glass, staring into
vacancy, and the man of beer reached for the
local bulk and poured him out two fingers.
“Sheemsh* me,” Smith went on, with an
air of renewed confidence.
“Nusli—nush —nushin’ o’ th’ hind!' burst
out the 40-year-old, bitterly. “Nush—nush
—nush,” and lost the thread of his discourse,
while Joe broke in with a final word.
“Rot! Rot!—all rosh!” he declared.
“ ’Orsh ain’t gorranope !” .
There was a short silence, during which
drinking took place. Smith was ready first.
“Sheemsh’ me,” he commenced again, hope-
“Worrall you ’ave, Smiss?” asked Green.
Smith raised his glass for the third time,
annoyed, and once more banged it heavily on
the beer-proof bar-covering.
“Sheemsh’ me,” he shouted passionately,
and Green leaned over and touched the bar-
man on the shoulder.
“Give’m dhink!” lie begged, earnestly.
“Pore shevil wance it!” . .
Again the gentle noise of lapped spirits,
and three comfortable sighs; and presently
the trio made for the door, the long, brown
Joe with an arm about each of his pals
shoulders. .
At the exit they jammed, filtering through
by degrees. Smith fell out last, and from
the street there came a strangled sound, as
of a stout man groping for his hat after talc-
ing, ten drinks.
“Sheemsh’ me.” But the rattle of a pass-
ing cab.obliterated the rest of the sentence,
if there was any, and the barman moaned
slightly to himself, and gloomily swallowed
a pony of dry ginger ale. His troubles about
the winner—on 35 bob a week !

WHY Every Farmer should
use ELECTRIC LIGHTS.
ELECTRIC LIGHT is the only modern method of illumination for all purposes. Electric
lighting is specially adapted for use in farm-houses, stables, barns, where there is danger of
tires if coal, oil, acetylene or gas lamps are used.
If you live in the country, you know without our telling how much you need Electric Lights.
You already know how handy, clean and convenient electric lamps are, and vou would, perhaps,
have installed them years ago if it had not been for the high cost of the old style, or for running
the wires out from the
nearest town.
You know how poor
oil lamps are, and how
you have to strain your
eyes trying to read,, while
you inhale the kerosene
smoky atmosphere, and
the trouble to keep your
lamps clean. Your doc-
tor knows how unhealthy
your lamps are; your
decorator how it pays
him.
Electric Lights are
Absolutely Safe —No
Danger of Fire.
In almost every news-
paper you pick up, you
read of an explosion of
an acetylene gas plant or
a kerosene lamp falling
over or setting the
place on fire, often with
fatal results.
